the beckon fair one 




i 

the three or four "to let" boards had stood within the low paling as 
long as the inhabitant of the little triangular "square" could remember, 
and if they had ever been vertical it was a very long time ago. they now 
overhung the palings each at its own angle, and resemble nothing so 
much as a row of wooden choppers, ever in the act of fall upon some 
passer-by, yet never cutting off a tenant for the old house from the 
stream of his fellows. not that there was ever any great "stream" through 
the square; the stream pass a furlong and more away, beyond the 
intricacy of tenement and alley and byways that had sprung up since the 
old house had been built, hem it in completely; and probably the 
house itself was only suffer to stand pending the falling-in of a lease 
or two, when doubtless a clearance would be made of the whole 
neighbourhood. 

it was of bloomy old red brick, and built into its wall were the crown 
and clasp hand and other insignia of insurance company long since 
defunct. the children of the secluded square had swung upon the low gate 
at the end of the entrance-alley until little more than the solid top bar 
of it remained, and the alley itself ran past board basement window on 
which tramp had chalk their cryptic marks. the path was wash and 
worn uneven by the spill of water from the eaves of the encroach 
next house, and cat and dogs had made the approach their own. the 
chance of a tenant did not seem such as to warrant the keeping of the 
"to let" boards in a state of legible and repair, and as a matter of 
fact they were not so kept. 

for six month oleron had pass the old place twice a day or oftener, on 
his way from his lodgings to the room, ten minutes' walk away, he had 
taken to work in; and for six month no hatchet-like notice-board had 
fallen across his path. this might have been due to the fact that he 
usually took the other side of the square. but he chance one morning to 
take the side that ran past the broken gate and the rain-worn entrance 
alley, and to pause before one of the inclined boards. the board bore, 
besides the agent's name, the announcement, written apparently about the 
time of oleron's own early youth, that the key was to be had at number 
six. 

now oleron was already paying, for his separate bedroom and workroom, 
more than an author who, without private means, habitual disregard his 
public, can afford; and he was pay in addition a small rent for the 
storage of the greater part of his grandmother's furniture. moreover, it 
invariable happen that the book he wish to read in bed was at his 
working-quarters half a mile and more away, while the note or letter he 
had sudden need of during the day was as likely as not to be in the 
pocket of another coat hanging behind his bedroom door. and there were 
other inconvenience in have a divide domicile. therefore oleron, 
brought sudden up by the hatchet-like notice-board, look first down 
through some scanty privet-bushes at the board basement windows, then 
up at the blank and grimy window of the first floor, and so up to the 
second floor and the flat stone coping of the leads. he stood for a 
minute thumb his lean and shaven jaw; then, with another glance at the 
board, he walk slow across the square to number six. 

he knocked, and wait for two or three minutes, but, although the door 
stood open, received no answer. he was knock again when a long-nosed 
man in shirt-sleeves appeared. 

"i was arsk a blessing on our food," he said in severe explanation. 

oleron ask if he might have the key of the old house; and the 
long-nosed man withdrew again. 

oleron wait for another five minutes on the step; then the man, 
appear again and masticate some of the food of which he had spoken, 
announce that the key was lost. 

"but you won't want it," he said. "the entrance door isn't closed, and a 
push'll open any of the others. i'm a agent for it, if you're thinking of 
taking it--" 

oleron recross the square, descended the two steps at the broken gate, 
pass along the alley, and turn in at the old wide doorway. to the 
right, immediately within the door, steps descended to the roomy cellars, 
and the staircase before him had a carve rail, and was broad and 
handsome and filthy. oleron ascend it, avoid contact with the rail 
and wall, and stop at the first landing. a door facing him had been 
board up, but he pushed at that on his right hand, and an insecure bolt 
or staple yielded. he enter the empty first floor. 

he spent a quarter of an hour in the place, and then came out again. 
without mount higher, he descended and recross the square to the 
house of the man who had lost the key. 

"can you tell me how much the rent is?" he asked. 

the man mention a figure, the comparative low of which seem 
account for by the character of the neighbourhood and the abominable 
state of unrepair of the place. 

"would it be possible to rent a single floor?" 

the long-nosed man did not know; they might.... 

"who are they?" 

the man gave oleron the name of a firm of lawyer in lincoln's inn. 

"you might mention my name--barrett," he added. 

pressure of work prevent oleron from go down to lincoln's inn that 
afternoon, but he went on the morrow, and was instantly offer the 
whole house as a purchase for fifty pound down, the remainder of the 
purchase-money to remain on mortgage. it took him half an hour to 
disabuse the lawyer's mind of the idea that he wish anything more of 
the place than to rent a single floor of it. this made certain hum and 
haw of a difference, and the lawyer was by no means certain that it lay 
within his power to do as oleron suggested; but it was finally extract 
from him that, provided the notice-boards were allow to remain up, and 
that, provided it was agree that in the event of the whole house 
letting, the arrangement should terminate automatic without further 
notice, something might be done. that the old place should sudden let 
over his head seem to oleron the slightest of risk to take, and he 
promise a decision within a week. on the morrow he visit the house 
again, went through it from top to bottom, and then went home to his 
lodgings to take a bath. 

he was immensely taken with that portion of the house he had already 
determined should be his own. scrape clean and repainted, and with 
that old furniture of oleron's grandmother's, it ought to be entire 
charming. he went to the storage warehouse to refresh his memory of his 
half-forgotten belongings, and to take measurements; and thence he went 
to a decorator's. he was very busy with his regular work, and could have 
wish that the notice-board had caught his attention either a few month 
early or else later in the year; but the quickest way would be to 
suspend work entire until after his removal.... 

a fortnight later his first floor was paint throughout in a tender, 
elder-flower white, the paint was dry, and oleron was in the middle of 
his installation. he was animated, delighted; and he rub his hand as 
he polish and made disposal of his grandmother's effects--the tall 
lattice-paned china cupboard with its derby and mason and spode, the 
large fold sheraton table, the long, low bookshelf (he had had two 
of them "copied"), the chairs, the sheffield candlesticks, the rivet 
rose-bowls. these things he set against his newly paint elder-white 
walls--walls of wood panel in the happiest proportions, and mould 
and coffer to the low-seated window-recesses in a mood of gaiety and 
rest that the builder of rooms no long know. the ceiling were lofty, 
and faint paint with an old pattern of stars; even the taper 
moulding of his iron fireplace were as delicate design as jewellery; 
and oleron walk about rubbing his hands, frequent stopping for the 
mere pleasure of the glimpse from white room to white room.... 

"charming, charming!" he said to himself. "i wonder what elsie bengough 
will think of this!" 

he bought a bolt and a yale lock for his door, and shut off his quarters 
from the rest of the house. if he now want to read in bed, his book 
could be had for step into the next room. all the time, he thought 
how exceedingly lucky he was to get the place. he put up a hat-rack in 
the little square hall, and hung up his hat and cap and coats; and 
pass through the small triangular square late at night, look up 
over the little serried row of wooden "to let" hatchets, could see the 
light within oleron's red blinds, or else the sudden darken of one 
blind and the illumination of another, as oleron, candlestick in hand, 
pass from room to room, making final settle of his furniture, or 
prepare to resume the work that his removal had interrupted. 


ii 

as far as the chief business of his life--his writing--was concerned, 
paul oleron treat the world a good deal better than he was treat by 
it; but he seldom took the trouble to strike a balance, or to compute how 
far, at forty-four years of age, he was behind his points on the 
handicap. to have done so wouldn't have alter matters, and it might 
have depressed oleron. he had chosen his path, and was committed to it 
beyond possibility of withdrawal. perhaps he had chosen it in the days 
when he had been easily sway by something a little disinterested, a 
little generous, a little noble; and had he ever thought of questioning 
himself he would still have held to it that a life without nobility and 
generous and disinterested was no life for him. only quite 
recently, and rarely, had he even vague suspect that there was more 
in it than this; but it was no good anticipate the day when, he 
supposed, he would reach that maximum point of his powers beyond which he 
must inevitable decline, and be left face to face with the question 
whether it would not have profit him better to have rule his life 
by less exigent ideals. 

in the meantime, his removal into the old house with the insurance mark 
built into its brick merely interrupt _romilly bishop_ at the fifteenth 
chapter. 

as this tall man with the lean, ascetic face move about his new abode, 
arranging, changing, altering, hardly yet into his working-stride again, 
he gave the impression of almost spinster-like precision and nicety. for 
twenty years past, in a score of lodgings, garrets, flats, and rooms 
furnish and unfurnished, he had been accustomed to do many things for 
himself, and he had discover that it save time and temper to be 
methodical. he had arrange with the wife of the long-nosed barrett, a 
stout welsh woman with a falsetto voice, the merionethshire accent of 
which long residence in london had not perceptible modified, to come 
across the square each morning to prepare his breakfast, and also to 
"turn the place out" on saturday mornings; and for the rest, he even 
welcome a little housework as a relaxation from the strain of writing. 

his kitchen, together with the adjoin strip of an apartment into 
which a modern bath had been fitted, overlook the alley at the side of 
the house; and at one end of it was a large closet with a door, and a 
square slide hatch in the upper part of the door. this had been a 
powder-closet, and through the hatch the elaborate dress head had 
been thrust to receive the click and puff of the powder-pistol. oleron 
puzzle a little over this closet; then, as its use occur to him, he 
smile faintly, a little moved, he knew not by what.... he would have to 
put it to a very different purpose from its original one; it would 
probably have to serve as his larder.... it was in this closet that 
he made a discovery. the back of it was shelved, and, rummage on an 
upper shelf that ran deep into the wall, oleron found a couple of 
mushroom-shaped old wooden wig-stands. he did not know how they had come 
to be there. doubtless the painter had turn them up somewhere or 
other, and had put them there. but his five rooms, as a whole, were 
short of cupboard and closet-room; and it was only by the exercise of 
some ingenuity that he was able to find place for the bestow of his 
household linen, his boxes, and his seldom-used but not-to-be-destroyed 
accumulation of papers. 

it was in early spring that oleron enter on his tenancy, and he was 
anxious to have _romilly_ ready for publication in the coming autumn. 
nevertheless, he did not intend to force its production. should it demand 
long in the doing, so much the worse; he realise its importance, its 
crucial importance, in his artistic development, and it must have its own 
length and time. in the workroom he had recently left he had been making 
excellent progress; _romilly_ had begun, as the saying is, to speak and 
act of herself; and he did not doubt she would continue to do so the 
moment the distraction of his removal was over. this distraction was 
almost over; he told himself it was time he pull himself together 
again; and on a march morning he went out, return again with two great 
bunch of yellow daffodils, placed one bunch on his mantelpiece between 
the sheffield sticks and the other on the table before him, and took out 
the half-completed manuscript of _romilly bishop_. 

but before beginning work he went to a small rosewood cabinet and took 
from a drawer his cheque-book and pass-book. he tot them up, and his 
monk-like face grew thoughtful. his installation had cost him more than 
he had intended it should, and his balance was rather less than fifty 
pounds, with no immediate prospect of more. 

"hm! i'd forgotten rug and chintz curtains and so forth mount up so," 
said oleron. "but it would have been a pity to spoil the place for the 
want of ten pound or so.... well, _romilly_ simply _must_ be out for the 
autumn, that's all. so here goes--" 

he drew his papers towards him. 

but he work badly; or, rather, he did not work at all. the square 
outside had its own noises, frequent and new, and oleron could only hope 
that he would speedy become accustomed to these. first came hawkers, 
with their cart and cries; at midday the children, return from 
school, troop into the square and swung on oleron's gate; and when the 
children had departed again for afternoon school, an itinerant musician 
with a mandolin post himself beneath oleron's window and began to 
strum. this was a not unpleasant distraction, and oleron, push up his 
window, threw the man a penny. then he return to his table again.... 

but it was no good. he came to himself, at long intervals, to find that 
he had been look about his room and wonder how it had formerly 
been furnished--whether a settee in buttercup or petunia satin had stood 
under the farther window, whether from the centre moulding of the light 
lofty ceiling had depend a glimmer crystal chandelier, or where the 
tambour-frame or the picquet-table had stood.... no, it was no good; he 
had far better be frankly do nothing than get fruitless tired; 
and he decided that he would take a walk, but, chance to sit down for a 
moment, doze in his chair instead. 

"this won't do," he yawn when he awoke at half-past four in the 
afternoon; "i must do better than this to-morrow--" 

and he felt so delicious lazy that for some minutes he even 
contemplate the breach of an appointment he had for the evening. 

the next morning he sat down to work without even permit himself to 
answer one of his three letters--two of them tradesmen's accounts, the 
third a note from miss bengough, forward from his old address. it was a 
jolly day of white and blue, with a gay noisy wind and a subtle turn in 
the colour of grow things; and over and over again, once or twice a 
minute, his room became sudden light and then subdued again, as the 
shining white cloud roll north-eastwards over the square. the soft 
fitful illumination was reflect in the polish surface of the table 
and even in the footworn old floor; and the morning noise had begun 
again. 

oleron made a pattern of dot on the paper before him, and then broke off 
to move the jar of daffodil exactly opposite the centre of a creamy 
panel. then he wrote a sentence that ran continuous for a couple of 
lines, after which it broke on into note and jottings. for a time he 
succeed in persuade himself that in making these memoranda he was 
really working; then he rose and began to pace his room. as he did so, he 
was struck by an idea. it was that the place might possibly be a little 
better for more positive colour. it was, perhaps, a thought _too_ 
pale--mild and sweet as a kind old face, but a little devitalised, even 
wan.... yes, decidedly it would bear a robust note--more and rich 
flowers, and possibly some warm and gay stuff for cushion for the 
window-seats.... 

"of course, i really can't afford it," he muttered, as he went for a 
two-foot and began to measure the width of the window recesses.... 

in stoop to measure a recess, his attitude sudden change to one of 
interest and attention. presently he rose again, rubbing his hand with 
gentle glee. 

"oho, oho!" he said. "these look to me very much like window-boxes, 
nail up. we must look into this! yes, those are boxes, or 
i'm ... oho, this is an adventure!" 

on that wall of his sitting-room there were two window (the third was in 
another corner), and, beyond the open bedroom door, on the same wall, was 
another. the seat of all had been painted, repainted, and paint again; 
and oleron's investigate finger had barely detect the old nailhead 
beneath the paint. under the ledge over which he stoop an old keyhole 
also had been putty up. oleron took out his penknife. 

he work careful for five minutes, and then went into the kitchen for 
a hammer and chisel. driving the chisel cautious under the seat, he 
start the whole lid slightly. again use the penknife, he cut along 
the hinge edge and outward along the ends; and then he fetch a 
wedge and a wooden mallet. 

"now for our little mystery--" he said. 

the sound of the mallet on the wedge seemed, in that sweet and pale 
apartment, somehow a little brutal--nay, even shocking. the panelling 
rang and rattle and vibrate to the blow like a sounding-board. the 
whole house seem to echo; from the roomy cellarage to the garret 
above a flock of echo seem to awake; and the sound got a little on 
oleron's nerves. all at once he paused, fetch a duster, and muffle the 
mallet.... when the edge was sufficient raise he put his finger under 
it and lifted. the paint flake and star a little; the rusty old 
nail squeak and grunted; and the lid came up, lay open the box 
beneath. oleron look into it. save for a couple of inch of scurf and 
mould and old cobweb it was empty. 

"no treasure there," said oleron, a little amuse that he should have 
fancy there might have been. "_romilly_ will still have to be out by 
the autumn. let's have a look at the others." 

he turn to the second window. 

the raise of the two remain seat occupy him until well into the 
afternoon. that of the bedroom, like the first, was empty; but from the 
second seat of his sitting-room he drew out something yielding and fold 
and fur over an inch thick with dust. he carry the object into the 
kitchen, and have swept it over a bucket, took a duster to it. 

it was some sort of a large bag, of an ancient frieze-like material, and 
when unfold it occupy the greater part of the small kitchen floor. in 
shape it was an irregular, a very irregular, triangle, and it had a 
couple of wide flaps, with the remains of strap and buckles. the patch 
that had been uppermost in the fold was of a fade yellowish brown; 
but the rest of it was of shades of crimson that varied according to the 
exposure of the parts of it. 

"now whatever can that have been?" oleron muse as he stood survey 
it.... "i give it up. whatever it is, it's settled my work for today, 
i'm afraid--" 

he fold the object up careless and thrust it into a corner of the 
kitchen; then, taking pan and brush and an old knife, he return to 
the sitting-room and began to scrape and to wash and to line with paper 
his newly discover receptacles. when he had finished, he put his spare 
boots and books and papers into them; and he closed the lid again, 
amuse with his little adventure, but also a little anxious for the hour 
to come when he should settle fairly down to his work again. 


iii 

it pique oleron a little that his friend, miss bengough, should dismiss 
with a glance the place he himself had found so singularly winning. 
indeed she scarcely lift her eyes to it. but then she had always been 
more or less like that--a little indifferent to the graces of life, 
careless of appearances, and perhaps a shade more herself when she ate 
biscuit from a paper bag than when she dine with greater observance of 
the convenances. she was an unattached journalist of thirty-four, large, 
showy, fair as butter, pink as a dog-rose, remind one of a florist's 
picked specimen bloom, and given to sudden and ample movements and moist 
and explosive utterances. she "pulled a better living out of the pool" 
(as she express it) than oleron did; and by cunning disguise puff 
of draper and haberdasher she "pulled" also the greater part of her 
very varied wardrobe. she left small whirlwind of air behind her when 
she moved, in which her veil and scarf flutter and spun. 

oleron heard the flurry of her skirt on his staircase and her single 
loud knock at his door when he had been a month in his new abode. her 
garment brought in the outer air, and she flung a bundle of ladies' 
journal down on a chair. 

"don't knock off for me," she said across a mouthful of large-headed 
hatpin as she remove her hat and veil. "i didn't know whether you were 
straight yet, so i've brought some sandwich for lunch. you've got 
coffee, i suppose?--no, don't get up--i'll find the kitchen--" 

"oh, that's all right, i'll clear these things away. to tell the truth, 
i'm rather glad to be interrupted," said oleron. 

he gather his work together and put it away. she was already in the 
kitchen; he heard the running of water into the kettle. he join her, 
and ten minutes later follow her back to the sitting-room with the 
coffee and sandwich on a tray. they sat down, with the tray on a small 
table between them. 

"well, what do you think of the new place?" oleron ask as she pour 
out coffee. 

"hm!... anybody'd think you were go to get married, paul." 

he laughed. 

"oh no. but it's an improvement on some of them, isn't it?" 

"is it? i suppose it is; i don't know. i like the last place, in spite 
of the black ceiling and no watertap. how's _romilly_?" 

oleron thumb his chin. 

"hm! i'm rather ashamed to tell you. the fact is, i've not got on very 
well with it. but it will be all right on the night, as you use to say." 

"stuck?" 

"rather stuck." 

"got any of it you care to read to me?..." 

oleron had long been in the habit of reading portion of his work to miss 
bengough occasionally. her comment were always quick and practical, 
sometimes directly useful, sometimes indirect suggestive. she, in 
return for his confidence, always kept all mention of her own work 
sedulous from him. his, she said, was "real work"; hers merely fill 
space, not always even grammatically. 

"i'm afraid there isn't," oleron replied, still meditative dry-shaving 
his chin. then he added, with a little burst of candour, "the fact 
is, elsie, i've not written--not actually written--very much more of 
it--_any_ more of it, in fact. but, of course, that doesn't mean i 
haven't progressed. i've progressed, in one sense, rather alarmingly. 
i'm now thinking of reconstruct the whole thing." 

miss bengough gave a gasp. "reconstructing!" 

"making romil herself a different type of woman. somehow, i've begun to 
feel that i'm not get the most out of her. as she stands, i've 
certainly lost interest in her to some extent." 

"but--but--" miss bengough protested, "you had her so real, so _living_, 
paul!" 

oleron smile faintly. he had been quite prepared for miss bengough's 
disapproval. he wasn't surprise that she like romil as she at present 
existed; she would. whether she realise it or not, there was much of 
herself in his fictitious creation. naturally romil would seem "real," 
"living," to her.... 

"but are you really serious, paul?" miss bengough ask presently, with a 
round-eyed stare. 

"quite serious." 

"you're really go to scrap those fifteen chapters?" 

"i didn't exactly say that." 

"that fine, rich love-scene?" 

"i should only do it reluctantly, and for the sake of something i thought 
better." 

"and that beautiful, _beau_tiful description of romil on the shore?" 

"it wouldn't necessarily be wasted," he said a little uneasily. 

but miss bengough made a large and windy gesture, and then let him have 
it. 

"really, you are _too_ trying!" she broke out. "i do wish sometimes you'd 
remember you're human, and live in a world! you know i'd be the _last_ to 
wish you to lower your standard one inch, but it wouldn't be lower it 
to bring it within human comprehension. oh, you're sometimes altogether 
too godlike!... why, it would be a wicked, criminal waste of your powers 
to destroy those fifteen chapters! look at it reasonably, now. you've 
been working for nearly twenty years; you've now got what you've been 
working for almost within your grasp; your affair are at a most critical 
stage (oh, don't tell me; i know you're about at the end of your money); 
and here you are, deliberate propose to withdraw a thing that will 
probably make your name, and to substitute for it something that ten to 
one nobody on earth will ever want to read--and small blame to them! 
really, you try my patience!" 

oleron had shaken his head slow as she had talked. it was an old story 
between them. the noisy, able, practical journalist was an admirable 
friend--up to a certain point; beyond that ... well, each of us know 
that point beyond which we stand alone. elsie bengough sometimes said 
that had she had one-tenth part of oleron's genius there were few things 
she could not have done--thus making that genius a quantitative 
divisible thing, a sort of ingredient, to be add to or subtract 
from in the admixture of his work. that it was a qualitative thing, 
essential, indivisible, informing, pass her comprehension. their 
spirits part company at that point. oleron knew it. she did not appear 
to know it. 

"yes, yes, yes," he said a little wearily, by-and-by, "practically you're 
quite right, entire right, and i haven't a word to say. if i could only 
turn _romilly_ over to you you'd make an enormous success of her. but 
that can't be, and i, for my part, am seriously doubt whether she's 
worth my while. you know what that means." 

"what do it mean?" she demand bluntly. 

"well," he said, smile wanly, "what _does_ it mean when you're 
convinced a thing isn't worth doing? you simply don't do it." 

miss bengough's eyes swept the ceiling for assistance against this 
impossible man. 

"what utter rubbish!" she broke out at last. "why, when i saw you last 
you were simply ooze _romilly_; you were turning her off at the rate of 
four chapter a week; if you hadn't move you'd have had her three-parts 
done by now. what on earth possessed you to move right in the middle of 
your most important work?" 

oleron try to put her off with a recital of inconveniences, but she 
wouldn't have it. perhaps in her heart she partly suspect the reason. 
he was simply mortally weary of the narrow circumstances of his life. he 
had had twenty years of it--twenty years of garret and roof-chambers 
and dingy flat and shabby lodgings, and he was tired of dingy and 
shabbiness. the reward was as far off as ever--or if it was not, he no 
long care as once he would have care to put out his hand and take it. 
it is all very well to tell a man who is at the point of exhaustion that 
only another effort is require of him; if he cannot make it he is as far 
off as ever.... 

"anyway," oleron sum up, "i'm happy here than i've been for a long 
time. that's some sort of a justification." 

"and do no work," said miss bengough pointedly. 

at that a trifling petulance that had been gathering in oleron came to a 
head. 

"and why should i do nothing but work?" he demanded. "how much happy am 
i for it? i don't say i don't love my work--when it's done; but i hate 
do it. sometimes it's an intolerable burden that i simply long to be 
rid of. once in many week it has a moment, one moment, of glow and 
thrill for me; i remember the days when it was all glow and thrill; and 
now i'm forty-four, and it's becoming drudgery. nobody wants it; i'm 
cease to want it myself; and if any ordinary sensible man were to ask 
me whether i didn't think i was a fool to go on, i think i should agree 
that i was." 

miss bengough's comely pink face was serious. 

"but you knew all that, many, many years ago, paul--and still you chose 
it," she said in a low voice. 

"well, and how should i have known?" he demanded. "i didn't know. i was 
told so. my heart, if you like, told me so, and i thought i knew. youth 
always think it knows; then one day it discover that it is nearly 
fifty--" 

"forty-four, paul--" 

"--forty-four, then--and it find that the glamour isn't in front, 
but behind. yes, i knew and chose, if _that's_ knowing and 
choose ... but it's a costly choice we're call on to make when 
we're young!" 

miss bengough's eyes were on the floor. without moving them she said, 
"you're not regret it, paul?" 

"am i not?" he took her up. "upon my word, i've lately thought i am! what 
_do_ i get in return for it all?" 

"you know what you get," she replied. 

he might have known from her tone what else he could have had for the 
holding up of a finger--herself. she knew, but could not tell him, that 
he could have done no better thing for himself. had he, any time these 
ten years, ask her to marry him, she would have reply quietly, 
"very well; when?" he had never thought of it.... 

"yours is the real work," she continue quietly. "without you we jackal 
couldn't exist. you and a few like you hold everything upon your 
shoulders." 

for a minute there was a silence. then it occur to oleron that this 
was common vulgar grumbling. it was not his habit. sudden he rose and 
began to stack cup and plate on the tray. 

"sorry you catch me like this, elsie," he said, with a little 
laugh.... "no, i'll take them out; then we'll go for a walk, if you 
like...." 

he carry out the tray, and then began to show miss bengough round his 
flat. she made few comments. in the kitchen she ask what an old fade 
square of reddish frieze was, that mrs. barrett use as a cushion for her 
wooden chair. 

"that? i should be glad if you could tell _me_ what it is," oleron 
reply as he unfold the bag and related the story of its finding in 
the window-seat. 

"i think i know what it is," said miss bengough. "it's been use to wrap 
up a harp before putt it into its case." 

"by jove, that's probably just what it was," said oleron. "i could make 
neither head nor tail of it...." 

they finished the tour of the flat, and return to the sitting-room. 

"and who lives in the rest of the house?" miss bengough asked. 

"i dare say a tramp sleep in the cellar occasionally. nobody else." 

"hm!... well, i'll tell you what i think about it, if you like." 

"i should like." 

"you'll never work here." 

"oh?" said oleron quickly. "why not?" 

"you'll never finish _romilly_ here. why, i don't know, but you won't. 
i know it. you'll have to leave before you get on with that book." 

he muse for a moment, and then said: 

"isn't that a little--prejudiced, elsie?" 

"perfectly ridiculous. as an argument it hasn't a leg to stand on. but 
there it is," she replied, her mouth once more full of the large-headed 
hat pins. 

oleron was reach down his hat and coat. he laughed. 

"i can only hope you're entire wrong," he said, "for i shall be in a 
serious mess if _romilly_ isn't out in the autumn." 


iv 

as oleron sat by his fire that evening, ponder miss bengough's 
prognostication that difficulty await him in his work, he came to the 
conclusion that it would have been far better had she kept her belief to 
herself. no man do a thing better for have his confidence damp at 
the outset, and to speak of difficulty is in a sense to make them. 
speech itself become a deterrent act, to which other discouragement 
accrete until the very event of which warning is given is as likely as 
not to come to pass. he heartily confounded her. an influence hostile 
to the completion of _romilly_ had been born. 

and in some illogical, dogmatic way women seem to have, she had attach 
this antagonist influence to his new abode. was ever anything so 
absurd! "you'll never finish _romilly_ here." ... why not? was this her 
idea of the luxury that sap the spring of action and bring a man down 
to indolence and drop out of the race? the place was well enough--it 
was entire charming, for that matter--but it was not so demoralise as 
all that! no; elsie had miss the mark that time.... 

he move his chair to look round the room that smiled, positively 
smiled, in the firelight. he too smiled, as if pity was to be 
entertain for a malign apartment. even that slight lack of robust 
colour he had remark was not noticeable in the soft glow. the drawn 
chintz curtains--they had a flowered and trellis pattern, with basket 
and oaten pipes--fell in long quiet fold to the window-seats; the row 
of binding in old bookcase took the light richly; the last trace of 
sallow had gone with the daylight; and, if the truth must be told, 
it had been elsie herself who had seem a little out of the picture. 

that reflection struck him a little, and presently he return to it. 
yes, the room had, quite accidentally, done miss bengough a disservice 
that afternoon. it had, in some subtle but unmistakable way, placed her, 
marked a contrast of qualities. assume for the sake of argument the 
slightly ridiculous proposition that the room in which oleron sat _was_ 
characterise by a certain sparse and lack of vigour; so much the worse 
for miss bengough; she certainly err on the side of redundancy and 
general muchness. and if one must contrast abstract qualities, oleron 
inclined to the austere in taste.... 

yes, here oleron had made a distinct discovery; he wonder he had not 
made it before. he picture miss bengough again as she had appear 
that afternoon--large, showy, moist pink, with that quality of the 
prize bloom exuding, as it were, from her; and instantly she suffer in 
his thought. he even recognise now that he had notice something odd at 
the time, and that unconscious his attitude, even while she had been 
there, had been one of criticism. the mechanism of her was a little 
obvious; her melting humidity was the result of analyse processes; and 
behind her there had seem to lurk some dim shape emblematic of 
mortality. he had never, during the ten years of their intimacy, dream 
for a moment of ask her to marry him; none the less, he now felt for 
the first time a thankful that he had not done so.... 

then, sudden and swiftly, his face flame that he should be thinking 
thus of his friend. what! elsie bengough, with whom he had spent week 
and week of afternoons--she, the good chum, on whose help he would have 
count had all the rest of the world fail him--she, whose loyalty to 
him would not, he knew, swerve as long as there was breath in her--elsie 
to be even in thought dissect thus! he was an ingrate and a cad.... 

had she been there in that moment he would have abase himself before 
her. 

for ten minutes and more he sat, still gaze into the fire, with that 
humiliate red fade slow from his cheeks. all was still within and 
without, save for a tiny musical tinkle that came from his kitchen--the 
dripping of water from an imperfect turned-off tap into the vessel 
beneath it. mechanical he began to beat with his finger to the faint 
heard fall of the drops; the tiny regular movement seem to hasten 
that shameful withdrawal from his face. he grew cool once more; and when 
he resume his meditation he was all unconscious that he took it up again 
at the same point.... 

it was not only her florid superfluity of build that he had approach in 
the attitude of criticism; he was conscious also of the wide difference 
between her mind and his own. he felt no thankful that up to a 
certain point their nature had ever run companionable side by side; he 
was now full of question beyond that point. their intellect diverged; 
there was no deny it; and, look back, he was inclined to doubt 
whether there had been any real coincidence. true, he had read his 
writings to her and she had appear to speak comprehending and to the 
point; but what can a man do who, have assume that another see as he 
does, is sudden brought up sharp by something that falsify and 
discredit all that has gone before? he doubt all now.... it did for a 
moment occur to him that the man who demand of a friend more than can be 
given to him is in danger of lose that friend, but he put the thought 
aside. 

again he cease to think, and again move his finger to the distant 
dripping of the tap.... 

and now (he resume by-and-by), if these things were true of elsie 
bengough, they were also true of the creation of which she was the 
prototype--romilly bishop. and since he could say of romil what for 
very shame he could not say of elsie, he gave his thought rein. he did 
so in that smiling, fire-lighted room, to the accompaniment of the 
faint heard tap. 

there was no long any doubt about it; he hate the central character 
of his novel. even as he had describe her physically she overpower 
the senses; she was coarse-fibred, over-coloured, rank. it became true 
the moment he formulate his thought; gulliver had describe the 
brobdingnagian maids-of-honour thus: and mental and spiritual she 
corresponded--was unsensitive, limited, common. the model (he closed his 
eyes for a moment)--the model stuck out through fifteen vulgar and 
blatant chapter to such a pitch that, without seeing the reason, he had 
been unable to begin the sixteenth. he marvel that it had only just 
dawn upon him. 

and _this_ was to have been his beatrice, his vision! as elsie she was to 
have gone into the furnace of his art, and she was to have come out the 
woman all men desire! her thought were to have been cull from his own 
finest, her form from his dearest dreams, and her setting wherever he 
could find one fit for her worth. he had brood long before making the 
attempt; then one day he had felt her stir within him as a mother feel 
a quickening, and he had begun to write; and so he had add chapter to 
chapter.... 

and those fifteen sodden chapter were what he had produced! 

again he sat, soft moving his finger.... 

then he bestir himself. 

she must go, all fifteen chapter of her. that was settled. for what was 
to take her place his mind was a blank; but one thing at a time; a man 
is not excuse from taking the wrong course because the right one is not 
immediately reveal to him. better would come if it was to come; 
in the meantime-- 

he rose, fetch the fifteen chapters, and read them over before he 
should drop them into the fire. 

but instead of putt them into the fire he let them fall from his hand. 
he became conscious of the dripping of the tap again. it had a tinkle 
gamut of four or five notes, on which it rang irregular changes, and it 
was foolish sweet and dulcimer-like. in his mind oleron could see the 
gathering of each drop, its little tremble on the lip of the tap, and the 
tiny percussion of its fall, "plink--plunk," minimise almost to 
inaudibility. following the lowest note there seem to be a brief 
phrase, irregular repeated; and presently oleron found himself wait 
for the recurrence of this phrase. it was quite pretty.... 

but it did not conduce to wakefulness, and oleron doze over his fire. 

when he awoke again the fire had burn low and the flame of the candle 
were licking the rim of the sheffield sticks. sluggish he rose, 
yawned, went his nightly round of door-locks and window-fastenings, and 
pass into his bedroom. soon he slept soundly. 

but a curious little sequel follow on the morrow. mrs. barrett usually 
tapped, not at his door, but at the wooden wall beyond which lay oleron's 
bed; and then oleron rose, put on his dressing-gown, and admitted her. he 
was not conscious that as he did so that morning he hum an air; but 
mrs. barrett linger with her hand on the door-knob and her face a 
little avert and smiling. 

"de-ar me!" her soft falsetto rose. "but that will be a very o-ald tune, 
mr. oleron! i will not have heard it this for-ty years!" 

"what tune?" oleron asked. 

"the tune, indeed, that you was humming, sir." 

oleron had his thumb in the flap of a letter. it remain there. 

"_i_ was humming?... sing it, mrs. barrett." 

mrs. barrett prut-prutted. 

"i have no voice for singing, mr. oleron; it was ann pugh was the singe 
of our family; but the tune will be very o-ald, and it is call 'the 
beckon fair one.'" 

"try to sing it," said oleron, his thumb still in the envelope; and mrs. 
barrett, with much dimple and confusion, hum the air. 

"they do say it was sung to a harp, mr. oleron, and it will be very 
o-ald," she concluded. 

"and _i_ was singing that?" 

"indeed you wass. i would not be likely to tell you lies." 

with a "very well--let me have breakfast," oleron open his letter; but 
the trifling circumstance struck him as more odd than he would have 
admitted to himself. the phrase he had hum had been that which he had 
associate with the fall from the tap on the evening before. 


v 

even more curious than that the commonplace dripping of an ordinary 
water-tap should have tally so close with an actually existing air 
was another result it had, namely, that it awakened, or seem to awaken, 
in oleron an abnormal sensitive to other noise of the old house. it 
has been remark that silence obtain its fullest and most impressive 
quality when it is broken by some minute sound; and, truth to tell, the 
place was never still. perhaps the mild of the spring air operate on 
its torpid old timbers; perhaps oleron's fire cause it to stretch its 
old anatomy; and certainly a whole world of insect life bore and 
burrow in its baulk and joists. at any rate, oleron had only to sit 
quiet in his chair and to wait for a minute or two in order to become 
aware of such a change in the auditory scale as come upon a man who, 
conceive the midsummer wood to be motionless and still, all at once 
find his ear sharpen to the crepitation of a myriad insects. 

and he smile to think of man's arbitrary distinction between that which 
has life and that which has not. here, quite apart from such recognise 
sound as the scamper of mice, the fall of plaster behind his 
panelling, and the pop of purse or coffin from his fire, was a 
whole house talk to him had he but known its language. beam settled 
with a tired sigh into their old mortices; creature tick in the walls; 
joint cracked, boards complained; with no palpable stirring of the air 
window-sashes change their position with a soft knock in their frames. 
and whether the place had life in this sense or not, it had at all event 
a winsome personality. it need but an hour of muse for oleron to 
conceive the idea that, as his own body stood in friendly relation to his 
soul, so, by an extension and an attenuation, his habitation might 
fantastic be supposed to stand in some relation to himself. he even 
amuse himself with the far-fetched fancy that he might so identify 
himself with the place that some future tenant, taking possession, might 
regard it as in a sense haunted. it would be rather a joke if he, a 
perfectly harmless author, with nothing on his mind worse than a novel he 
had discover he must begin again, should turn out to be lay the 
foundation of a future ghost!... 

in proportion, however, as he felt this grow attachment to the fabric 
of his abode, elsie bengough, from being merely unattracted, began to 
show a dislike of the place that was more and more marked. and she did 
not scruple to speak of her aversion. 

"it doesn't belong to to-day at all, and for you especially it's bad," 
she said with decision. "you're only too ready to let go your hold on 
actual things and to slip into apathy; _you_ ought to be in a place 
with concrete floor and a patent gas-meter and a tradesmen's lift. and 
it would do you all the good in the world if you had a job that made you 
scramble and rub elbow with your fellow-men. now, if i could get you a 
job, for, say, two or three days a week, one that would allow you heap 
of time for your proper work--would you take it?" 

somehow, oleron resent a little being diagnose like this. he thank 
miss bengough, but without a smile. 

"thank you, but i don't think so. after all each of us has his own life 
to live," he could not refrain from adding. 

"his own life to live!... how long is it since you were out, paul?" 

"about two hours." 

"i don't mean to buy stamp or to post a letter. how long is it since you 
had anything like a stretch?" 

"oh, some little time perhaps. i don't know." 

"since i was here last?" 

"i haven't been out much." 

"and has _romilly_ progress much better for your being coop up?" 

"i think she has. i'm lay the foundations of her. i shall begin the 
actual writing presently." 

it seem as if miss bengough had forgotten their tussle about the first 
_romilly_. she frowned, turn half away, and then quick turn again. 

"ah!... so you've still got that ridiculous idea in your head?" 

"if you mean," said oleron slowly, "that i've discard the old 
_romilly_, and am at work on a new one, you're right. i have still got 
that idea in my head." 

something uncordial in his tone struck her; but she was a fighter. his 
own absurd sensitive harden her. she gave a "pshaw!" of impatience. 

"where is the old one?" she demand abruptly. 

"why?" ask oleron. 

"i want to see it. i want to show some of it to you. i want, if you're 
not wool-gathering entirely, to bring you back to your senses." 

this time it was he who turn his back. but when he turn round again 
he spoke more gently. 

"it's no good, elsie. i'm responsible for the way i go, and you must 
allow me to go it--even if it should seem wrong to you. believe me, i 
am give thought to it.... the manuscript? i was on the point of burning 
it, but i didn't. it's in that window-seat, if you must see it." 

miss bengough cross quick to the window-seat, and lift the lid. 
sudden she gave a little exclamation, and put the back of her hand 
to her mouth. she spoke over her shoulder: 

"you ought to knock those nail in, paul," she said. 

he strode to her side. 

"what? what is it? what's the matter?" he asked. "i did knock them 
in--or, rather, pull them out." 

"you left enough to scratch with," she replied, showing her hand. from 
the upper wrist to the knuckle of the little finger a well red wound 
showed. 

"good--gracious!" oleron ejaculated.... "here, come to the bathroom and 
bathe it quickly--" 

he hurried her to the bathroom, turn on warm water, and bath and 
cleanse the bad gash. then, still holding the hand, he turn cold water 
on it, utter broken phrase of astonishment and concern. 

"good lord, how did that happen! as far as i knew i'd ... is this water 
too cold? do that hurt? i can't imagine how on earth ... there; that'll 
do--" 

"no--one moment longer--i can bear it," she murmured, her eyes closed.... 

presently he led her back to the sitting-room and bound the hand in one 
of his handkerchiefs; but his face did not lose its expression of 
perplexity. he had spent half a day in opening and making serviceable the 
three window-boxes, and he could not conceive how he had come to leave an 
inch and a half of rusty nail standing in the wood. he himself had open 
the lid of each of them a dozen times and had not notice any nail; but 
there it was.... 

"it shall come out now, at all events," he muttered, as he went for a 
pair of pincers. and he made no mistake about it that time. 

elsie bengough had sunk into a chair, and her face was rather white; but 
in her hand was the manuscript of _romilly_. she had not finished with 
_romilly_ yet. presently she return to the charge. 

"oh, paul, it will be the greatest mistake you ever, _ever_ made if you 
do not publish this!" she said. 

he hung his head, genuine distressed. he couldn't get that incident of 
the nail out of his head, and _romilly_ occupy a second place in his 
thought for the moment. but still she insisted; and when presently he 
spoke it was almost as if he ask her pardon for something. 

"what can i say, elsie? i can only hope that when you see the new 
version, you'll see how right i am. and if in spite of all you _don't_ 
like her, well ..." he made a hopeless gesture. "don't you see that i 
_must_ be guide by my own lights?" 

she was silent. 

"come, elsie," he said gently. "we've got along well so far; don't let us 
split on this." 

the last words had hardly pass his lip before he regret them. she 
had been nursing her injure hand, with her eyes once more closed; but 
her lip and lid quiver simultaneously. her voice shook as she spoke. 

"i can't help saying it, paul, but you are so greatly changed." 

"hush, elsie," he murmur soothingly; "you've had a shock; rest for a 
while. how could i change?" 

"i don't know, but you are. you've not been yourself ever since you came 
here. i wish you'd never seen the place. it's stop your work, it's 
making you into a person i hardly know, and it's made me horrible anxious 
about you.... oh, how my hand is beginning to throb!" 

"poor child!" he murmured. "will you let me take you to a doctor and have 
it properly dressed?" 

"no--i shall be all right presently--i'll keep it raised----" 

she put her elbow on the back of her chair, and the bandage hand rest 
lightly on his shoulder. 

at that touch an entire new anxiety stir sudden within him. 
hundred of times previously, on their jaunt and excursions, she had 
slip her hand within his arm as she might have slip it into the arm 
of a brother, and he had accept the little affectionate gesture as a 
brother might have accept it. but now, for the first time, there rush 
into his mind a hundred startle questions. her eyes were still closed, 
and her head had fallen pathetic back; and there was a lost and 
ineffable smile on her part lips. the truth broke in upon him. good 
god!... and he had never divine it! 

and stranger than all was that, now that he did see that she was lost in 
love of him, there came to him, not sorrow and humility and abasement, 
but something else that he struggle in vain against--something entire 
strange and new, that, had he analyse it, he would have found to be 
petulance and irritation and resentment and ungentleness. the sudden 
selfish prompt master him before he was aware. he all but gave it 
words. what was she do there at all? why was she not get on with 
her own work? why was she here interfere with his? who had given her 
this guardianship over him that lately she had put forward so 
assertively?--"changed?" it was she, not himself, who had changed.... 

but by the time she had open her eyes again he had overcome his 
resentment sufficient to speak gently, albeit with reserve. 

"i wish you would let me take you to a doctor." 

she rose. 

"no, thank you, paul," she said. "i'll go now. if i need a dressing i'll 
get one; take the other hand, please. good-bye--" 

he did not attempt to detain her. he walk with her to the foot of the 
stairs. half-way along the narrow alley she turned. 

"it would be a long way to come if you happen not to be in," she said; 
"i'll send you a postcard the next time." 

at the gate she turn again. 

"leave here, paul," she said, with a mournful look. "everything's wrong 
with this house." 

then she was gone. 

oleron return to his room. he cross straight to the window-box. he 
open the lid and stood long look at it. then he closed it again and 
turn away. 

"that's rather frightening," he muttered. "it's simply not possible that 
i should not have remove that nail...." 


vi 

oleron knew very well what elsie had meant when she had said that her 
next visit would be precede by a postcard. she, too, had realise that 
at last, at last he knew--knew, and didn't want her. it gave him a 
miserable, pitiful pang, therefore, when she came again within a week, 
knock at the door unannounced. she spoke from the landing; she did not 
intend to stay, she said; and he had to press her before she would so 
much as enter. 

her excuse for calling was that she had heard of an inquiry for short 
story that he might be wise to follow up. he thank her. then, her 
business over, she seem anxious to get away again. oleron did not seek 
to detain her; even he saw through the pretext of the stories; and he 
accompany her down the stairs. 

but elsie bengough had no luck whatever in that house. a second accident 
befell her. half-way down the staircase there was the sharp sound of 
splinter wood, and she checked a loud cry. oleron knew the woodwork to 
be old, but he himself had ascend and descended frequent enough 
without mishap.... 

elsie had put her foot through one of the stairs. 

he sprang to her side in alarm. 

"oh, i say! my poor girl!" 

she laugh hysterically. 

"it's my weight--i know i'm get fat--" 

"keep still--let me clear these splinter away," he mutter between his 
teeth. 

she continue to laugh and sob that it was her weight--she was get 
fat-- 

he thrust downwards at the broken boards. the extricate was no easy 
matter, and her torn boot show him how badly the foot and ankle 
within it must be abraded. 

"good god--good god!" he mutter over and over again. 

"i shall be too heavy for anything soon," she sob and laughed. 

but she refuse to reascend and to examine her hurt. 

"no, let me go quickly--let me go quickly," she repeated. 

"but it's a frightful gash!" 

"no--not so bad--let me get away quickly--i'm--i'm not wanted." 

at her words, that she was not wanted, his head drop as if she had 
given him a buffet. 

"elsie!" he choked, broken and shocked. 

but she too made a quick gesture, as if she put something violent 
aside. 

"oh, paul, not _that_--not _you_--of course i do mean that too in a 
sense--oh, you know what i mean!... but if the other can't be, spare 
me this now! i--i wouldn't have come, but--but--oh, i did, i _did_ try to 
keep away!" 

it was intolerable, heartbreaking; but what could he do--what could he 
say? he did not love her... 

"let me go--i'm not wanted--let me take away what's left of me--" 

"dear elsie--you are very dear to me--" 

but again she made the gesture, as of putt something violent aside. 

"no, not that--not anything less--don't offer me anything less--leave me 
a little pride--" 

"let me get my hat and coat--let me take you to a doctor," he muttered. 

but she refused. she refuse even the support of his arm. she gave 
another unsteady laugh. 

"i'm sorry i broke your stairs, paul.... you will go and see about the 
short stories, won't you?" 

he groaned. 

"then if you won't see a doctor, will you go across the square and let 
mrs. barrett look at you? look, there's barrett passing now--" 

the long-nosed barrett was look curious down the alley, but as 
oleron was about to call him he made off without a word. elsie seem 
anxious for nothing so much as to be clear of the place, and finally 
promise to go straight to a doctor, but insist on go alone. 

"good-bye," she said. 

and oleron watch her until she was past the hatchet-like "to let" 
boards, as if he fear that even they might fall upon her and maim her. 

that night oleron did not dine. he had far too much on his mind. he 
walk from room to room of his flat, as if he could have walk away 
from elsie bengough's haunting cry that still rang in his ears. "i'm 
not wanted--don't offer me anything less--let me take away what's left 
of me--" 

oh, if he could only have persuade himself that he love her! 

he walk until twilight fell, then, without lighting candles, he stir 
up the fire and flung himself into a chair. 

poor, poor elsie!... 

but even while his heart ache for her, it was out of the question. 
if only he had known! if only he had use common observation! but 
those walks, those sisterly takings of the arm--what a fool he had 
been!... well, it was too late now. it was she, not he, who must now 
act--act by keeping away. he would help her all he could. he himself 
would not sit in her presence. if she came, he would hurry her out again 
as fast as he could.... poor, poor elsie! 

his room grew dark; the fire burn dead; and he continue to sit, 
wince from time to time as a fresh torture phrase rang again in his 
ears. 

then suddenly, he knew not why, he found himself anxious for her in a new 
sense--uneasy about her personal safety. a horrible fancy that even then 
she might be look over an embankment down into dark water, that she 
might even now be glancing up at the hook on the door, took him. women 
had been known to do those things.... then there would be an inquest, and 
he himself would be call upon to identify her, and would be ask how 
she had come by an ill-healed wound on the hand and a bad abrasion of the 
ankle. barrett would say that he had seen her leave his house.... 

then he recognise that his thought were morbid. by an effort of will he 
put them aside, and sat for a while listen to the faint creak 
and ticking and rap within his panelling.... if only he could have 
married her!... but he couldn't. her face had risen before him again 
as he had seen it on the stairs, drawn with pain and ugly and swollen 
with tears. ugly--yes, positively blubbered; if tear were women's 
weapons, as they were said to be, such tear were weapon turn against 
themselves ... suicide again.... 

then all at once he found himself attentive considering her two 
accidents. 

extraordinary they had been, both of them. he _could not_ have left that 
old nail standing in the wood; why, he had fetch tool specially from 
the kitchen; and he was convinced that that step that had broken beneath 
her weight had been as sound as the others. it was inexplicable. if these 
things could happen, anything could happen. there was not a beam nor a 
jamb in the place that might not fall without warning, not a plank that 
might not crash inwards, not a nail that might not become a dagger. the 
whole place was full of life even now; as he sat there in the dark he 
heard its crowd of noise as if the house had been one great 
microphone.... 

only half conscious that he did so, he had been sitting for some time 
identify these noises, attribute to each crack or creak or knock its 
material cause; but there was one noise which, again not fully conscious 
of the omission, he had not sought to account for. it had last come some 
minutes ago; it came again now--a sort of soft sweeping rustle that 
seem to hold an almost inaudible minute crackling. for half a minute or 
so it had oleron's attention; then his heavy thought were of elsie 
bengough again. 

he was near to loving her in that moment than he had ever been. he 
thought how to some men their love one were but the dear for those 
poor mortal blemish that tell us we are but sojourn on earth, with a 
common fate not far distant that make it hardly worth while to do 
anything but love for the time remaining. strangle sobs, bleare 
tears, body buffet by sickness, heart and mind callous and hard with 
the rub of the world--how little love there would be were these things a 
barrier to love! in that sense he did love elsie bengough. what her 
happiness had never move in him her sorrow almost awoke.... 

sudden his meditation went. his ear had once more become conscious 
of that soft and repeated noise--the long sweep with the almost 
inaudible crackle in it. again and again it came, with a curious 
insistence and urgency. it quicken a little as he became increasingly 
attentive ... it seem to oleron that it grew louder.... 

all at once he start bolt upright in his chair, tense and listening. 
the silky rustle came again; he was try to attach it to something.... 

the next moment he had leapt to his feet, unnerve and terrified. his 
chair hung poised for a moment, and then went over, setting the 
fire-irons clatter as it fell. there was only one noise in the world 
like that which had cause him to spring thus to his feet.... 

the next time it came oleron felt behind him at the empty air with his 
hand, and back slow until he found himself against the wall. 

"god in heaven!" the ejaculation broke from oleron's lips. the sound had 
ceased. 

the next moment he had given a high cry. 

"what is it? what's there? _who's_ there?" 

a sound of scuttle cause his knee to bend under him for a moment; but 
that, he knew, was a mouse. that was not something that his stomach 
turn sick and his mind reel to entertain. that other sound, the like 
of which was not in the world, had now entire ceased; and again he 
called.... 

he call and continue to call; and then another terror, a terror of the 
sound of his own voice, seize him. he did not dare to call again. his 
shake hand went to his pocket for a match, but found none. he thought 
there might be match on the mantelpiece-- 

he work his way to the mantelpiece round a little recess, without for a 
moment leave the wall. then his hand encounter the mantelpiece, and 
grope along it. a box of match fell to the hearth. he could just see 
them in the firelight, but his hand could not pick them up until he had 
corner them inside the fender. 

then he rose and struck a light. 

the room was as usual. he struck a second match. a candle stood on the 
table. he light it, and the flame sank for a moment and then burn up 
clear. again he look round. 

there was nothing. 

there was nothing; but there had been something, and might still be 
something. formerly, oleron had smile at the fantastic thought that, 
by a merge and interplay of identity between himself and his 
beautiful room, he might be prepare a ghost for the future; it had not 
occur to him _that there might have been a similar merge and 
coalesce in the past_. yet with this staggering impossible he was 
now face to face. something did persist in the house; it had a tenant 
other than himself; and that tenant, whatsoever or whosoever, had 
appall oleron's soul by produce the sound of a woman brush her 
hair. 


vii 

without quite knowing how he came to be there oleron found himself 
stride over the loose board he had temporary placed on the step 
broken by miss bengough. he was hatless, and descend the stairs. not 
until later did there return to him a hazy memory that he had left the 
candle burning on the table, had open the door no wide than was 
necessary to allow the passage of his body, and had sidle out, close 
the door soft behind him. at the foot of the stairs another shock 
await him. something dashed with a flurry up from the disused cellar 
and disappear out of the door. it was only a cat, but oleron gave a 
childish sob. 

he pass out of the gate, and stood for a moment under the "to let" 
boards, pluck foolish at his lip and look up at the glimmer 
of light behind one of his red blinds. then, still look over his 
shoulder, he move stumbling up the square. there was a small 
public-house round the corner; oleron had never enter it; but he 
enter it now, and put down a shilling that miss the counter by 
inches. 

"b--b--bran--brandy," he said, and then stoop to look for the shilling. 

he had the little sawdust bar to himself; what company there 
was--carters and labourer and the small tradesmen of the 
neighbourhood--was gather in the farther compartment, beyond the space 
where the white-haired landlady move among her taps and bottles. oleron 
sat down on a hardwood settee with a perforate seat, drank half his 
brandy, and then, thinking he might as well drink it as spill it, 
finished it. 

then he fell to wonder which of the men whose voice he heard across 
the public-house would undertake the removal of his effects on the 
morrow. 

in the meantime he ordered more brandy. 

for he did not intend to go back to that room where he had left the 
candle burning. oh no! he couldn't have face even the entry and the 
staircase with the broken step--certainly not that pith-white, 
fascinating room. he would go back for the present to his old 
arrangement, of workroom and separate sleeping-quarters; he would 
go to his old landlady at once--presently--when he had finished his 
brandy--and see if she could put him up for the night. his glass was 
empty now.... 

he rose, had it refilled, and sat down again. 

and if anybody ask his reason for remove again? oh, he had reason 
enough--reason enough! nail that put themselves back into wood again 
and gash people's hands, steps that broke when you trod on them, and 
women who came into a man's place and brush their hair in the dark, 
were reason enough! he was querulous and injure about it all. he had 
taken the place for himself, not for invisible women to brush their 
hair in; that lawyer fellow in lincoln's inn should be told so, too, 
before many hour were out; it was outrageous, letting people in for 
agreement like that! 

a cut-glass partition divide the compartment where oleron sat from the 
space where the white-haired landlady moved; but it stop seven or 
eight inch above the level of the counter. there was no partition at 
the farther bar. presently oleron, raise his eyes, saw that face were 
watch him through the aperture. the face disappear when he look 
at them. 

he move to a corner where he could not be seen from the other bar; but 
this brought him into line with the white-haired landlady. 

she knew him by sight--had doubtless seen him passing and repassing; and 
presently she made a remark on the weather. oleron did not know what he 
replied, but it suffice to call forth the further remark that the winter 
had been a bad one for influenza, but that the spring weather seem to 
be coming at last.... even this slight contact with the commonplace 
steady oleron a little; an idle, nascent wonder whether the landlady 
brush her hair every night, and, if so, whether it gave out those 
little electric cracklings, was shut down with a snap; and oleron was 
better.... 

with his next glass of brandy he was all for go back to his flat. not 
go back? indeed, he would go back! they should very soon see whether he 
was to be turn out of his place like that! he began to wonder why he 
was do the rather unusual thing he was do at that moment, unusual 
for him--sitting hatless, drink brandy, in a public-house. suppose he 
were to tell the white-haired landlady all about it--to tell her that a 
caller had scratch her hand on a nail, had later had the bad luck to 
put her foot through a rotten stair, and that he himself, in an old house 
full of squeak and creak and whispers, had heard a minute noise and had 
bolt from it in fright--what would she think of him? that he was mad, 
of course.... pshaw! the real truth of the matter was that he hadn't been 
do enough work to occupy him. he had been dream his days away, 
filling his head with a lot of moonshine about a new _romilly_ (as if the 
old one was not good enough), and now he was surprise that the devil 
should enter an empty head! 

yes, he would go back. he would take a walk in the air first--he hadn't 
walk enough lately--and then he would take himself in hand, settle 
the hash of that sixteenth chapter of _romilly_ (fancy, he had actually 
been fool enough to think of destroy fifteen chapters!) and 
thenceforward he would remember that he had obligation to his fellow-men 
and work to do in the world. there was the matter in a nutshell. 

he finished his brandy and went out. 

he had walk for some time before any other bearing of the matter than 
that on himself occur to him. at first, the fresh air had increase 
the heady effect of the brandy he had drunk; but afterwards his mind grew 
clear than it had been since morning. and the clear it grew, the less 
final did his boastful self-assurances become, and the firm his 
conviction that, when all explanation had been made, there remain 
something that could not be explained. his hysteria of an hour before had 
passed; he grew steady calmer; but the disquiet conviction remained. 
a deep fear took possession of him. it was a fear for elsie. 

for something in his place was inimical to her safety. of themselves, her 
two accident might not have persuade him of this; but she herself had 
said it. "_i'm not want here_..." and she had declared that there was 
something wrong with the place. she had seen it before he had. well and 
good. one thing stood out clearly: namely, that if this was so, she must 
be kept away for quite another reason than that which had so confounded 
and humiliate oleron. lucky she had express her intention of stay 
away; she must be held to that intention. he must see to it. 

and he must see to it all the more that he now saw his first impulse, 
never to set foot in the place again, was absurd. people did not do that 
kind of thing. with elsie made secure, he could not with any respect to 
himself suffer himself to be turn out by a shadow, nor even by a danger 
merely because it was a danger. he had to live somewhere, and he would 
live there. he must return. 

he master the faint chill of fear that came with the decision, and 
turn in his walk abruptly. should fear grow on him again he would, 
perhaps, take one more glass of brandy.... 

but by the time he reach the short street that led to the square he was 
too late for more brandy. the little public-house was still lighted, but 
closed, and one or two men were standing talk on the kerb. oleron 
notice that a sudden silence fell on them as he passed, and he notice 
further that the long-nosed barrett, whom he pass a little lower down, 
did not return his good-night. he turn in at the broken gate, hesitate 
merely an instant in the alley, and then mount his stairs again. 

only an inch of candle remain in the sheffield stick, and oleron did 
not light another one. deliberate he forced himself to take it up and 
to make the tour of his five rooms before retiring. it was as he return 
from the kitchen across his little hall that he notice that a letter lay 
on the floor. he carry it into his sitting-room, and glance at the 
envelope before opening it. 

it was unstamped, and had been put into the door by hand. its handwriting 
was clumsy, and it ran from beginning to end without comma or period. 
oleron read the first line, turn to the signature, and then finished 
the letter. 

it was from the man barrett, and it informed oleron that he, barrett, 
would be oblige if mr. oleron would make other arrangement for the 
prepare of his breakfast and the cleaning-out of his place. the sting 
lay in the tail, that is to say, the postscript. this consist of a text 
of scripture. it embody an allusion that could only be to elsie 
bengough.... 

a seldom-seen frown had cut deep into oleron's brow. so! that was it! 
very well; they would see about that on the morrow.... for the rest, this 
seem merely another reason why elsie should keep away.... 

then his suppress rage broke out.... 

the foul-minded lot! the devil himself could not have given a leer at 
anything that had ever pass between paul oleron and elsie bengough, 
yet this nose rascal must be pry and talking!... 

oleron crumple the paper up, held it in the candle flame, and then 
ground the ashes under his heel. 

one useful purpose, however, the letter had served: it had create in 
oleron a wrathful blaze that effectually banish pale shadows. 
nevertheless, one other puzzle circumstance was to close the day. as he 
undressed, he chance to glance at his bed. the coverlet bore an impress 
as if somebody had lain on them. oleron could not remember that he 
himself had lain down during the day--off-hand, he would have said that 
certainly he had not; but after all he could not be positive. his 
indignation for elsie, acting possibly with the residue of the brandy in 
him, exclude all other considerations; and he put out his candle, lay 
down, and pass immediately into a deep and dreamless sleep, which, in 
the absence of mrs. barrett's morning call, last almost once round the 
clock. 


viii 

to the man who pay heed to that voice within him which warn him that 
twilight and danger are settle over his soul, terror is apt to appear 
an absolute thing, against which his heart must be safeguard in a twink 
unless there is to take place an alteration in the whole range and scale 
of his nature. mercifully, he has never far to look for safeguards. of 
the immediate and small and common and momentary things of life, of 
usage and observance and mode and conventions, he build up 
fortification against the powers of darkness. he is even content that, 
not terror only, but joy also, should for working purpose be placed in 
the category of the absolute things; and the last treason he will commit 
will be that break down of terms and limit that strikes, not at one 
man, but at the welfare of the soul of all. 

in his own person, oleron began to commit this treason. he began to 
commit it by admit the inexplicable and horrible to an increase 
familiarity. he did it insensibly, unconsciously, by a neglect of the 
things that he now regard it as an impertinence in elsie bengough to 
have prescribed. two month before, the words "a haunt house," applied 
to his lovely bemuse dwelling, would have chill his marrow; now, 
his scale of sensation becoming depressed, he could ask "haunted by 
what?" and remain unconscious that horror, when it can be prove to be 
relative, by so much lose its proper quality. he was setting aside the 
landmarks. mists and confusion had begun to enwrap him. 

and he was conscious of nothing so much as of a voracious 
inquisitiveness. he want _to know_. he was resolve to know. nothing 
but the knowledge would satisfy him; and crafty he cast about for means 
whereby he might attain it. 

he might have spare his craft. the matter was the easiest imaginable. as 
in time past he had known, in his writing, moments when his thought 
had seem to rise of themselves and to embody themselves in words not to 
be alter afterwards, so now the question he put himself seem to be 
answer even in the moment of their asking. there was exhilarate in 
the swift, easy processes. he had known no so such joy in his own power 
since the days when his writing had been a daily fresh and a delight 
to him. it was almost as if the course he must pursue was being dictate 
to him. 

and the first thing he must do, of course, was to define the problem. he 
define it in terms of mathematics. grant that he had not the place to 
himself; grant that the old house had inexpressible caught and engaged 
his spirit; grant that, by virtue of the common denominator of the 
place, this unknown co-tenant stood in some relation to himself: what 
next? clearly, the nature of the other numerator must be ascertained. 

and how? ordinarily this would not have seem simple, but to oleron it 
was now pellucid clear. the key, _of course_, lay in his half-written 
novel--or rather, in both _romillys_, the old and the propose new one. 

a little while before oleron would have thought himself mad to have 
embrace such an opinion; now he accept the dizzy hypothesis without 
a quiver. 

he began to examine the first and second _romillys_. 

from the moment of his do so the thing advanced by leap and bounds. 
swift he review the history of the _romilly_ of the fifteen chapters. 
he remember clearly now that he had found her insufficient on the very 
first morning on which he had sat down to work in his new place. other 
instance of his aversion leap up to confirm his obscure investigation. 
there had come the night when he had hardly forborne to throw the whole 
thing into the fire; and the next morning he had begun the plan of 
the new _romilly_. it had been on that morning that mrs. barrett, 
overhear him hum a brief phrase that the dripping of a tap the 
night before had suggested, had informed him that he was singing some air 
he had never in his life heard before, call "the beckon fair 
one."... 

the beckon fair one!... 

with scarcely a pause in thought he continued: 

the first _romilly_ have been definitely thrown over, the second had 
instantly fasten herself upon him, clamour for birth in his brain. 
he even fancy now, look back, that there had been something like 
passion, hate almost, in the supplanting, and that more than once a stray 
thought given to his discard creation had--(it was astonish how 
credible oleron found the almost unthinkable idea)--had offend the 
supplanter. 

yet that a malignancy almost homicidal should be extend to his 
fiction's poor mortal prototype.... 

in spite of his inure to a scale in which the horrible was now a thing 
to be finger and turn this way and that, a "good god!" broke from 
oleron. 

this intrusion of the first _romilly's_ prototype into his thought 
again was a factor that for the moment brought his inquiry into the 
nature of his problem to a termination; the mere thought of elsie was 
fatal to anything abstract. for another thing, he could not yet think of 
that letter of barrett's, nor of a little scene that had follow it, 
without a mount of colour and a quick contraction of the brow. for, 
wise or not, he had had that argument out at once. stride across the 
square on the following morning, he had bearded barrett on his own 
doorstep. coming back again a few minutes later, he had been strong of 
opinion that he had only made matter worse. the man had been vague 
itself. he had not been to be either challenge or browbeaten into 
anything more definite than a mutter farrago in which the words 
"certain things ... mrs. barrett ... respectable house ... if the cap 
fit ... proceedings that shall be nameless," had been constant 
repeated. 

"not that i make any charge--" he had concluded. 

"charge!" oleron had cried. 

"i 'ave my idear of things, as i don't doubt you 'ave yours--" 

"ideas--mine!" oleron had cry wrathfully, immediately drop his 
voice as head had appear at window of the square. "look you here, my 
man; you've an unwholesome mind, which probably you can't help, but a 
tongue which you can help, and shall! if there is a breath of this 
repeated ..." 

"i'll not be talk to on my own doorstep like this by anybody,..." 
barrett had blustered.... 

"you shall, and i'm do it ..." 

"don't you forget there's a gawd above all, who 'as said..." 

"you're a low scandalmonger!..." 

and so forth, continue badly what was already badly begun. oleron had 
return wrathful to his own house, and thenceforward, look out 
of his windows, had seen barrett's face at odd times, lift blind or 
peer round curtains, as if he sought to put himself in possession of 
heaven knew what evidence, in case it should be require of him. 

the unfortunate occurrence made certain minor difference in oleron's 
domestic arrangements. barrett's tongue, he gathered, had already been 
busy; he was look at askance by the dwell of the square; and he 
judge it better, until he should be able to obtain other help, to make 
his purchase of provisions a little farther afield rather than at the 
small shop of the immediate neighbourhood. for the rest, housekeeping 
was no new thing to him, and he would resume his old bachelor habits.... 

besides, he was deep in certain rather abstruse investigations, in which 
it was better that he should not be disturbed. 

he was look out of his window one midday rather tired, not very well, 
and glad that it was not very likely he would have to stir out of doors,
