when he saw elsie bengough crossing the square towards his house. the 
weather had broken; it was a raw and gusty day; and she had to force 
her way against the wind that set her ample skirt belly about her 
opulent figure and her veil spin and stream behind her. 

oleron act swift and instinctively. seize his hat, he sprang to the 
door and descended the stairs at a run. a sort of panic had seize him. 
she must be prevent from setting foot in the place. as he ran along the 
alley he was conscious that his eyes went up to the eaves as if something 
drew them. he did not know that a slate might not accidental fall.... 

he met her at the gate, and spoke with curious volubleness. 

"this is really too bad, elsie! just as i'm urgent call away! i'm 
afraid it can't be help though, and that you'll have to think me an 
inhospitable beast." he pour it out just as it came into his head. 

she ask if he was go to town. 

"yes, yes--to town," he replied. "i've got to call on--on chambers. you 
know chambers, don't you? no, i remember you don't; a big man you once 
saw me with.... i ought to have gone yesterday, and--" this he felt to be 
a brilliant effort--"and he's go out of town this afternoon. to 
brighton. i had a letter from him this morning." 

he took her arm and led her up the square. she had to remind him that his 
way to town lay in the other direction. 

"of course--how stupid of me!" he said, with a little loud laugh. "i'm so 
use to go the other way with you--of course; it's the other way to 
the bus. will you come along with me? i am so awfully sorry it's happen 
like this...." 

they took the street to the bus terminus. 

this time elsie bore no sign of have gone through interior struggles. 
if she detect anything unusual in his manner she made no comment, and 
he, seeing her calm, began to talk less reckless through silences. by 
the time they reach the bus terminus, nobody, seeing the pallid-faced 
man without an overcoat and the large ample-skirted girl at his side, 
would have supposed that one of them was ready to sink on his knee for 
thankful that he had, as he believed, save the other from a wildly 
unthinkable danger. 

they mount to the top of the bus, oleron protest that he should not 
miss his overcoat, and that he found the day, if anything, rather 
oppressive hot. they sat down on a front seat. 

now that this meeting was forced upon him, he had something else to say 
that would make demand upon his tact. it had been on his mind for some 
time, and was, indeed, peculiarly difficult to put. he revolve it for 
some minutes, and then, remember the success of his story of a sudden 
call to town, cut the knot of his difficulty with another lie. 

"i'm thinking of go away for a little while, elsie," he said. 

she merely said, "oh?" 

"somewhere for a change. i need a change. i think i shall go to-morrow, 
or the day after. yes, to-morrow, i think." 

"yes," she replied. 

"i don't quite know how long i shall be," he continued. "i shall have to 
let you know when i am back." 

"yes, let me know," she reply in an even tone. 

the tone was, for her, suspicious even. he was a little uneasy. 

"you don't ask me where i'm going," he said, with a little cumbrous 
effort to rally her. 

she was look straight before her, past the bus-driver. 

"i know," she said. 

he was startled. "how, you know?" 

"you're not go anywhere," she replied. 

he found not a word to say. it was a minute or so before she continued, 
in the same control voice she had employ from the start. 

"you're not go anywhere. you weren't go out this morning. you only 
came out because i appeared; don't behave as if we were strangers, paul." 

a flush of pink had mount to his cheeks. he notice that the wind had 
given her the pink of early rhubarb. still he found nothing to say. 

"of course, you ought to go away," she continued. "i don't know whether 
you look at yourself often in the glass, but you're rather noticeable. 
several people have turn to look at you this morning. so, of course, 
you ought to go away. but you won't, and i know why." 

he shivered, cough a little, and then broke silence. 

"then if you know, there's no use in continue this discussion," he said 
curtly. 

"not for me, perhaps, but there is for you," she replied. "shall i tell 
you what i know?" 

"no," he said in a voice slightly raised. 

"no?" she asked, her round eyes earnest on him. 

"no." 

again he was get out of patience with her; again he was conscious of 
the strain. her devotion and fidelity and love plague him; she was only 
humiliate both herself and him. it would have been bad enough had he 
ever, by word or deed, given her cause for thus fastening herself on 
him ... but there; that was the worst of that kind of life for a woman. 
women such as she, business women, in and out of offices all the time, 
always, whether they realise it or not, made comradeship a cover for 
something else. they accept the unconventional status, came and 
went freely, as men did, were honestly taken by men at their own 
valuation--and then it turn out to be the other thing after all, and 
they went and fell in love. no wonder there was gossip in shop and 
square and public houses! in a sense the gossip were in the right of 
it. independent, yet not efficient; with some of womanhood's graces 
forgone, and yet with all the woman's hunger and need; half 
sophisticated, yet not wise; oleron was tired of it all.... 

and it was time he told her so. 

"i suppose," he said tremblingly, look down between his knees, "i 
suppose the real trouble is in the life women who earn their own living 
are oblige to lead." 

he could not tell in what sense she took the lame generality; she merely 
replied, "i suppose so." 

"it can't be helped," he continued, "but you do sacrifice a good deal." 

she agreed: a good deal; and then she add after a moment, "what, for 
instance?" 

"you may or may not be gradual attain a new status, but you're in a 
false position to-day." 

it was very likely, she said; she hadn't thought of it much in that 
light-- 

"and," he continue desperately, "you're bound to suffer. your most 
innocent acts are misunderstood; motive you never dream of are 
attribute to you; and in the end it come to--" he hesitate a moment 
and then took the plunge, "--to the sidelong look and the leer." 

she took his meaning with perfect ease. she merely shiver a little as 
she pronounced the name. 

"barrett?" 

his silence told her the rest. 

anything further that was to be said must come from her. it came as the 
bus stop at a stage and fresh passenger mount the stairs. 

"you'd better get down here and go back, paul," she said. "i understand 
perfectly--perfectly. it isn't barrett. you'd be able to deal with 
barrett. it's merely convenient for you to say it's barrett. i know what 
it is ... but you said i wasn't to tell you that. very well. but before 
you go let me tell you why i came up this morning." 

in a dull tone he ask her why. again she look straight before her as 
she replied: 

"i came to force your hand. things couldn't go on as they have been 
going, you know; and now that's all over." 

"all over," he repeated stupidly. 

"all over. i want you now to consider yourself, as far as i'm concerned, 
perfectly free. i make only one reservation." 

he hardly had the spirit to ask her what that was. 

"if _i_ merely need _you_," she said, "please don't give that a thought; 
that's nothing; i shan't come near for that. but," she drop her voice, 
"if _you're_ in need of _me_, paul--i shall know if you are, _and you 
will be_--then i shall come at no matter what cost. you understand that?" 

he could only groan. 

"so that's understood," she concluded. "and i think that's all. now go 
back. i should advise you to walk back, for you're shivering--good-bye--" 

she gave him a cold hand, and he descended. he turn on the edge of the 
kerb as the bus start again. for the first time in all the years he had 
known her she part from him with no smile and no wave of her long arm. 


ix 

he stood on the kerb plunge in misery, look after her as long as she 
remain in sight; but almost instantly with her disappearance he felt 
the heavy lift a little from his spirit. she had given him his 
liberty; true, there was a sense in which he had never part with it, 
but now was no time for splitting hairs; he was free to act, and all was 
clear ahead. swift the sense of lightness grew on him: it became a 
positive rejoicing in his liberty; and before he was halfway home he had 
decided what must be done next. 

the vicar of the parish in which his dwelling was situated live within 
ten minutes of the square. to his house oleron turn his steps. it was 
necessary that he should have all the information he could get about this 
old house with the insurance mark and the slope "to let" boards, and 
the vicar was the person most likely to be able to furnish it. this last 
preliminary out of the way, and--aha! oleron chuckled--things might be 
expect to happen! 

but he gain less information than he had hope for. the house, the 
vicar said, was old--but there need no vicar to tell oleron that; it 
was reputed (oleron prick up his ears) to be haunted--but there were 
few old house about which some such rumour did not circulate among the 
ignorant; and the deplorable lack of faith of the modern world, the vicar 
thought, did not tend to dissipate these superstitions. for the rest, 
his manner was the soothe manner of one who prefer not to make 
statement without knowing how they will be taken by his hearer. oleron 
smile as he perceive this. 

"you may leave my nerves out of the question," he said. "how long has the 
place been empty?" 

"a dozen years, i should say," the vicar replied. 

"and the last tenant--did you know him--or her?" oleron was conscious of 
a tingle of his nerves as he offer the vicar the alternative of sex. 

"him," said the vicar. "a man. if i remember rightly, his name was 
madley; an artist. he was a great recluse; seldom went out of the place, 
and--" the vicar hesitate and then broke into a little gush of candour 
"--and since you appear to have come for this information, and since it 
is better that the truth should be told than that garble version should 
get about, i don't mind saying that this man madley die there, under 
somewhat unusual circumstances. it was ascertain at the post-mortem 
that there was not a particle of food in his stomach, although he was 
found to be not without money. and his frame was simply worn out. suicide 
was spoken of, but you'll agree with me that deliberate starvation is, to 
say the least, an uncommon form of suicide. an open verdict was 
returned." 

"ah!" said oleron.... "does there happen to be any comprehensive history 
of this parish?" 

"no; partial one only. i myself am not guiltless of have made a number 
of note on its purely ecclesiastical history, its register and so 
forth, which i shall be happy to show you if you would care to see them; 
but it is a large parish, i have only one curate, and my leisure, as you 
will readily understand ..." 

the extent of the parish and the scanty of the vicar's leisure 
occupy the remainder of the interview, and oleron thank the vicar, 
took his leave, and walk slow home. 

he walk slow for a reason, twice turning away from the house within a 
stone's-throw of the gate and taking another turn of twenty minutes or 
so. he had a very ticklish piece of work now before him; it require the 
greatest mental concentration; it was nothing less than to bring his 
mind, if he might, into such a state of unpreoccupation and receptive 
that he should see the place as he had seen it on that morning when, 
his removal accomplished, he had sat down to begin the sixteenth chapter 
of the first _romilly_. 

for, could he recapture that first impression, he now hope for far more 
from it. formerly, he had carry no end of mental lumber. before the 
influence of the place had been able to find him out at all, it had had 
the inertia of those dreary chapter to overcome. no result had shown. 
the process had been one of slow saturation, charging, filling up to a 
brim. but now he was light, unburdened, rid at last both of that 
_romilly_ and of her prototype. now for the new unknown, coy, jealous, 
bewitching, beckon fair!... 

at half-past two of the afternoon he put his key into the yale lock, 
entered, and closed the door behind him.... 

his fantastic attempt was instantly and astonishing successful. he 
could have shout with triumph as he enter the room; it was as if he 
had _escaped_ into it. once more, as in the days when his writing had had 
a daily fresh and wonder and promise for him, he was conscious of 
that new ease and mastery and exhilarate and release. the air of the 
place seem to hold more oxygen; as if his own specific gravity had 
changed, his very tread seem less ponderable. the flower in the bowls, 
the fair proportions of the meadowsweet-coloured panel and mouldings, 
the polish floor, and the lofty and faint star ceiling, fairly 
laugh their welcome. oleron actually laugh back, and spoke aloud. 

"oh, you're pretty, pretty!" he flatter it. 

then he lay down on his couch. 

he spent that afternoon as a convalescent who expect a dear visitor 
might have spent it--in a delicious vacancy, smile now and then as 
if in his sleep, and ever lift drowsy and contented eyes to his 
allure surroundings. he lay thus until dark came, and, with 
darkness, the nocturnal noise of the old house.... 

but if he wait for any specific happening, he wait in vain. 

he wait similarly in vain on the morrow, maintaining, though with less 
ease, that sensitised-plate-like condition of his mind. nothing occur 
to give it an impression. whatever it was which he so patient wooed, it 
seem to be both shy and exacting. 

then on the third day he thought he understood. a look of gentle drollery 
and cunning came into his eyes, and he chuckled. 

"oho, oho!... well, if the wind sit in _that_ quarter we must see what 
else there is to be done. what is there, now?... no, i won't send for 
elsie; we don't need a wheel to break the butterfly on; we won't go to 
those lengths, my butterfly...." 

he was standing musing, thumb his lean jaw, look aslant; sudden 
he cross to his hall, took down his hat, and went out. 

"my lady is coquettish, is she? well, we'll see what a little neglect 
will do," he chuckle as he went down the stairs. 

he sought a railway station, got into a train, and spent the rest of the 
day in the country. oh, yes: oleron thought _he_ was the man to deal with 
fair one who beckoned, and invited, and then took refuge in shy and 
hanging back! 

he did not return until after eleven that night. 

"_now_, my fair beckoner!" he murmur as he walk along the alley and 
felt in his pocket for his keys.... 

inside his flat, he was perfectly composed, perfectly deliberate, 
exceedingly careful not to give himself away. as if to intimate that he 
intended to retire immediately, he light only a single candle; and as 
he set out with it on his nightly round he affected to yawn. he went 
first into his kitchen. there was a full moon, and a lozenge of 
moonlight, almost peacock-blue by contrast with his candle-frame, lay on 
the floor. the window was uncurtained, and he could see the reflection of 
the candle, and, faintly, that of his own face, as he move about. the 
door of the powder-closet stood a little ajar, and he closed it before 
sitting down to remove his boots on the chair with the cushion made of 
the fold harp-bag. from the kitchen he pass to the bathroom. there, 
another slant of blue moonlight cut the windowsill and lay across the 
pipes on the wall. he visit his seldom-used study, and stood for a 
moment gaze at the silver roof across the square. then, walking 
straight through his sitting-room, his stocking feet making no noise, 
he enter his bedroom and put the candle on the chest of drawers. his 
face all this time wore no expression save that of tiredness. he had 
never been wily nor more alert. 

his small bedroom fireplace was opposite the chest of drawers on which 
the mirror stood, and his bed and the window occupy the remain 
side of the room. oleron drew down his blind, took off his coat, and 
then stoop to get his slipper from under the bed. 

he could have given no reason for the conviction, but that the 
manifestation that for two days had been withheld was close at hand he 
never for an instant doubted. nor, though he could not form the faintest 
guess of the shape it might take, did he experience fear. startle or 
surprising it might be; he was prepared for that; but that was all; his 
scale of sensation had become depressed. his hand move this way and that 
under the bed in search of his slippers.... 

but for all his caution and method and preparedness, his heart all at 
once gave a leap and a pause that was almost horrid. his hand had found 
the slippers, but he was still on his knees; save for this circumstance 
he would have fallen. the bed was a low one; the grope for the slipper 
account for the turn of his head to one side; and he was careful to 
keep the attitude until he had partly recover his self-possession. when 
presently he rose there was a drop of blood on his lower lip where he had 
caught at it with his teeth, and his watch had jerk out of the pocket 
of his waistcoat and was dangle at the end of its short leather 
guard.... 

then, before the watch had cease its little oscillation, he was himself 
again. 

in the middle of his mantelpiece there stood a picture, a portrait of his 
grandmother; he placed himself before this picture, so that he could see 
in the glass of it the steady flame of the candle that burn behind him 
on the chest of drawers. he could see also in the picture-glass the 
little glancing of light from the bevel and facet of the object about 
the mirror and candle. but he could see more. these twinkling and 
reflection and re-reflections did not change their position; but there 
was one gleam that had motion. it was faint than the rest, and it move 
up and down through the air. it was the reflection of the candle on 
oleron's black vulcanite comb, and each of its downward movements was 
accompany by a silky and crackling rustle. 

oleron, watch what went on in the glass of his grandmother's portrait, 
continue to play his part. he felt for his dangle watch and began 
slow to wind it up. then, for a moment cease to watch, he began to 
empty his trousers pocket and to place methodical in a little row on 
the mantelpiece the penny and halfpenny he took from them. the 
sweeping, minutely electric noise fill the whole bedroom, and had 
oleron alter his point of observation he could have brought the dim 
gleam of the moving comb so into position that it would almost have 
outline his grandmother's head. 

any other head of which it might have been following the outline was 
invisible. 

oleron finished the empty of his pockets; then, under cover of another 
simulated yawn, not so much summon his resolution as overmaster by 
an exhorbitant curiosity, he swung sudden round. that which was being 
comb was still not to be seen, but the comb did not stop. it had 
alter its angle a little, and had move a little to the left. it was 
passing, in fairly regular sweeps, from a point rather more than five 
feet from the ground, in a direction roughly vertical, to another point a 
few inch below the level of the chest of drawers. 

oleron continue to act to admiration. he walk to his little washstand 
in the corner, pour out water, and began to wash his hands. he remove 
his waistcoat, and continue his preparation for bed. the comb did 
not cease, and he stood for a moment in thought. again his eyes twinkled. 
the next was very cunning-- 

"hm!... _i think i'll read for a quarter of an hour_," he said aloud.... 

he pass out of the room. 

he was away a couple of minutes; when he return again the room was 
sudden quiet. he glance at the chest of drawers; the comb lay still, 
between the collar he had remove and a pair of gloves. without 
hesitation oleron put out his hand and picked it up. it was an ordinary 
eighteenpenny comb, taken from a card in a chemist's shop, of a substance 
of a definite specific gravity, and no more capable of rebellion against 
the law by which it exist than are the world that keep their orbit 
through the void. oleron put it down again; then he glance at the bundle 
of papers he held in his hand. what he had gone to fetch had been the 
fifteen chapter of the original _romilly_. 

"hm!" he mutter as he threw the manuscript into a chair.... "as i 
thought.... she's just blindly, ragingly, murderous jealous." 

* * * * * 

on the night after that, and on the following night, and for many nights 
and days, so many that he began to be uncertain about the count of them, 
oleron, courting, cajoling, neglecting, threatening, beseeching, eaten 
out with unappeased curiosity and regardless that his life was becoming 
one consume passion and desire, continue his search for the unknown 
co-numerator of his abode. 


x 

as time went on, it came to pass that few except the postman mount 
oleron's stairs; and since men who do not write letters receive few, even 
the postman's tread became so infrequent that it was not heard more than 
once or twice a week. there came a letter from oleron's publishers, 
ask when they might expect to receive the manuscript of his new book; 
he delay for some days to answer it, and finally forgot it. a second 
letter came, which also he fail to answer. he received no third. 

the weather grew bright and warm. the privet bush among the 
chopper-like notice-boards flowered, and in the street where oleron did 
his shop the basket of flower-women line the kerbs. oleron 
purchase flower daily; his room clamour for flowers, fresh and 
continual renewed; and oleron did not stint its demands. nevertheless, 
the necessity for go out to buy them began to irk him more and more, 
and it was with a greater and ever greater sense of relief that he 
return home again. he began to be conscious that again his scale of 
sensation had suffer a subtle change--a change that was not restoration 
to its former capacity, but an extension and enlarge that once more 
included terror. it admitted it in an entire new form. _lux orco, 
tenebrae jovi_. the name of this terror was agoraphobia. oleron had begun 
to dread air and space and the horror that might pounce upon the 
unguarded back. 

presently he so contrived it that his food and flower were deliver 
daily at his door. he rub his hand when he had hit upon this 
expedient. that was better! now he could please himself whether he went 
out or not.... 

quick he was confirmed in his choice. it became his pleasure to remain 
immured. 

but he was not happy--or, if he was, his happiness took an extraordinary 
turn. he fret discontentedly, could sometimes have wept for mere 
weakness and misery; and yet he was dim conscious that he would not 
have exchange his sad for all the noisy mirth of the world outside. 
and speak of noise: noise, much noise, now cause him the acutest 
discomfort. it was hardly more to be endure than that new-born fear that 
kept him, on the increasingly rare occasion when he did go out, sidle 
close to wall and feeling friendly railing with his hand. he move from 
room to room soft and in slippers, and sometimes stood for many seconds 
close a door so gently that not a sound broke the still that was in 
itself a delight. sunday now became an intolerable day to him, for, since 
the coming of the fine weather, there had begun to assemble in the square 
under his window each sunday morning certain member of the sect to 
which the long-nosed barrett adhered. these came with a great drum and 
large brass-bellied instruments; men and women uplift anguished voices, 
struggle with their god; and barrett himself, with upraise face and 
closed eyes and working brows, pray that the sound of his voice might 
penetrate the ear of all unbelievers--as it certainly did oleron's. one 
day, in the middle of one of these rhapsodies, oleron sprang to his blind 
and pull it down, and heard as he did so his own name made the subject 
of a fresh torrent of outpouring. 

and sometimes, but not as expect a reply, oleron stood still and 
call softly. once or twice he call "romilly!" and then waited; but 
more often his whisper did not take the shape of a name. 

there was one spot in particular of his abode that he began to haunt with 
increase persistency. this was just within the opening of his bedroom 
door. he had discover one day that by opening every door in his place 
(always excepting the outer one, which he only open unwillingly) and by 
place himself on this particular spot, he could actually see to a 
greater or less extent into each of his five rooms without change his 
position. he could see the whole of his sitting-room, all of his bedroom 
except the part hidden by the open door, and glimpse of his kitchen, 
bathroom, and of his rarely use study. he was often in this place, 
breathless and with his finger on his lip. one day, as he stood there, he 
sudden found himself wonder whether this madley, of whom the vicar 
had spoken, had ever discover the strategic importance of the bedroom 
entry. 

light, moreover, now cause him greater disquietude than did darkness. 
direct sunlight, of which, as the sun pass daily round the house, each 
of his rooms had now its share, was like a flame in his brain; and even 
diffuse light was a dull and numb ache. he began, at successive hour 
of the day, one after another, to lower his crimson blinds. he made short 
and daring excursion in order to do this; but he was ever careful to 
leave his retreat open, in case he should have sudden need of it. 
presently this lower of the blind had become a daily methodical 
exercise, and his rooms, when he had been his round, had the blood-red 
half-light of a photographer's darkroom. 

one day, as he drew down the blind of his little study and back in good 
order out of the room again, he broke into a soft laugh. 

"_that_ bilk mr. barrett!" he said; and the baffling of barrett 
continue to afford him mirth for an hour. 

but on another day, soon after, he had a fright that left him tremble 
also for an hour. he had seize the cord to darken the window over the 
seat in which he had found the harp-bag, and was standing with his back 
well protect in the embrasure, when he thought he saw the tail of a 
black-and-white check skirt disappear round the corner of the house. he 
could not be sure--had he run to the window of the other wall, which was 
blinded, the skirt must have been already past--but he was _almost_ sure 
that it was elsie. he listen in an agony of suspense for her tread on 
the stairs.... 

but no tread came, and after three or four minutes he drew a long breath 
of relief. 

"by jove, but that would have compromise me horribly!" he muttered.... 

and he continue to mutter from time to time, "horribly 
compromise ... _no_ woman would stand that ... not _any_ kind of 
woman ... oh, compromise in the extreme!" 

yet he was not happy. he could not have assign the cause of the fit of 
quiet weeping which took him sometimes; they came and went, like the 
fitful illumination of the cloud that travelled over the square; and 
perhaps, after all, if he was not happy, he was not unhappy. before 
he could be unhappy something must have been withdrawn, and nothing had 
yet been withdrawn from him, for nothing had been granted. he was wait 
for that granting, in that flower-laden, frightfully entice apartment 
of his, with the pith-white wall tinge and subdued by the crimson 
blind to a blood-like gloom. 

he paid no heed to it that his stock of money was running perilous low, 
nor that he had cease to work. cease to work? he had not cease to 
work. they knew very little about it who supposed that oleron had cease 
to work! he was in truth only now beginning to work. he was prepare 
such a work ... such a work ... such a mistress was a-making in the 
gestation of his art ... let him but get this period of probation and 
poignant wait over and men should see.... how _should_ men know her, 
this fair one of oleron's, until oleron himself knew her? lovely radiant 
creation are not thrown off like how-d'ye-do's. the men to whom it is 
committed to father them must weep wretched tears, as oleron did, must 
swell with vain presumptuous hopes, as oleron did, must pursue, as oleron 
pursued, the capricious, fair, mocking, slippery, eager spirit that, ever 
eluding, ever see to it that the chase do not slacken. let oleron but 
hunt this huntress a little longer... he would have her sparkle 
and pant in his arms yet.... oh no: they were very far from the truth 
who supposed that oleron had cease to work! 

and if all else was fall away from oleron, gladly he was letting it 
go. so do we all when our fair one beckon. quite at the beginning we 
wink, and promise ourselves that we will put her ladyship through her 
paces, neglect her for a day, turn her own jealous wiles against her, 
flout and ignore her when she come wheedling; perhaps there lurk within 
us all the time a heartless sprite who is never fooled; but in the end 
all falls away. she beckons, beckons, and all goes.... 

and so oleron kept his strategic post within the frame of his bedroom 
door, and watched, and waited, and smiled, with his finger on his 
lips.... it was his duteous service, his worship, his troth-plighting, 
all that he had ever known of love. and when he found himself, as he now 
and then did, hate the dead man madley, and wish that he had never 
lived, he felt that that, too, was an acceptable service.... 

but, as he thus prepared himself, as it were, for a marriage, and moped 
and chafe more and more that the bride made no sign, he made a discovery 
that he ought to have made week before. 

it was through a thought of the dead madley that he made it. since that 
night when he had thought in his green that a little studied neglect 
would bring the lovely beckon to her knees, and had made use of her own 
jealousy to banish her, he had not set eyes on those fifteen discard 
chapter of _romilly_. he had thrown them back into the window-seat, 
forgotten their very existence. but his own jealousy of madley put him in 
mind of hers of her jilt rival of flesh and blood, and he remember 
them.... fool that he had been! had he, then, expect his desire to 
manifest herself while there still exist the evidence of his divide 
allegiance? what, and she with a passion so fierce and centre that it 
had not hesitate at the destruction, twice attempted, of her rival? fool 
that he had been!... 

but if _that_ was all the pledge and sacrifice she require she should 
have it--ah, yes, and quickly! 

he took the manuscript from the window-seat, and brought it to the fire. 

he kept his fire always burning now; the warmth brought out the last 
vestige of odour of the flower with which his room was banked. he did 
not know what time it was; long since he had allow his clock to run 
down--it had seem a foolish measure of time in regard to the 
stupendous things that were happening to oleron; but he knew it was late. 
he took the _romilly_ manuscript and knelt before the fire. 

but he had not finished remove the fastening that held the sheet 
together before he sudden gave a start, turn his head over his 
shoulder, and listen intently. the sound he had heard had not been 
loud--it had been, indeed, no more than a tap, twice or thrice 
repeated--but it had fill oleron with alarm. his face grew dark as 
it came again. 

he heard a voice outside on his landing. 

"paul!... paul!..." 

it was elsie's voice. 

"paul!... i know you're in... i want to see you...." 

he cursed her under his breath, but kept perfectly still. he did not 
intend to admit her. 

"paul!... you're in trouble.... i believe you're in danger... at least 
come to the door!..." 

oleron smother a low laugh. it somehow amuse him that she, in such 
danger herself, should talk to him of _his_ danger!... well, if she was, 
serve her right; she knew, or said she knew, all about it.... 

"paul!... paul!..." 

"_paul!... paul!_..." he mimicke her under his breath. 

"oh, paul, it's _horrible_!..." 

horrible, was it? thought oleron. then let her get away.... 

"i only want to help you, paul.... i didn't promise not to come if you 
need me...." 

he was impervious to the pitiful sob that interrupt the low cry. the 
devil take the woman! should he shout to her to go away and not come 
back? no: let her call and knock and sob. she had a gift for sobbing; she 
mustn't think her sob would move him. they irritate him, so that he set 
his teeth and shook his fist at her, but that was all. let her sob. 

"_paul!... paul!_..." 

with his teeth hard set, he drop the first page of _romilly_ into the 
fire. then he began to drop the rest in, sheet by sheet. 

for many minutes the calling behind his door continued; then sudden it 
ceased. he heard the sound of feet slow descend the stairs. he 
listen for the noise of a fall or a cry or the crash of a piece of the 
handrail of the upper landing; but none of these things came. she was 
spared. apparently her rival suffer her to crawl abject and beaten 
away. oleron heard the passing of her steps under his window; then she 
was gone. 

he drop the last page into the fire, and then, with a low laugh rose. 
he look fondly round his room. 

"lucky to get away like that," he remarked. "she wouldn't have got away 
if i'd given her as much as a word or a look! what devil these women 
are!... but no; i oughtn't to say that; one of 'em show 
forbearance...." 

who show forbearance? and what was forborne? ah, oleron 
knew!... contempt, no doubt, had been at the bottom of it, but that 
didn't matter: the pester creature had been allow to go unharmed. 
yes, she was lucky; oleron hope she knew it.... 

and now, now, now for his reward! 

oleron cross the room. all his door were open; his eyes shone as he 
placed himself within that of his bedroom. 

fool that he had been, not to think of destroy the manuscript 
sooner!... 

* * * * * 

how, in a houseful of shadows, should he know his own shadow? how, in a 
houseful of noises, distinguish the summons he felt to be at hand? ah, 
trust him! he would know! the place was full of a jugglery of dim lights. 
the blind at his elbow that allow the light of a street lamp to 
struggle vague through--the glimpse of greeny blue moonlight seen 
through the distant kitchen door--the sulky glow of the fire under the 
black ashes of the burnt manuscript--the glimmer of the tulip and the 
moon-daisies and narcissi in the bowls and jug and jars--these did not 
so trick and bewilder his eyes that he would not know his own! it was he, 
not she, who had been delay the shadowy bridal; he hung his head for a 
moment in mute acknowledgment; then he bent his eyes on the deceiving, 
puzzle gloom again. he would have call her name had he known it--but 
now he would not ask her to share even a name with the other.... 

his own face, within the frame of the door, glimmer white as the 
narcissi in the darkness.... 

a shadow, light as fleece, seem to take shape in the kitchen (the time 
had been when oleron would have said that a cloud had pass over the 
unseen moon). the low illumination on the blind at his elbow grew dim 
(the time had been when oleron would have conclude that the lamplighter 
go his rounds had turn low the flame of the lamp). the fire settled, 
letting down the black and char papers; a flower fell from a bowl, 
and lay indistinct upon the floor; all was still; and then a stray 
draught move through the old house, passing before oleron's face.... 

suddenly, incline his head, he withdrew a little from the door-jamb. 
the wandering draught cause the door to move a little on its hinges. 
oleron tremble violently, stood for a moment longer, and then, putt 
his hand out to the knob, soft drew the door to, sat down on the 
nearest chair, and waited, as a man might await the calling of his name 
that should summon him to some weighty, high and privy audience.... 


xi 

one know not whether there can be human compassion for anemia of the 
soul. when the pitch of life is dropped, and the spirit is so put over 
and reverse that that only is horrible which before was sweet and 
worldly and of the day, the human relation disappears. the sane soul 
turn appall away, lest not merely itself, but sanity should suffer. 
we are not gods. we cannot drive out devils. we must see selfish 
to it that devil do not enter into ourselves. 

and this we must do even though love so transfuse us that we may well 
deem our nature to be half divine. we shall but speak of honour and duty 
in vain. the letter drop within the dark door will lie unregarded, or, 
if regard for a brief instant between two unspeakable lapses, left and 
forgotten again. the telegram will be undelivered, nor will the whistle 
messenger (wiselier guide than he know to whistle) be conscious as he 
walk away of the drawn blind that is pushed aside an inch by a finger 
and then fearful replace again. no: let the miserable wrestle with his 
own shadows; let him, if indeed he be so mad, clip and strain and enfold 
and couch the succubus; but let him do so in a house into which not an 
air of heaven penetrates, nor a bright finger of the sun pierce the 
filthy twilight. the lost must remain lost. humanity has other business 
to attend to. 

for the handwriting of the two letters that oleron, steal noiseless 
one june day into his kitchen to rid his sitting-room of an armful of 
fetid and decay flowers, had seen on the floor within his door, had 
had no more meaning for him than if it had belong to some dim and 
faraway dream. and at the beating of the telegraph-boy upon the door, 
within a few feet of the bed where he lay, he had gnash his teeth and 
stop his ears. he had picture the lad standing there, just beyond his 
partition, among packet of provisions and bundle of dead and die 
flowers. for his outer landing was litter with these. oleron had fear 
to open his door to take them in. after a week, the errand lad had 
report that there must be some mistake about the order, and had left no 
more. inside, in the red twilight, the old flower turn brown and fell 
and decay where they lay. 

gradual his power was drain away. the abomination fasten on 
oleron's power. the steady sap sometimes left him for many hour 
of prostration gaze vacant up at his red-tinged ceiling, idle 
suffering such fancies as came of themselves to have their way with him. 
even the strongest of his memory had no more than a precarious hold 
upon his attention. sometimes a flit half-memory, of a novel to be 
written, a novel it was important that he should write, tantalise him 
for a space before vanish again; and sometimes whole novels, perfect, 
splendid, establish to endure, rose magical before him. and sometimes 
the memory were absurd remote and trivial, of garret he had 
inhabit and lodgings that had sheltered him, and so forth. oleron had 
known a good deal about such things in his time, but all that was now 
past. he had at last found a place which he did not intend to leave until 
they fetch him out--a place that some might have thought a little on 
the green-sick side, that other might have considered to be a little too 
redolent of long-dead and morbid things for a living man to be mew up 
in, but ah, so irresistible, with such an authority of its own, with such 
an associate of its own, and a place of such delight when once a man had 
cease to struggle against its inexorable will! a novel? somebody ought 
to write a novel about a place like that! there must be lot to write 
about in a place like that if one could but get to the bottom of it! it 
had probably already been painted, by a man call madley who had live 
there ... but oleron had not known this madley--had a strong feeling 
that he wouldn't have like him--would rather he had live somewhere 
else--really couldn't stand the fellow--hated him, madley, in fact. (aha! 
that was a joke!). he seriously doubt whether the man had led the life 
he ought; oleron was in two mind sometimes whether he wouldn't tell that 
long-nosed guardian of the public morals across the way about him; but 
probably he knew, and had made his pray hullabaloo for him also. 
that was his line. why, oleron himself had had a dust-up with him about 
something or other ... some girl or other ... elsie bengough her name 
was, he remembered.... 

oleron had moments of deep uneasy about this elsie bengough. or 
rather, he was not so much uneasy about her as restless about the things 
she did. chief of these was the way in which she persist in thrust 
herself into his thoughts; and, whenever he was quick enough, he sent her 
packing the moment she made her appearance there. the truth was that she 
was not merely a bore; she had always been that; it had now come to the 
pitch when her very presence in his fancy was inimical to the full 
enjoyment of certain experiences.... she had no tact; really ought to 
have known that people are not at home to the thought of everybody all 
the time; ought in mere polite to have allow him certain season 
quite to himself; and was monstrous ignorant of things if she did not 
know, as she appear not to know, that there were certain special hour 
when a man's vein ran with fire and daring and power, in which ... well, 
in which he had a reasonable right to treat folk as he had treat that 
pry barrett--to shut them out completely.... but no: up she popped, 
the thought of her, and ruin all. bright towering fabrics, by the side 
of which even those perfect, magical novel of which he dream were dun 
and grey, vanish utterly at her intrusion. it was as if a fog should 
sudden quench some fair-beaming star, as if at the threshold of some 
golden portal prepared for oleron a pit should sudden gape, as if a 
bat-like shadow should turn the grow dawn to mirk and dark 
again.... therefore, oleron strove to stifle even the nascent thought 
of her. 

nevertheless, there came an occasion on which this woman bengough 
absolutely refuse to be suppressed. oleron could not have told exactly 
when this happened; he only knew by the glimmer of the street lamp on his 
blind that it was some time during the night, and that for some time she 
had not present herself. 

he had no warning, none, of her coming; she just came--was there. strive 
as he would, he could not shake off the thought of her nor the image of 
her face. she haunt him. 

but for her to come at that moment of all moments!... really, it was past 
belief! how she could endure it, oleron could not conceive! actually, to 
look on, as it were, at the triumph of a rival.... good god! it was 
monstrous! tact--reticence--he had never credit her with an 
overwhelming amount of either: but he had never attribute mere--oh, 
there was no word for it! monstrous--monstrous! did she intend 
thenceforward.... good god! to look on!... 

oleron felt the blood rush up to the roots of his hair with anger against 
her. 

"damnation take her!" he choked.... 

but the next moment his heat and resentment had change to a cold sweat 
of cower fear. panic-stricken, he strove to comprehend what he had 
done. for though he knew not what, he knew he had done something, 
something fatal, irreparable, blasting. anger he had felt, but not _this_ 
blaze of ire that sudden flood the twilight of his consciousness with 
a white infernal light. _that_ appalling flash was not his--not his 
_that_ open rift of bright and searing hell--not his, not his! his had 
been the hand of a child, prepare a puny blow; but what was _this 
other_ horrific hand that was drawn back to strike in the same place? had 
_he_ set that in motion? had _he_ provided the spark that had touched off 
the whole accumulate power of that formidable and relentless place? he 
did not know. he only knew that that poor ignite particle in himself 
was blown out, that--oh, impossible!--a clinging kiss (how else to 
express it?) had change on his very lip to a gnash and a removal, 
and that for very pity of the awful odds he must cry out to her against 
whom he had lately rage to guard herself ... guard herself.... 

"_look out!_" he shriek aloud.... 

* * * * * 

the revulsion was instant. as if a cold slow billow had broken over him, 
he came to to find that he was lie in his bed, that the mist and horror 
that had for so long enwrapp him had departed, that he was paul oleron, 
and that he was sick, naked, helpless, and unutterable abandoned and 
alone. his faculties, though weak, answer at last to his call upon 
them; and he knew that it must have been a hideous nightmare that had 
left him sweat and shake thus. 

yes, he was himself, paul oleron, a tired novelist, already past the 
summit of his best work, and slip downhill again empty-handed from it 
all. he had struck short in his life's aim. he had try too much, had 
over-estimated his strength, and was a failure, a failure.... 

it all came to him in the single word, enwrapp and complete; it need 
no sequential thought; he was a failure. he had missed.... 

and he had miss not one happiness, but two. he had miss the ease of 
this world, which men love, and he had miss also that other shining 
prize for which men forgo ease, the snatch and holding and triumphant 
bearing up aloft of which is the only justification of the mad adventurer 
who hazard the enterprise. and there was no second attempt. fate has no 
morrow. oleron's morrow must be to sit down to profitless, ill-done, 
unrequired work again, and so on the morrow after that, and the morrow 
after that, and as many morrow as there might be.... 

he lay there, weak yet sane considering it.... 

and since the whole attempt had failed, it was hardly worth while to 
consider whether a little might not be save from the general wreck. no 
good would ever come of that half-finished novel. he had intended that it 
should appear in the autumn; was under contract that it should appear; no 
matter; it was better to pay forfeit to his publisher than to waste what 
days were left. he was spent; age was not far off; and path of wisdom 
and sad were the properest for the remainder of the journey.... 

if only he had chosen the wife, the child the faithful friend at the 
fireside, and let them follow an _ignis fatuus_ that list!... 

in the meantime it began to puzzle him exceedingly what he should be so 
weak, that his room should smell so overpowering of decay vegetable 
matter, and that his hand, chance to stray to his face in the darkness, 
should encounter a beard. 

"most extraordinary!" he began to mutter to himself. "have i been ill? am 
i ill now? and if so, why have they left me alone?... extraordinary!..." 

he thought he heard a sound from the kitchen or bathroom. he rose a 
little on his pillow, and listened.... ah! he was not alone, then! it 
certainly would have been extraordinary if they had left him ill and 
alone--alone? oh no. he would be look after. he wouldn't be left, ill, 
to shift for himself. if everybody else had forsaken him, he could trust 
elsie bengough, the dearest chum he had, for that ... bless her faithful 
heart! 

but sudden a short, stifled, splutter cry rang sharp out: 

"_paul!_" 

it came from the kitchen. 

and in the same moment it flash upon oleron, he knew not how, that two, 
three, five, he knew not how many minutes before, another sound, unmarked 
at the time but sudden transfix his attention now, had striven to 
reach his intelligence. this sound had been the slight touch of metal on 
metal--just such a sound as oleron made when he put his key into the 
lock. 

"hallo!... who's that?" he call sharp from his bed. 

he had no answer. 

he call again. "hallo!... who's there?... who is it?" 

this time he was sure he heard noises, soft and heavy, in the kitchen. 

"this is a queer thing altogether," he muttered. "by jove, i'm as weak as 
a kitten too.... hallo, there! somebody called, didn't they?... elsie! is 
that you?..." 

then he began to knock with his hand on the wall at the side of his bed. 

"elsie!... elsie!... you called, didn't you?... please come here, whoever 
it is!..." 

there was a sound as of a close door, and then silence. oleron began to 
get rather alarmed. 

"it may be a nurse," he muttered; "elsie'd have to get me a nurse, of 
course. she'd sit with me as long as she could spare the time, brave 
lass, and she'd get a nurse for the rest.... but it was awfully like her 
voice.... elsie, or whoever it is!... i can't make this out at all. i 
must go and see what's the matter...." 

he put one leg out of bed. feeling its feebleness, he reach with his 
hand for the additional support of the wall.... 

* * * * * 

but before putt out the other leg he stop and considered, pick 
at his new-found beard. he was sudden wonder whether he _dared_ go 
into the kitchen. it was such a frightfully long way; no man knew what 
horror might not leap and huddle on his shoulder if he went so far; 
when a man has an overmaster impulse to get back into bed he ought to 
take heed of the warning and obey it. besides, why should he go? what 
was there to go for? if it was that bengough creature again, let her look 
after herself; oleron was not go to have things cramp themselves on 
his defenceless back for the sake of such a spoilsport as _she_!... if 
she was in, let her let herself out again, and the soon the better for 
her! oleron simply couldn't be bothered. he had his work to do. on the 
morrow, he must set about the writing of a novel with a heroine so 
winsome, capricious, adorable, jealous, wicked, beautiful, inflaming, and 
altogether evil, that men should stand amazed. she was coming over him 
now; he knew by the alteration of the very air of the room when she was 
near him; and that soft thrill of bliss that had begun to stir in him 
never came unless she was beckoning, beckoning.... 

he let go the wall and fell back into bed again as--oh, unthinkable!--the 
other half of that kiss that a gnash had interrupt was placed (how else 
convey it?) on his lips, rob him of very breath.... 


xii 

in the bright june sunlight a crowd fill the square, and look up at 
the window of the old house with the antique insurance mark in its 
wall of red brick and the agents' notice-boards hanging like wooden 
choppers over the paling. two constable stood at the broken gate of the 
narrow entrance-alley, keeping folk back. the women kept to the outskirts 
of the throng, moving now and then as if to see the drawn red blind of 
the old house from a new angle, and talk in whispers. the children 
were in the houses, behind closed doors. 

a long-nosed man had a little group about him, and he was telling some 
story over and over again; and another man, little and fat and wide-eyed, 
sought to capture the long-nosed man's audience with some relation in 
which a key figured. 

"... and it was reveal to me that there'd been something that very 
afternoon," the long-nosed man was saying. "i was standing there, where 
constable saunder is--or rather, i was passing about my business, when 
they came out. there was no deceive me, oh, no deceive _me! i_ saw 
her face...." 

"what was it like, mr. barrett?" a man asked. 

"it was like hers whom our lord said to, 'woman, doth any man accuse 
thee?'--white as paper, and no mistake! don't tell _me_!... and so i 
walk straight across to mrs. barrett, and 'jane,' i says, 'this must 
stop, and stop at once; we are command to avoid evil,' i says, 'and it 
must come to an end now; let him get help elsewhere.' 

"and she say to me, 'john,' she says, 'it's four-and-sixpence a 
week'--them was her words. 

"'jane,' i says, 'if it was forty-six thousand pound it should 
stop'... and from that day to this she hasn't set foot inside that gate." 

there was a short silence: then, 

"did mrs. barrett ever..._ see_ anythink, like?" somebody vague 
inquired. 

barrett turn austere on the speaker. 

"what mrs. barrett saw and mrs. barrett didn't see shall not pass these 
lips; even as it is written, keep thy tongue from speak evil," he 
said. 

another man spoke. 

"he was pretty near canned up in the _waggon and horses_ that night, 
weren't he, jim?" 

"yes, 'e 'adn't 'alf cop it...." 

"not standing treat much, neither; he was in the bar, all on his own...." 

"so 'e was; we talk about it...." 

the fat, scared-eyed man made another attempt. 

"she got the key off of me--she 'ad the number of it--she come into my 
shop of a tuesday evening...." 

nobody heed him. 

"shut your heads," a heavy labourer comment gruffly, "she hasn't been 
found yet. 'ere's the inspectors; we shall know more in a bit." 

two inspector had come up and were talk to the constable who guarded 
the gate. the little fat man ran eager forward, saying that she had 
bought the key of him. "i remember the number, because of it's being 
three one's and three three's--111333!" he exclaim excitedly. 

an inspector put him aside. 

"nobody's been in?" he ask of one of the constables. 

"no, sir." 

"then you, brackley, come with us; you, smith, keep the gate. there's a 
squad on its way." 

the two inspector and the constable pass down the alley and enter 
the house. they mount the wide carve staircase. 

"this don't look as if he'd been out much lately," one of the inspector 
mutter as he kick aside a litter of dead leaves and paper that lay 
outside oleron's door. "i don't think we need knock--break a pane, 
brackley." 

the door had two glaze panels; there was a sound of shatter glass; and 
brackley put his hand through the hole his elbow had made and drew back 
the latch. 

"faugh!"... choke one of the inspector as they entered. "let some light 
and air in, quick. it stink like a hearse--" 

the assembly out in the square saw the red blind go up and the window 
of the old house flung open. 

"that's better," said one of the inspectors, putt his head out of a 
window and drawing a deep breath.... "that seem to be the bedroom in 
there; will you go in, simms, while i go over the rest?..." 

they had drawn up the bedroom blind also, and the waxy-white, emaciate 
man on the bed had made a blink of his hand against the torture 
flood of brightness. nor could he believe that his hearing was not 
play trick with him, for there were two policemen in his room, 
bend over him and ask where "she" was. he shook his head. 

"this woman bengough... go by the name of miss elsie bengough... d'ye 
hear? where is she?... no good, brackley; get him up; be careful with 
him; i'll just shove _my_ head out of the window, i think...." 

the other inspector had been through oleron's study and had found 
nothing, and was now in the kitchen, kick aside an ankle-deep mass of 
vegetable refuse that cumber the floor. the kitchen window had no 
blind, and was over-shadowed by the blank end of the house across the 
alley. the kitchen appear to be empty. 

but the inspector, kick aside the dead flowers, notice that a 
shuffle track that was not of his making had been swept to a cupboard 
in the corner. in the upper part of the door of the cupboard was a square 
panel that look as if it slid on runners. the door itself was closed. 

the inspector advanced, put out his hand to the little knob, and slid the 
hatch along its groove. 

then he took an involuntary step back again. 

frame in the aperture, and fall forward a little before it jam 
again in its frame, was something that resemble a large lumpy pudding, 
done up in a pudding-bag of fade browny red frieze. 

"ah!" said the inspector. 

to close the hatch again he would have had to thrust that pudding back 
with his hand; and somehow he did not quite like the idea of touching 
it. instead, he turn the handle of the cupboard itself. there was 
weight behind it, so much weight that, after opening the door three or 
four inch and peer inside, he had to put his shoulder to it in order 
to close it again. in close it he left stick out, a few inch from 
the floor, a triangle of black and white check skirt. 

he went into the small hall. 

"all right!" he called. 

they had got oleron into his clothes. he still use his hand as 
blinkers, and his brain was very confused. a number of things were 
happening that he couldn't understand. he couldn't understand the 
extraordinary mess of dead flower there seem to be everywhere; he 
couldn't understand why there should be police officer in his room; he 
couldn't understand why one of these should be sent for a four-wheeler 
and a stretcher; and he couldn't understand what heavy article they 
seem to be moving about in the kitchen--his kitchen.... 

"what's the matter?" he mutter sleepily.... 

then he heard a murmur in the square, and the stopping of a four-wheeler 
outside. a police officer was at his elbow again, and oleron wonder 
why, when he whisper something to him, he should run off a string of 
words--something about "used in evidence against you." they had lift 
him to his feet, and were assist him towards the door.... 

no, oleron couldn't understand it at all. 

they got him down the stairs and along the alley. oleron was aware of 
confuse angry shoutings; he gather that a number of people want to 
lynch somebody or other. then his attention became fixed on a little fat 
frightened-eyed man who appear to be making a statement that an officer 
was taking down in a notebook. 

"i'd seen her with him ... they was often together ... she came into my 
shop and said it was for him ... i thought it was all right ... 111333 
the number was," the man was saying. 

the people seem to be very angry; many police were keeping them back; 
but one of the inspector had a voice that oleron thought quite kind and 
friendly. he was telling somebody to get somebody else into the cab 
before something or other was brought out; and oleron notice that a 
four-wheeler was drawn up at the gate. it appear that it was himself 
who was to be put into it; and as they lift him up he saw that the 
inspector try to stand between him and something that stood behind the 
cab, but was not quick enough to prevent oleron seeing that this 
something was a hooded stretcher. the angry voice sound like a sea; 
something hard, like a stone, hit the back of the cab; and the inspector 
follow oleron in and stood with his back to the window near the side 
where the people were. the door they had put oleron in at remain open, 
apparently till the other inspector should come; and through the opening 
oleron had a glimpse of the hatchet-like "to let" boards among the 
privet-trees. one of them said that the key was at number six.... 

sudden the rage of voice was hushed. along the entrance-alley 
shuffle steps were heard, and the other inspector appear at the 
cab door. 

"right away," he said to the driver. 

he entered, fasten the door after him, and block up the second window 
with his back. between the two inspector oleron slept peacefully. the 
cab move down the square, the other vehicle went up the hill. the 
mortuary lay that way.

_"for, barring all pother, 
with this, or the other, 
still briton are lords of the main._" 


the chapter of admiral 


i 

as abel keel lay on the galleon's deck, held from rolling down it only 
by his own weight and the sun-blackened hand that lay outstretched upon 
the planks, his gaze wandered, but ever return to the bell that hung, 
jam with the dangerous heel-over of the vessel, in the small 
ornamental belfry immediately abaft the mainmast. the bell was of cast 
bronze, with half-obliterated boss upon it that had been the head of 
cherubs; but wind and salt spray had given it a thick incrustation of 
bright, beautiful, lichenous green. it was this colour that abel 
keeling's eyes liked. 

for wherever else on the galleon his eyes rest they found only 
whiteness--the white of extreme eld. there were slightly vary 
degree in her whiteness; here she was of a white that glisten like 
salt-granules, there of a greyish chalky white, and again her white 
had the yellowish cast of decay; but everywhere it was the mild, 
disquiet white of material out of which the life had departed. 
her cordage was bleach as old straw is bleached, and half her ropes 
kept their shape little more firm than the ash of a string keeps its 
shape after the fire has passed; her pallid timber were white and clean 
as bone found in sand; and even the wild frankincense with which (for 
lack of tar, at her last touching of land) she had been pitched, had 
dry to a pale hard gum that sparkle like quartz in her open seams. the 
sun was yet so pale a buckler of silver through the still white mists 
that not a cord or timber cast a shadow; and only abel keeling's face and 
hand were black, carke and cinder-black from exposure to his pitiless 
rays. 

the galleon was the _mary of the tower_, and she had a frightful list to 
starboard. so cant was she that her mainyard dip one of its steel 
sickle into the glassy water, and, had her foremast remained, or more 
than the broken stump of her bonaventure mizzen, she must have turn 
over completely. many days ago they had strip the mainyard of its 
course, and had pass the sail under the mary's bottom, in the hope that 
it would stop the leak. this it had partly done as long as the galleon 
had continue to glide one way; then, without coming about, she had begun 
to glide the other, the ropes had parted, and she had drag the sail 
after her, leave a broad tarnish on the silver sea. 

for it was broadside that the galleon glided, almost imperceptibly, ever 
suck down. she glide as if a loadstone drew her, and, at first, abel 
keel had thought it was a loadstone, pull at her iron, drawing her 
through the pearly mists that lay like face-cloths to the water and hid 
at a short distance the tarnish left by the sail. but later he had known 
that it was no loadstone drawing at her iron. the motion was due--must 
be due--to the absolute dead of the calm in that silent, sinister, 
three-miles-broad waterway. with the eye of his mind he saw that 
loadstone now as he lay against a gun-truck, all but topple down the 
deck. soon that would happen again which had happen for five days past. 
he would hear again the chatter of monkey and the scream of 
parrots, the mat of green and yellow weeds would creep in towards the 
mary over the quicksilver sea, once more the sheer wall of rock would 
rise, and the men would run.... 

but no; the men would not run this time to drop the fenders. there were 
no men left to do so, unless bligh was still alive. perhaps bligh was 
still alive. he had walk half-way down the quarter-deck steps a little 
before the sudden nightfall of the day before, had then fallen and lain 
for a minute (dead, abel keel had supposed, watch him from his 
place by the gun-truck), and had then got up again and totter forward 
to the forecastle, his tall figure sway and his long arms waving. abel 
keel had not seen him since. most likely, he had die in the 
forecastle during the night. if he had not been dead he would have come 
aft again for water.... 

at the remembrance of the water abel keel lift his head. the strand 
of lean muscle about his emaciate mouth worked, and he made a little 
pressure of his sun-blackened hand on the deck, as if to verify its 
steep and his own balance. the mainmast was some seven or eight yard 
away.... he put one stiff leg under him and began, seat as he was, to 
make shuffle movements down the slope. 

to the mainmast, near the belfry, was affix his contrivance for 
catching water. it consist of a collar of rope set lower at one side 
than at the other (but that had been before the mast had steeve so many 
degree away from the zenith), and tallow beneath. the mists linger 
later in that gully of a strait than they did on the open ocean, and the 
collar of rope serve as a collector for the dew that condense on the 
mast. the drops fell into a small earthen pipkin placed on the deck 
beneath it. 

abel keel reach the pipkin and look into it. it was nearly a third 
full of fresh water. good. if bligh, the mate, was dead, so much the more 
water for abel keeling, master of the _mary of the tower_. he dip two 
finger into the pipkin and put them into his mouth. this he did several 
times. he did not dare to raise the pipkin to his black and broken lip 
for dread of a remember agony, he could not have told how many days 
ago, when a devil had whisper to him, and he had gulp down the 
contents of the pipkin in the morning, and for the rest of the day had 
gone waterless.... again he moisten his finger and suck them; then 
he lay sprawl against the mast, idle watch the drops of water 
as they fell. 

it was odd how the drops formed. slow they collected at the edge of the 
tallow collar, tremble in their full for an instant, and fell, 
another beginning the process instantly. it amuse abel keel to watch 
them. why (he wondered) were all the drops the same size? what cause and 
compulsion did they obey that they never varied, and what frail tenuity 
held the little globule intact? it must be due to some cause.... he 
remember that the aromatic gum of the wild frankincense with which they 
had parcel the seam had hung on the bucket in great sluggish gouts, 
obedient to a different compulsion; oil was different again, and so were 
juice and balsams. only quicksilver (perhaps the heavy and motionless 
sea put him in mind of quicksilver) seem obedient to no law.... why was 
it so? 

bligh, of course, would have had his explanation: it was the hand of god. 
that suffice for bligh, who had gone forward the evening before, and 
whom abel keel now seem vague and as at a distance to remember as 
the deep-voiced fanatic who had sung his hymn as, man by man, he had 
committed the body of the ship's company to the deep. bligh was that 
sort of man; accept things without question; was content to take things 
as they were and be ready with the fender when the wall of rock rose out 
of the opalescent mists. bligh, too, like the waterdrops, had his law, 
that was his and nobody else's.... 

there float down from some rotten rope up aloft a flake of scurf, that 
settled in the pipkin. abel keel watch it dul as it settled 
towards the pipkin's rim. when presently he again dip his finger into 
the vessel the water ran into a little vortex, drawing the flake with it. 
the water settled again; and again the minute flake determined towards 
the rim and adhere there, as if the rim had power to draw it.... 

it was exactly so that the galleon was gliding towards the wall of rock, 
the yellow and green weeds, and the monkey and parrots. put out into 
mid-water again (while there had been men to put her out) she had glide 
to the other wall. one force drew the chip in the pipkin and the ship 
over the trance sea. it was the hand of god, said bligh.... 

abel keeling, his mind now note minute things and now cloud with 
torpor, did not at first hear a voice that was quaking lift up 
over by the forecastle--a voice that drew nearer, to an accompaniment of 
swirl water. 

_"o thou, that jona in the fish 
three days didst keep from pain, 
which was a figure of thy death 
and rising up again--"_ 

it was bligh, singing one of his hymns: 

_"o thou, that noah keptst from flood 
and abram, day by day, 
as he along through egypt pass 
didst guide him in the way--"_ 

the voice ceased, leave the pious period uncompleted. bligh was alive, 
at any rate.... abel keel resume his fitful musing. 

yes, that was the law of bligh's life, to call things the hand of god; 
but abel keeling's law was different; no better, no worse, only 
different. the hand of god, that drew chip and galleons, must work by 
some method; and abel keeling's eyes were dul on the pipkin again as if 
he sought the method there.... 

then conscious thought left him for a space, and when he resume it was 
without obvious connection. 

oars, of course, were the thing. with oars, men could laugh at calms. 
oars, that only pinnace and galliasse now used, had had their 
advantages. but oar (which was to say a method, for you could say if you 
like that the hand of god grasp the oar-loom, as the breath of god 
fill the sail)--oars were antiquated, belong to the past, and meant a 
throwing-over of all that was good and new and a return to fine lines, a 
battle-formation abreast to give effect to the shock of the ram, and a 
day or two at sea and then to port again for provisions. oar ... no. 
abel keel was one of the new men, the men who swore by the line-ahead, 
the broadside fire of sake and demi-cannon, and week and month 
without a landfall. perhaps one day the wit of such men as he would 
devise a craft, not oar-driven (because oar could not penetrate into the 
remote sea of the world)--not sail-driven (because men who trust to 
sail found themselves in an airless, three-mile strait, suspend 
motionless between cloud and water, ever gliding to a wall of rock)--but 
a ship ... a ship ... 

"_to noah and his son with him 
god spake, and thus said he: 
a covenant set i up with you 
and your posterity_--" 

it was bligh again, wandering somewhere in the waist. abel keeling's mind 
was once more a blank. then slowly, slowly, as the water drops collected 
on the collar of rope, his thought took shape again. 

a galliasse? no, not a galliasse. the galliasse made shift to be two 
things, and was neither. this ship, that the hand of man should one day 
make for the hand of god to manage, should be a ship that should take and 
conserve the force of the wind, take it and store it as she store her 
victuals; at rest when she wished, go ahead when she wished; turning 
the forces both of calm and storm against themselves. for, of course, her 
force must be wind--stored wind--a bag of the winds, as the children's 
tale had it--wind probably direct upon the water astern, driving it 
away and urge forward the ship, acting by reaction. she would have a 
wind-chamber, into which wind would be pump with pumps.... bligh would 
call that equally the hand of god, this driving-force of the ship of the 
future that abel keel dim foreshadow as he lay between the 
mainmast and the belfry, turning his eyes now and then from ashy white 
timber to the vivid green bronze-rust of the bell above him.... 

bligh's face, liver-coloured with the sun and ravage from inwards by the 
faith that consume him, appear at the head of the quarter-deck steps. 
his voice beat uncontrolled out. 

_"and in the earth here is no place 
of refuge to be found, 
nor in the deep and water-course 
that passeth under ground--"_ 


ii 

bligh's eyes were lidded, as if in contemplation of his inner ecstasy. 
his head was thrown back, and his brow work up and down tormentedly. 
his wide mouth remain open as his hymn was sudden interrupt on the 
long-drawn note. from somewhere in the shimmer mists the note was 
taken up, and there drum and rang and reverberate through the strait 
a windy, hoarse, and dismal bellow, alarm and sustained. a tremor rang 
through bligh. moving like a sightless man, he stumble forward from the 
head of the quarter-deck steps, and abel keel was aware of his gaunt 
figure behind him, tall for the steep of the deck. as that vast 
empty sound die away, bligh laugh in his mania. 

"lord, hath the grave's wide mouth a tongue to praise thee? lo, again--" 

again the cavernous sound possessed the air, loud and nearer. through 
it came another sound, a slow throb, throb--throb, throb--again the 
sound ceased. 

"even leviathan lifteth up his voice in praise!" bligh sobbed. 

abel keel did not raise his head. there had return to him the memory 
of that day when, before the morning mists had lift from the strait, he 
had empty the pipkin of the water that was the allowance until night 
should fall again. during that agony of thirst he had seen shape and 
heard sound with other than his mortal eyes and ears, and even in the 
moments that had alternate with his lightness, when he had known these 
to be hallucinations, they had come again. he had heard the bell on a 
sunday in his own kentish home, the calling of children at play, the 
unconcerned singing of men at their daily labour, and the laughter and 
gossip of the women as they had spread the linen on the hedge or 
distribute bread upon the platters. these voice had rung in his brain, 
interrupt now and then by the groan of bligh and of two other men who 
had been alive then. some of the voice he had heard had been silent on 
earth this many a long year, but abel keeling, thirst-tortured, had heard 
them, even as he was now hearing that vacant moan with the 
intermittent throb that fill the strait with alarm.... 

"praise him, praise him, praise him!" bligh was calling deliriously. 

then a bell seem to sound in abel keeling's ears, and, as if something 
in the mechanism of his brain had slipped, another picture rose in his 
fancy--the scene when the _mary of the tower_ had put out, to a bravery 
of swinging bell and shrill fife and valiant trumpets. she had not been 
a leper-white galleon then. the scroll-work on her prow had twinkle with 
gilding; her belfry and stern-galleries and elaborate lantern had 
flash in the sun with gold; and her fighting-tops and the war-pavesse 
about her waist had been gay with paint coat and scutcheons. to her 
sail had been stitch gaudy ramp lion of scarlet saye, and from her 
mainyard, now dip in the water, had hung the broad two-tailed pennant 
with the virgin and child embroider upon it.... 

then sudden a voice about him seem to be saying, "_and a 
half-seven--and a half-seven--_" and in a twink the picture in abel 
keeling's brain change again. he was at home again, instruct his son, 
young abel, in the casting of the lead from the skiff they had pull out 
of the harbour. 

"_and a half-seven!_" the boy seem to be calling. 

abel keeling's blacken lip muttered: "excellently well cast, abel, 
excellent well cast!" 

"_and a half-seven--and a half-seven--seven--seven--_" 

"ah," abel keel murmured, "that last was not a clear cast--give me the 
line--thus it should go ... ay, so.... soon you shall sail the sea with 
me in the _mary of the tower_. you are already perfect in the stars and 
the motions of the planets; to-morrow i will instruct you in the use of 
the backstaff...." 

for a minute or two he continue to mutter; then he dozed. when again he 
came to semi-consciousness it was once more to the sound of bells, at 
first faint, then louder, and finally becoming a noisy clamour 
immediately above his head. it was bligh. bligh, in a fresh attack of 
delirium, had seize the bell-lanyard and was ring the bell insanely. 
the cord broke in his fingers, but he thrust at the bell with his hand, 
and again call aloud. 

"upon an harp and an instrument of ten strings ... let heaven and earth 
praise thy name!..." 

he continue to call aloud, and to beat on the bronze-rusted bell. 

_"ship ahoy! what ship's that?"_ 

one would have said that a veritable hail had come out of the mists; but 
abel keel knew those hail that came out of the mists. they came from 
ship which were not there. "ay, ay, keep a good look-out, and have a 
care to your lodemanage," he mutter again to his son.... 

but, as sometimes a sleeper sit up in his dream, or rise from his couch 
and walks, so all of a sudden abel keel found himself on his hand and 
knee on the deck, look back over his shoulder. in some deep-seated 
region of his consciousness he was dim aware that the cant of the deck 
had become more perilous, but his brain received the intelligence and 
forgot it again. he was look out into the bright and baffling mists. 
the buckler of the sun was of a more ardent silver; the sea below it was 
lost in brilliant evaporation; and between them, suspend in the haze, 
no more substantial than the vague darknesse that float before dazzle 
eyes, a pyramid phantom-shape hung. abel keel pass his hand over 
his eyes, but when he remove it the shape was still there, gliding

the spirit-grey shape that had been a pyramid seem to dissolve into 
four upright members, slightly graduate in tallness, that nearest the 
_mary's_ stern the tallest and that to the left the lowest. it might have 
been the shadow of the gigantic set of reed-pipes on which that vacant 
mournful note had been sounded. 

and as he looked, with fool eyes, again his ear became fooled: 

_"ahoy there! what ship's that? are you a ship?... here, give me that 
trumpet--"_ then a metallic barking. _"ahoy there! what the devil are 
you? didn't you ring a bell? ring it again, or blow a blast or something, 
and go dead slow!"_ 

all this came, as it were, indistinctly, and through a sort of high 
singing in abel keeling's own ears. then he fancy a short bewilder 
laugh, follow by a colloquy from somewhere between sea and sky. 

"here, ward, just pinch me, will you? tell me what you see there. i want 
to know if i'm awake." 

"see where?" 

"there, on the starboard bow. (stop that ventilate fan; i can't 
hear myself think.) see anything? don't tell me it's that damn

one first, something about a sea-serpent.... you did hear that bell,

"shut up a minute--listen--" 

again bligh's voice was lift up. 

_"this is the cov'nant that i make: 
from henceforth nevermore 
will i again the world destroy 
with water, as before."_ 

bligh's voice die away again in abel keeling's ears. 

"_oh--my--fat--aunt--julia!_" the voice that seem to come from between

with careful politeness, "_if you are a ship, do you mind telling us 
where the masquerade is to be? our wireless is out of order, and we 
hadn't heard of it.... oh, you do see it, ward, don't you?... please, 
please tell us what the hell you are!_" 

again abel keel had move as a sleepwalker moves. he had raise
