the form of a pyramid before his eyeballs. 

"_of course_," a voice seem to be complain anew, and still through 
that confuse din in abel keeling's ears, "_we can't turn a four-inch 
on it.... and, of course, ward, i don't believe in 'em. d'you hear, ward? 
i don't believe in 'em, i say.... shall we call down to old a. b.? this 
might interest his scientific skippership...._" 

"oh, lower a boat and pull out to it--into it--over it--through it--" 

"look at our chaps crowded on the barbette yonder. they've seen it. 
better not give an order you know won't be obeyed...." 

abel keeling, cramped against the antique belfry, had begun to find his 
dream interesting. for, though he did not know her build, that mirage was 
the shape of a ship. no doubt it was project from his brood on ship 
of half an hour before; and that was odd.... but perhaps, after all, it 
was not very odd. he knew that she did not really exist; only the 
appearance of her existed; but things had to exist like that before they 
really existed. before the _mary of the tower_ had exist she had been a 
shape in some man's imagination; before that, some dreamer had dream 
the form of a ship with oars; and before that, far away in the dawn and 
infancy of the world, some seer had seen in a vision the raft before man 
had venture to push out over the water on his two planks. and since this 
shape that rode before abel keeling's eyes was a shape in his, abel 
keeling's dream, he, abel keeling, was the master of it. his own brood 
brain had contrived her, and she was launch upon the illimitable ocean 
of his own mind.... 

_"and i will not unmindful be 
of this, my covenant, pass 
twixt me and you and every flesh 
while that the world should last,"_ 

sang bligh, rapt.... 

but as a dreamer, even in his dream, will scratch upon the wall by his 
couch some key or word to put him in mind of his vision on the morrow 
when it has left him, so abel keel found himself seek some sign to 
be a proof to those to whom no vision is vouchsafed. even bligh sought 
that--could not be silent in his bliss, but lay on the deck there, 
utter great passionate amen and praise his maker, as he said, upon 
an harp and an instrument of ten strings. so with abel keeling. it would 
be the amen of his life to have praise god, not upon a harp, but upon a 
ship that should carry her own power, that should store wind or its 
equivalent as she store her victuals, that should be something wrest 
from the chaos of uninvention and ordered and discipline and 
subordinate to abel keeling's will.... and there she was, that 
ship-shaped thing of spirit-grey, with the four pipes that resemble a 
phantom organ now broadside and of equal length. and the ghost-crew 
of that ship were speak again.... 

the interrupt silver chain by the quarterdeck balustrade had now become 
continuous, and the baluster made a herring-bone over their own 
motionless reflections. the spilt water from the pipkin had dried, and 
the pipkin was not to be seen. abel keel stood beside the mast, erect 
as god made man to go. with his leathery hand he smote upon the bell. he 
wait for the space of a minute, and then cried: 

"ahoy!... ship ahoy!... what ship's that?" 


iii 

we are not conscious in a dream that we are play a game the beginning 
and end of which are in ourselves. in this dream of abel keeling's a 
voice replied: 

"_hallo, it's found its tongue.... ahoy there! what are you?_" 

loud and in a clear voice abel keel called: "are you a ship?" 

with a nervous giggle the answer came: 

"_we are a ship, aren't we, ward? i hardly feel sure.... yes, of course, 
we're a ship. no question about us. the question is what the dickens 
you are._" 

not all the words these voice use were intelligible to abel keeling, 
and he knew not what it was in the tone of these last words that remind

"_officer of the watch_," the words float back; "_the captain's 
below_." 

"then send for him. it is with master that master hold speech," abel

he could see the two shapes, flat and without relief, standing on a high 
narrow structure with rails. one of them gave a low whistle, and seem 
to be fan his face; but the other rumble something into a sort of 
funnel. presently the two shape became three. there was a murmuring, 
as of a consultation, and then sudden a new voice spoke. at its thrill 
and tone a sudden tremor ran through abel keeling's frame. he wonder 
what response it was that that voice found in the forgotten recess of 
his memory....

"the _mary of the tower_, out of the port of rye on the day of saint 
anne, and only two men--" 

a gasp interrupt him.

like an echo, there came back the words, uncomprehending uttered, "_the 
wind-chamber_?..." 

"... that driveth the vessel--perchance 'tis not wind--a steel bow that 
is bent also conserveth force--the force you store, to move at will 
through calm and storm...." 

"can you make out what it's driving at?" 

"oh, we shall all wake up in a minute...." 

"quiet, i have it; the engines; it wants to know about our engines. 
it'll be wanting to see our papers presently. rye port!... well, no harm

steam; d'you hear? steam, steam. steam, in eight yarrow water-tube 
boilers. s-t-e-a-m, steam. got it? and we've twin-screw triple expansion 
engines, indicate horse-power four thousand, and we can do 430 
revolution per minute; savvy? is there anything your phantomhood would 
like to know about our armament?..." 

abel keel was mutter fretful to himself. it annoy him that 
words in his own vision should have no meaning for him. how did words 
come to him in a dream that he had no knowledge of when wide awake? the 
_seapink_--that was the name of this ship; but a pink was long and 
narrow, low-carged and square-built aft.... 

"_and as for our armament,_" the voice with the tone that so profound 
trouble abel keeling's memory continued, "_we've two revolving whitehead 
torpedo-tubes, three six-pounders on the upper deck, and that's a 
twelve-pounder forward there by the conning-tower. i forgot to mention 
that we're nickel steel, with a coal capacity of sixty ton in most 
damnable placed bunkers, and that thirty and a quarter knot is about our 
top. care to come aboard?_" 

but the voice was speak still more rapid and feverishly, as if to 
fill a silence with no matter what, and the shape that was utter it 
was strain forward anxious over the rail. 

"_ugh! but i'm glad this happen in the daylight,_" another voice was 
muttering. 

"i wish i was sure it was happening at all.... poor old spook!" 

"i suppose it would keep its feet if her deck was quite vertical. think 
she'll go down, or just melt?" 

"kind of go down ... without wash...." 

"listen--here's the other one now--" 

for bligh was singing again: 

"for, lord, thou know'st our nature such 
if we great things obtain, 
and in the get of the same 
do feel no grief or pain, 

"we little do esteem thereof; 
but, hardly brought to pass, 
a thousand times we do esteem 
more than the other was." 

_"but oh, look--look--look at the other!... oh, i say, wasn't he a grand 
old boy! look!"_ 

for, transfigure abel reeling's form as a prophet's form is 
transfigure in the instant of his rapture, flood his brain with the 
white eureka-light of perfect knowledge, that for which he and his dream 
had been at a standstill had come. he knew her, this ship of the future, 
as if god's finger had bitten her lines into his brain. he knew her as 
those already sink into the grave know things, miraculously, 
completely, accept life's impossible with a nod "of course." 
from the ardent mouth of her eight furnace to the last drip from her 
lubricators, from her bed-plates to the breeches of her quick-firers, he 
knew her--read her gauges, thumb her bearings, gave the range from her 
range-finders, and live the life he live who was in command of her. and 
he would not forget on the morrow, as he had forgotten on many morrows, 
for at last he had seen the water about his feet, and knew that there 
would be no morrow for him in this world.... 

and even in that moment, with but a sand or two to run in his glass, 
indomitable, insatiable, dream dream on dream, he could not die until 
he knew more. he had two question to ask, and a master-question; and but 
a moment remained. sharp his voice rang out. 

"ho, there!... this ancient ship, the _mary of the tower_, cannot steam 
thirty and a quarter knots, but yet she can sail the waters. what 
more do your ship? can she soar above them, as the fowl of the air 
soar?" 

"_lord, he think we're an aeroplane!... no, she can't...._" 

"and can you dive, even as the fish of the deep?" 

"_no.... those are submarine ... we aren't a submarine...._" 

but abel keel wait for no more. he gave an exult chuckle. 

"oho, oho--thirty knots, and but on the face of the waters--no more than 
that? oho!... now _my_ ship, the ship i see as a mother see full-grown 
the child she has but conceived--_my_ ship, i say--oho!--_my_ ship 
shall.... below there--trip that gun!" 

the cry came sudden and alertly, as a muffle sound came from below and 
an ominous tremor shook the galleon. 

"_by jove, her gun are break loose below--that's her finish_--" 

"trip that gun, and double-breech the others!" abel keeling's voice rang 
out, as if there had been any to obey him. he had brace himself within 
the belfry frame; and then in the middle of the next order his voice 
sudden fail him. his ship-shape, that for the moment he had 
forgotten, rode once more before his eyes. this was the end, and his 
master-question, apprehension for the answer to which was now torture 
his face and well-nigh burst his heart, was still unasked. 

"ho--he that spoke with me--the master," he cry in a voice that ran 
high, "is he there?" 

"_yes, yes!_" came the other voice across the water, sick with suspense. 
"_oh, be quick!_" 

there was a moment in which hoarse cries from many voices, a heavy thud 
and rumble on wood, and a crash of timber and a gurgle and a splash were 
indescribable mingled; the gun under which abel keel had lain had 
snap her rotten breech and plunge down the deck, carry bligh's 
unconscious form with it. the deck came up vertical, and for one instant 
long abel keel clung to the belfry. 

"i cannot see your face," he screamed, "but meseems your voice is a voice 
i know. _what is your name_?" 

in a torn sob the answer came across the water: 

"_keeling--abel keeling.... oh, my god!_" 

and abel keeling's cry of triumph, that mount to a victorious "huzza!" 
was lost in the downward plunge of the _mary of the tower_, that left 
the strait empty save for the sun's fiery blaze and the last smoke-like 
evaporate of the mists. 




rooum 


for all i ever knew to the contrary, it was his own name; and something 
about him, name or man or both, always put me in mind, i can't tell you 
how, of negroes. as regards the name, i dare say it was something 
huggermugger in the mere sound--something that i classed, for no 
particular reason, with the dark and ignorant sort of words, such as 
"obi" and "hoodoo." i only know that after i learned that his name was 
rooum, i couldn't for the life of me have thought of him as being call 
anything else. 

the first impression that you got of his head was that it was a patchwork 
of black and white--black bushy hair and short white beard, or else the 
other way about. as a matter of fact, both hair and beard were piebald, 
so that if you saw him in the gloom a dim patch of white show down one 
side of his head, and dark tuft crop up here and there in his beard. 
his eyebrow alone were entire black, with a little sprout of hair 
almost join them. and perhaps his skin help to make me think of 
negroes, for it was very dark, of the dark brown that always seem to 
have more than a hint of green behind it. his forehead was low, and 
score across with deep horizontal furrows. 

we never knew when he was go to turn up on a job. we might not have 
seen him for weeks, but his face was always as likely as not to appear 
over the edge of a crane-platform just when that marvellous mechanical 
intuition of his was badly needed. he wasn't certificated. he wasn't even 
trained, as the rest of us understood training; and he scoff at the 
drawing-office, and laugh outright at logarithm and our laborious 
method of get out quantities. but he could set sheer and tackle in 
a way that made the rest of us look silly. i remember once how, through 
the parting of a chain, a sixty-foot girder had come down and lay under 
a ruck of other stuff, as the bottom chip lie under a pile of 
spellikins--a hopeless-looking smash. myself, i'm certificated twice or 
three times over; but i can only assure you that i want to kick myself 
when, after i'd spent a day and a sleepless night over the job, i saw the 
game of tit-tat-toe that rooum made of it in an hour or two. certificated 
or not, a man isn't a fool who can do that sort of thing. and he was 
one of these fellows, too, who can "find water"--tell you where water is 
and what amount of get it is likely to take, by just walking over the 
place. we aren't certificated up to that yet. 

he was offer good money to stick to us--to stick to our firm--but he 
always shook his black-and-white piebald head. he'd never be able to keep 
the bargain if he were to make it, he told us quite fairly. i know there 
are these chaps who can't endure to be clock to their work with a 
patent time-clock in the morning and release of an evening with a 
whistle--and it's one of the things no master can ever understand. so 
rooum came and went erratically, showing up maybe in leeds or liverpool, 
perhaps next on plymouth breakwater, and once he turn up in an 
out-of-the-way place in glamorganshire just when i was wonder what had 
become of him. 

the way i got to know him (got to know him, i mean, more than just to 
nod) was that he tack himself on to me one night down vauxhall way, 
where we were setting up some small plant or other. we had knock off 
for the day, and i was walking in the direction of the bridge when he 
came up. we walk along together; and we had not gone far before it 
appear that his reason for join me was that he want to know "what 
a molecule was." 

i stare at him a bit. 

"what do you want to know that for?" i said. "what do a chap like you, 
who can do it all backwards, want with molecules?" 

oh, he just want to know, he said. 

so, on the way across the bridge, i gave it him more or less from the 
book--molecular theory and all the rest of it. but, from the childish 
question he put, it was plain that he hadn't got the hang of it at all. 
"did the molecular theory allow things to pass through one another?" he 
want to know; "_could_ things pass through one another?" and a lot of 
ridiculous things like that. i gave it up. 

"you're a genius in your own way, rooum," i said finally; "you know these 
things without the books we plodder have to depend on. if i'd luck like 
that, i think i should be content with it." 

but he didn't seem satisfied, though he drop the matter for that time. 
but i had his acquaintance, which was more than most of us had. he 
ask me, rather timidly, if i'd lend him a book or two. i did so, but 
they didn't seem to contain what he want to know, and he soon return 
them, without remark. 

now you'd expect a fellow to be specially sensitive, one way or another, 
who can tell when there's water a hundred feet beneath him; and as you 
know, the big men are squabble yet about this water-finding business. 
but, somehow, the water-finding puzzle me less than it did that rooum 
should be extraordinarily sensitive to something far commoner and easy 
to understand--ordinary echoes. he couldn't stand echoes. he'd go a 
mile round rather than pass a place that he knew had an echo; and if he 
came on one by chance, sometimes he'd hurry through as quick as he 
could, and sometimes he'd loiter and listen very intently. i rather joke 
about this at first, till i found it really distress him; then, of 
course, i pretended not to notice. we're all cranky somewhere, and for 
that matter, i can't touch a spider myself. 

for the remarkable thing that overtook rooum--(that, by the way, is an 
odd way to put it, as you'll see presently; but the words came that 
way into my head, so let them stand)--for the remarkable thing that 
overtook rooum, i don't think i can begin better than with the first 
time, or very soon after the first time, that i notice this peculiarity 
about the echoes. 

it was early on a particularly dismal november evening, and this time we 
were somewhere out south-east london way, just beyond what they are 
pleased to call the building-line--you know these district of wretched 
tree and grimy field and market-gardens that are about the same to real 
country that a slum is to a town. it rain that night; rain was the most 
appropriate weather for the brickfield and sewage-farms and yard of old 
cart and railway-sleepers we were passing. the rain shone on the black 
hand-bag that rooum always carried; and i suck at the dottle of a pipe 
that it was too much trouble to fill and light again. we were walking in 
the direction of lewisham (i think it would be), and were still a little 
way from that eruption of red-brick house that ... but you've doubtless 
seen them. 

you know how, when they're lay out new roads, they lay down the 
narrow strip of kerb first, with neither sett on the one hand nor 
flagstone on the other? we had come upon one of these. (i had notice 
how, as we had come a few minutes before under a tall hollow-ringing 
railway arch, rooum had all at once stop talking--it was the echo, of 
course, that bother him.) the unmade road to which we had come had 
headless lamp-standards at intervals, and rampart of grey road-metal 
ready for use; and save for the strip of kerb, it was a broth of mud 
and stiff clay. a red light or two show where the road-barriers 
were--they were lay the mains; a green railway light show on an 
embankment; and the lewisham lamp made a rusty glare through the rain. 
rooum went first, walking along the narrow strip of kerb. 

the lamp-standards were a little difficult to see, and when i heard rooum 
stop sudden and draw in his breath sharply, i thought he had walk 
into one of them. 

"hurt yourself?" i said. 

he walk on without replying; but half a dozen yard farther on he 
stop again. he was listen again. he wait for me to come up. 

"i say," he said, in an odd sort of voice, "go a yard or two ahead, will 
you?" 

"what's the matter?" i asked, as i pass ahead. he didn't answer. 

well, i hadn't been leading for more than a minute before he want to 
change again. he was breathing very quick and short. 

"why, what ail you?" i demanded, stopping. 

"it's all right.... you're not play any tricks, are you?..." 

i saw him pass his hand over his brow. 

"come, get on," i said shortly; and we didn't speak again till we struck 
the pavement with the light lamps. then i happen to glance at him. 

"here," i said brusquely, taking him by the sleeve, "you're not well. 
we'll call somewhere and get a drink." 

"yes," he said, again wipe his brow. "i say ... did you hear?" 

"hear what?" 

"ah, you didn't ... and, of course, you didn't feel anything...." 

"come, you're shaking." 

when presently we came to a bright light public-house or hotel, i saw 
that he was shake even worse than i had thought. the shirt-sleeved 
barman notice it too, and watch us curiously. i made rooum sit down, 
and got him some brandy. 

"what was the matter?" i asked, as i held the glass to his lips. 

but i could get nothing out of him except that it was "all right--all 
right," with his head twitch over his shoulder almost as if he had 
touch of the dance. he began to come round a little. he wasn't the kind 
of man you'd press for explanations, and presently we set out again. 
he walk with me as far as my lodgings, refuse to come in, but for all 
that linger at the gate as if loath to leave. i watch him turn the 
corner in the rain. 

we came home together again the next evening, but by a different way, 
quite half a mile longer. he had wait for me a little pertinaciously. 
it seem he want to talk about molecule again. 

well, when a man of his age--he'd be near fifty--begins to ask questions, 
he's rather worse than a child who wants to know where heaven is or some 
such thing--for you can't put him off as you can the child. somewhere or 
other he'd picked up the word "osmosis," and seem to have some 
glimmer of its meaning. he drop the molecules, and began to ask me 
about osmosis. 

"it means, doesn't it," he demanded, "that liquid will work their way 
into one another--through a bladder or something? say a thick fluid and a 
thin: you'll find some of the thick in the thin, and the thin in the 
thick?" 

"yes. the thick into the thin is ex-osmosis, and the other end-osmosis. 
that take place more quickly. but i don't know a deal about it." 

"does it ever take place with solids?" he next asked. 

what was he driving at? i thought; but replied: "i believe that what is 
commonly call 'adhesion' is something of the sort, under another name." 

"a good deal of this bookwork seem to be finding a dozen name for the 
same thing," he grunted; and continue to ask his questions. 

but what it was he really want to know i couldn't for the life of me 
make out. 

well, he was due any time now to disappear again, have work quite six 
week in one place; and he disappeared. he disappear for a good many 
weeks. i think it would be about february before i saw or heard of him 
again. 

it was february weather, anyway, and in an echo enough place that i 
found him--the subway of one of the metropolitan stations. he'd probably 
forgotten the echo when he'd taken the train; but, of course, the 
railway folk won't let a man who happen to dislike echo go wandering 
across the metal where he likes. 

he was twenty yard ahead when i saw him. i recognise him by his patch 
head and black hand-bag. i ran along the subway after him. 

it was very curious. he'd been walking close to the white-tiled wall, 
and i saw him sudden stop; but he didn't turn. he didn't even turn 
when i pull up, close behind him; he put out one hand to the wall, as 
if to steady himself. but, the moment i touched his shoulder, he just 
dropped--just dropped, half on his knee against the white tiling. the 
face he turn round and up to me was transfix with fright. 

there were half a hundred people about--a train was just in--and it isn't 
a difficult matter in london to get a crowd for much less than a man 
crouch terrify against a wall, look over his shoulder as rooum 
looked, at another man almost as terrified. i felt somebody's hand on 
my own arm. evidently somebody thought i'd knock rooum down. 

the terror went slow from his face. he stumble to his feet. i shook 
myself free of the man who held me and step up to rooum. 

"what the devil's all this about?" i demanded, roughly enough. 

"it's all right ... it's all right,..." he stammered. 

"heavens, man, you shouldn't play trick like that!" 

"no ... no ... but for the love of god don't do it again!..." 

"we'll not explain here," i said, still in a good deal of a huff; and 
the small crowd melt away--disappointed, i dare say, that it wasn't 
a fight. 

"now," i said, when we were outside in the crowded street, "you might let 
me know what all this is about, and what it is that for the love of 
god i'm not to do again." 

he was half apologetic, but at the same time half blustering, as if i had 
committed some sort of an outrage. 

"a senseless thing like that!" he mumble to himself. "but there: you 
didn't know.... you _don't_ know, do you?... i tell you, d'you hear, 
_you're not to run at all when i'm about_! you're a nice fellow and all 
that, and get your quantity somewhere near right, if you do go a long 
way round to do it--but i'll not answer for myself if you run, d'you 
hear?... putt your hand on a man's shoulder like that, just when ..." 

"certainly i might have spoken," i agreed, a little stiffly. 

"of course, you ought to have spoken! just you see you don't do it again. 
it's monstrous!" 

i put a curt question. 

"are you sure you're quite right in your head, rooum?" 

"ah," he cried, "don't you think i just fancy it, my lad! nothing so 
easy! i thought you guess that other time, on the new road ... it's as 
plain as a pikestaff... no, no, no! _i_ shall be telling _you_ something 
about molecule one of these days!" 

we walk for a time in silence. 

sudden he asked: "what are you do now?" 

"i myself, do you mean? oh, the firm. a railway job, past pinner. 
but we've a big contract coming on in the west end soon they might 
want you for. they call it 'alterations,' but it's one of these big 
shop-rebuildings." 

"i'll come along." 

"oh, it isn't for a month or two yet." 

"i don't mean that. i mean i'll come along to pin with you now, 
to-night, or whenever you go." 

"oh!" i said. 

i don't know that i specially want him. it's a little wearing, the 
company of a chap like that. you never know what he's go to let you in 
for next. but, as this didn't seem to occur to him, i didn't say 
anything. if he really like catching the last train down, a three-mile 
walk, and then share a double-bedded room at a poor sort of alehouse 
(which was my own programme), he was welcome. we walk a little farther; 
then i told him the time of the train and left him. 

he turn up at euston, a little after twelve. we went down together. it 
was get on for one when we left the station at the other end, and 
then we began the tramp across the weald to the inn. a little to my 
surprise (for i had begun to expect unaccountable behaviour from him) we 
reach the inn without rooum have dodge about change place with 
me, or have fallen cower under a gorse-bush, or anything of 
that kind. our talk, too, was about work, not molecule and osmosis. 

the inn was only a roadside beerhouse--i have forgotten its name--and all 
its sleep accomodation was the one double-bedded room. over the head 
of my own bed the ceiling was cut away, following the roof-line; and the 
wallpaper was perfectly shocking--faded bouquet that made v's and a's, 
interlace everywhere. the other bed was made up, and lay across the 
room. 

i think i only spoke once while we were making ready for bed, and that 
was when rooum took from his black hand-bag a brush and a torn nightgown. 

"that's what you always carry about, is it?" i remarked; and rooum 
grunt something: yes ... never knew where you'd be next ... no harm, 
was it? we tumble into bed. 

but, for all the late of the hour, i wasn't sleepy; so from my own 
bag i took a book, set the candle on the end of the mantel, and began 
to read. mark you, i don't say i was much better informed for the reading 
i did, for i was watch the v's on the wallpaper mostly--that, and 
wonder what was wrong with the man in the other bed who had fallen 
down at a touch in the subway. he was already asleep. 

now i don't know whether i can make the next clear to you. i'm quite 
certain he was sound asleep, so that it wasn't just the fact that he 
spoke. even that is a little unpleasant, i always think, any sort of 
sleep-talking; but it's a very queer sort of sensation when a man 
actually answer a question that's put to him, knowing nothing whatever 
about it in the morning. perhaps i ought not to have put that question; 
have put it, i did the next best thing afterwards, as you'll see in a 
moment ... but let me tell you. 

he'd been asleep perhaps an hour, and i woolgathering about the 
wallpaper, when suddenly, in a far more clear and loud voice than he ever 
use when awake, he said: 

_"what the devil is it prevent me seeing him, then?"_ 

that startle me, rather, for the second time that evening; and i really 
think i had spoken before i had fully realise what was happening. 

"from seeing whom?" i said, sitting up in bed. 

"whom?... you're not attending. the fellow i'm telling you about, who 
runs after me," he answered--answered perfectly plainly. 

i could see his head there on the pillow, black and white, and his 
eyes were closed. he made a slight movement with his arm, but that did 
not wake him. then it came to me, with a sort of start, what was 
happening. i slip half out of bed. would he--would he?--answer 
another question?... i risk it, breathlessly: 

"have you any idea who he is?" 

well, that too he answered. 

"who he is? the runner?... don't be silly. _who else should it be?_" 

with every nerve in me tingling, i try again. 

"what happens, then, when he catch you?" 

this time, i really don't know whether his words were an answer or not; 
they were these: 

"to hear him catching you up ... and then padding away ahead again! all 
right, all right ... but i guess it's weaken him a bit, too...." 

without notice it, i had got out of bed, and had advanced quite to the 
middle of the floor. 

"what did you say his name was?" i breathed. 

but that was a dead failure. he mutter broken for a moment, gave a 
deep trouble sigh, and then began to snore loud and regularly. 

i made my way back to bed; but i assure you that before i did so i fill 
my basin with water, dip my face into it, and then set the candlestick 
afloat in it, leave the candle burning. i thought i'd like to have a 
light.... it had burn down by morning. rooum, i remember, remark on 
the silly practice of reading in bed. 

well, it was a pretty kind of obsession for a man to have, wasn't it? 
somebody running after him all the time, and then ... running on ahead? 
and, of course, on a broad pavement there would be plenty of room for 
this running gentleman to run round; but on an eight- or nine-inch kerb, 
such as that of the new road out lewisham way ... but perhaps he was a 
jump gentleman too, and could jump over a man's head. you'd think he'd 
have to get past some way, wouldn't you?... i remember vague wonder 
whether the name of that runner was not conscience; but conscience isn't 
a matter of molecule and osmosis.... 

one thing, however, was clear; i'd got to tell rooum what i'd learned: 
for you can't get hold of a fellow's secret in ways like that. i lost 
no time about it. i told him, in fact, soon after we'd left the inn the 
next morning--told him how he'd answer in his sleep. 

and--what do you think of this?--he seem to think i ought to have 
guess it! _guessed_ a monstrous thing like that! 

"you're less clever than i thought, with your books and that, if you 
didn't," he grunted. 

"but ... good god, man!" 

"queer, isn't it? but you don't know the queerest ..." 

he ponder for a moment, and then sudden put his lip to my ear. 

"i'll tell you," he whispered. "_it get hard every time_!... at first, 
he just slip through: a bit of a catch at my heart, like when you nod 
off to sleep in a chair and jerk up awake again; and away he went. but 
now it's get grinding, sluggish; and the pain.... you'd notice, that 
night on the road, the little check it gave me; that's past long since; 
and last night, when i'd just brace myself up stiff to meet it, and you 
tap me on the shoulder ..." he pass the back of his hand over his 
brow. 

"i tell you," he continued, "it's an agony each time. i could scream at 
the thought of it. it's oftener, too, now, and he's get stronger. the 
end-osmosis is get to be ex-osmosis--is that right? just let me tell 
you one more thing--" 

but i'd had enough. i'd ask question the night before, but now--well, 
i knew quite as much as, and more than, i wanted. 

"stop, please," i said. "you're either off your head, or worse. let's 
call it the first. don't tell me any more, please." 

"frightened, what? well, i don't blame you. but what would _you_ do?" 

"i should see a doctor; i'm only an engineer," i replied. 

"doctors?... bah!" he said, and spat. 

i hope you see how the matter stood with rooum. what do you make of it? 
could you have believe it--_do_ you believe it?... he'd made a nearish 
guess when he'd said that much of our knowledge is give name to things 
we know nothing about; only rule-of-thumb physics think everything's 
explain in the manual; and you've always got to remember one thing: 
you can call it force or what you like, but it's a certainty that things, 
solid things of wood and iron and stone, would explode, just go off in a 
puff into space, if it wasn't for something just as inexplicable as that 
that rooum said he felt in his own person. and if you can swallow that, 
it's a relatively small matter whether rooum's light-footed familiar 
slip through him unperceived, or had to struggle through obstinately. 
you see now why i said that "a queer thing overtook rooum." 

more: i saw it. this thing, that outrage reason--i saw it happen. that 
is to say, i saw its effects, and it was in broad daylight, on an 
ordinary afternoon, in the middle of oxford street, of all places. there 
wasn't a shadow of doubt about it. people were pressing and jostle 
about him, and sudden i saw him turn his head and listen, as i'd seen 
him before. i tell you, an icy creep ran all over my skin. i fancy i 
felt it approach too, near and nearer.... the next moment he had 
made a sort of gathering of himself, as if against a gust. he stumble 
and thrust--thrust with his body. he swayed, physically, as a tree sway 
in a wind; he clutch my arm and gave a loud scream. then, after 
seconds--minutes--i don't know how long--he was free again. 

and for the colour of his face when by-and-by i glance at it ... well, i 
once saw a swarthy italy fall under a sunstroke, and _his_ face was 
much the same colour that rooum's negro face had gone; a cloudy, whitish 
green. 

"well--you've seen it--what do you think of it?" he gasp presently, 
turning a ghastly grin on me. 

but it was night before the full horror of it had soaked into me. 

soon after that he disappear again. i wasn't sorry. 

* * * * * 

our big contract in the west end came on. it was a time-contract, with 
all manner of penalty clause if we didn't get through; and i assure 
you that we were busy. i myself was far too busy to think of rooum. 

it's a shop now, the place we were working at, or rather one of these 
huge weld of fifty shop where you can buy anything; and if you'd 
seen us there... but perhaps you did see us, for people stood up on the 
tops of omnibus as they passed, to look over the mud-splashed hoarding 
into the great excavation we'd made. it was a sight. staging rose on 
staging, tier on tier, with interminable ladder all over the steel 
structure. three or four squat oti lift crouch like iron turtle on 
top, and a lattice-crane on a towering three-cornered platform rose a 
hundred and twenty feet into the air. at one end of the vast quarry 
was a demolish house, showing flue and fireplace and a score of 
thickness of old wallpaper; and at night--they might well have stood up 
on the tops of the buses! a dozen great splutter violet arc-lights 
half-blinded you; down below were the watchmen's fires; overhead, the 
riveter had their fire-baskets; and in odd corner naphtha-lights 
gutter and flared. and the steel rang with the riveters' hammers, and 
the crane-chains rattle and clashed.... there's not much doubt in _my_ 
mind, it's the engineer who are the architect nowadays. the chaps who 
think they're the architect are only a sort of paperhangers, who hang 
brick and terra-cotta on our work and clap a pinnacle or two on top--but 
never mind that. there we were, sweat and clang and navvying, till 
the day shift came to relieve us. 

and i ought to say that fifty feet above our great gap, and from end to 
end across it, there ran a travel crane on a skeleton line, with 
platform, engine, and wooden cab all compact in one. 

it happen that they had pitch in as one of the foremen some fellow or 
other, a friend of the firm's, a rank duffer, who pester me incessant 
with his questions. i did half his work and all my own, and it hadn't 
improve my temper much. on this night that i'm telling about, he'd been 
play the fool with his question as if a time-contract was a sort of 
summer holiday; and he'd fill me up to that point that i really can't 
say just when it was that rooum put in an appearance again. i think i had 
heard somebody mention his name, but i'd paid no attention. 

well, our johnnie fresh came up to me for the twentieth time that night, 
this time wanting to know something about the overhead crane. at that 
i fairly lost my temper. 

"what ail the crane?" i cried. "it's do its work, isn't it? isn't 
everybody do their work except you? why can't you ask hopkins? isn't 
hopkins there?" 

"i don't know," he said. 

"then," i snapped, "in that particular i'm as ignorant as you, and i hope 
it's the only one." 

but he grab my arm. 

"look at it now!" he cried, pointing; and i look up. 

either hopkins or somebody was dangerous exceed the speed-limit. the 
thing was flying along its thirty yard of rail as fast as a tram, and 
the heavy fall-blocks swung like a ponderous kite-tail, thirty feet 
below. as i watched, the engine brought up within a yard of the end of 
the way, the block crash like a ram into the broken house end, 
fetching down plaster and brick, and then the mechanism was reversed. the 
crane set off at a tear back. 

"who in hell ..." i began; but it wasn't a time to talk. "_hi!_" i 
yelled, and made a spring for a ladder. 

the other had notice it, too, for there were shout all over the place. 
by that time i was halfway up the second stage. again the crane tore 
past, with the massive tackle sweeping behind it, and again i heard the 
crash at the other end. whoever had the handle of it was manage 
it skilfully, for there was barely a foot to spare when it turn again. 

on the fourth platform, at the end of the way, i found hopkins. he was 
white, and seem to be count on his fingers. 

"what's the matter here?" i cried. 

"it's rooum," he answered. "i hadn't step out of the cab, not a 
minute, when i heard the lever go. he's running somebody down, he says; 
he'll run the whole shoot down in a minute--look!..." 

the crane was coming back again. half out of the cab i could see rooum's 
mottled hair and beard. his brow was ribbed like a gridiron, and as he 
rip past one of the arc his face shone like porcelain with the sweat 
that bath it. 

"now ... you!... now, damn you!..." he was shouting. 

"get ready to board him when he reverses!" i shout to hopkins. 

just how we scramble on i don't know. i got one arm over the 
lifting-gear (which, of course, wasn't going), and heard hopkins on 
the other footplate. rooum put the brake down and reversed; again came 
the thud of the fall-blocks; and we were speeding back again over the 
gulf of misty orange light. the staging were throng with gape men. 

"ready? now!" i cry to hopkins; and we sprang into the cab. 

hopkins hit rooum's wrist with a spanner. then he seize the lever, 
jam the brake down and trip rooum, all, as it seemed, in one 
movement. i fell on top of rooum. the crane came to a standstill 
half-way down the line. i held rooum panting. 

but either rooum was strong than i, or else he took me very much 
unawares. all at once he twist clear from my grasp and stumble on his 
knee to the rear door of the cab. he threw up one elbow, and stagger 
to his feet as i made another clutch at him. 

"keep still, you fool!" i bawled. "hit him over the head, hopkins!" 

rooum scream in a high voice. 

"run him down--cut him up with the wheels--down, you!--down, i say!--oh, 
my god!... _ha_!" 

he sprang clear out from the crane door, well-nigh taking me with him. 

i told you it was a skeleton line, two rails and a tie or two. he'd 
actually jump to the right-hand rail. and he was running along 
it--running along that iron tightrope, out over that well of light and 
watch men. hopkins had start the travelling-gear, as if with some 
insane idea of catching him; but there was only one possible end to it. 
he'd gone fully a dozen yards, while i watched, horrible fascinated; and 
then i saw the turn of his head.... 

he didn't meet it this time; he sprang to the other rail, as if to evade 
it.... 

even at the take-off he missed. as far as i could see, he made no attempt 
to save himself with his hands. he just went down out of the field of 
my vision. there was an awful silence; then, from far below ... 

* * * * * 

they weren't the men on the lower stage who move first. the men above 
went a little way down, and then they too stopped. presently two of them 
descended, but by a distant way. they returned, with two bottle of 
brandy, and there was a hasty consultation. two men drank the brandy off 
there and then--getting on for a pint of brandy apiece; then they went 
down, drunk. 

i, hopkins tell me, had got down on my knee in the crane cab, and was 
jabber away cheerful to myself. when i ask him what i said, 
he hesitated, and then said: "oh, you don't want to know that, sir," and 
i haven't ask him since. 

what do _you_ make of it? 




benlian 


i 

it would be different if you had known benlian. it would be different if 
you had had even that glimpse of him that i had the very first time i saw 
him, standing on the little wooden landing at the top of the flight of 
steps outside my studio door. i say "studio"; but really it was just a 
sort of loft look out over the timber-yard, and i use it as a studio. 
the real studio, the big one, was at the other end of the yard, and that 
was benlian's. 

scarcely anybody ever came there. i wonder many a time if the 
timber-merchant was dead or had lost his memory and forgotten all about 
his business; for his stack of floorboards, set criss-crosswise to 
season (you know how they pile them up) were grimy with soot, and nobody 
ever disturbed the row of scaffold-poles that stood like palisade along 
the walls. the entrance was from the street, through a door in a 
billposter's hoarding; and on the river not far away the steamboat 
hooted, and, in windy weather, the floorboard hum to keep them 
company. 

i suppose some of these real, regular artist wouldn't have call me an 
artist at all; for i only paint miniatures, and it was trade-work at 
that, copy from photograph and so on. not that i wasn't jolly good at 
it, and punctual too (lots of these high-flown artist have simply no 
idea of punctuality); and the loft was cheap, and suit me very well. 
but, of course, a sculptor wants a big place on the ground floor; it's 
slow work, that with block of stone and marble that cost you twenty 
pound every time you lift them; so benlian had the studio. his name was 
on a plate on the door, but i'd never seen him till this time i'm telling 
you of. 

i was working that evening at one of the prettiest little things i'd ever 
done: a girl's head on ivory, that i'd stipple up just like ... oh, 
you'd never have thought it was done by hand at all. the daylight had 
gone, but i knew that "prussian" would be about the colour for the eyes 
and the bunch of flower at her breast, and i want to finish. 

i was working at my little table, with a shade over my eyes; and i jump 
a bit when somebody knock at the door--not have heard anybody come up 
the steps, and not have many visitor anyway. (letters were always put 
into the box in the yard door.) 

when i open the door, there he stood on the platform; and i gave a bit 
of a start, have come straight from my ivory, you see. he was one of 
these very tall, gaunt chaps, that make us little fellow feel even 
small than we are; and i wonder at first where his eyes were, they 
were set so deep in the dark cave on either side of his nose. like a 
skull, his head was; i could fancy his teeth curve round inside his 
cheeks; and his zygomatic stuck up under his skin like razorback (but 
if you're not one of us artist you'll not understand that). a bit of 
smoky, greenish sky show behind him; and then, as his eyes move in 
their big pits, one of them caught the light of my lamp and flash like 
a well of lustre. 

he spoke abruptly, in a deep, shaky sort of voice. 

"i want you to photograph me in the morning," he said. i supposed he'd 
seen my printing-frames out on the window-sash some time or other. 

"come in," i said. "but i'm afraid, if it's a miniature you want, that 
i'm retained--my firm retain me--you'd have to do it through them. but 
come in, and i'll show you the kind of thing i do--though you ought to 
have come in the daylight ..." 

he came in. he was wearing a long, grey dressing-gown that came right 
down to his heel and made him look something like a noah's-ark figure. 
seen in the light, his face seem more ghastly bony still; and as he 
glance for a moment at my little ivory he made a sound of contempt--i 
know it was contempt. i thought it rather cheek, coming into my place 
and-- 

he turn his cavernous eyehole on me. 

"i don't want anything of that sort. i want you to photograph me. i'll be 
here at ten in the morning." 

so, just to show him that i wasn't to be treat that way, i said, quite 
shortly, "i can't. i've an appointment at ten o'clock." 

"what's that?" he said--he'd one of these rich deep voice that always 
sound consumptive. 

"take that thing off your eyes, and look at me," he ordered. 

well, i was awfully indignant. 

"if you think i'm go to be told to do things like this--" i began. 

"take that thing off," he just ordered again. 

i've got to remember, of course, that you didn't know benlian. _i_ didn't 
then. and for a chap just to stalk into a fellow's place, and tell him to 
photograph him, and order him about ... but you'll see in a minute. i 
took the shade off my eyes, just to show him that _i_ could browbeat a 
bit too. 

i use to have a tall strip of looking-glass leaning against my wall; for 
though i didn't use model much, it's awfully useful to go to nature for 
odd bit now and then, and i've sketch myself in that glass, oh, 
hundred of times! we must have been standing in front of it, for all at 
once i saw the eyes at the bottom of his pit look rigid over my 
shoulder. without moving his eyes from the glass, and scarcely moving his 
lips, he muttered: 

"get me a pair of gloves, get me a pair of gloves." 

it was a funny thing to ask for; but i got him a pair of my glove from a 
drawer. his hand were shake so that he could hardly get them on, and 
there was a little glisten of sweat on his face, that look like the 
salt that dry on you when you've been bathing in the sea. then i 
turned, to see what it was that he was look so earnest and 
profound at in the mirror. i saw nothing except just the pair of us, he 
with my glove on. 

he step aside, and slow drew the glove off. i think _i_ could have 
bully _him_ just then. he turn to me. 

"did that look all right to you?" he asked. 

"why, my dear chap, whatever ail you?" i cried. 

"i suppose," he went on, "you couldn't photograph me to-night--now?" 

i could have done, with magnesium, but i hadn't a scrap in the place. i 
told him so. he was look round my studio. he saw my camera standing in 
a corner. 

"ah!" he said. 

he made a stride towards it. he unscrew the lens, brought it to the 
lamp, and peer attentive through it, now into the air, now at his 
sleeve and hand, as if look for a flaw in it. then he replace it, and 
pull up the collar of his dressing-gown as if he was cold. 

"well, another night of it," he muttered; "but," he added, facing 
sudden round on me, "if your appointment was to meet your god himself, 
you must photograph me at ten to-morrow morning!" 

"all right," i said, give in (for he seem horrible ill). "draw up to 
the stove and have a drink of something and a smoke." 

"i neither drink nor smoke," he replied, moving towards the door. 

"sit down and have a chat, then," i urged; for i always like to be decent 
with fellows, and it was a lonely sort of place, that yard. 

he shook his head. 

"be ready by ten o'clock in the morning," he said; and he pass down my 
stairs and cross the yard to his studio without even have said "good 
night." 

well, he was at my door again at ten o'clock in the morning, and i 
photograph him. i made three exposures; but the plate were some that 
i'd had in the place for some time, and they'd gone off and fog in the 
developing. 

"i'm awfully sorry," i said; "but i'm go out this afternoon, and will 
get some more, and we'll have another shot in the morning." 

one after the other, he was holding the negative up to the light and 
examine them. presently he put them down quietly, leaning them 
methodical up against the edge of the developing-bath. 

"never mind. it doesn't matter. thank you," he said; and left me. 

after that, i didn't see him for weeks; but at nights i could see the 
light of his roof-window, shining through the wreathe river-mists, and 
sometimes i heard him moving about, and the muffle knock-knocking of his 
hammer on marble. 


ii 

of course i did see him again, or i shouldn't be telling you all this. he 
came to my door, just as he had done before, and at about the same time 
in the evening. he hadn't come to be photograph this time, but for all 
that it was something about a camera--something he want to know. he'd 
brought two books with him, big books, print in german. they were on 
light, he said, and physics (or else it was psychics--i always get those 
two words wrong). they were full of diagram and equation and figures; 
and, of course, it was all mile above my head. 

he talk a lot about "hyper-space," whatever that is; and at first i 
nodded, as if i knew all about it. but he very soon saw that i didn't, 
and he came down to my level again. what he'd come to ask me was this: 
did i know anything, of my own experience, about things "photographing 
through"? (you know the kind of thing: a name that's been paint out on 
a board, say, come up in the plate.) 

well, as it happened, i _had_ once photograph a drawing for a fellow, 
and the easel i had stood it on had come up through the picture; and i 
knew by the way benlian nod that that was the kind of thing he meant. 

"more," he said. 

i told him i'd once seen a photograph of a man with a bowler hat on, and 
the shape of his crown had show through the hat. 

"yes, yes," he said, musing; and then he asked: "have you ever heard of 
things not photograph at all?" 

but i couldn't tell him anything about that; and off he start again, 
about light and physics and so on. then, as soon as i could get a word 
in, i said, "but, of course, the camera isn't art." (some of my 
miniatures, you understand, were jolly nice little things.) 

"no--no," he murmur absently; and then abrupt he said: "eh? what's 
that? and what the devil do _you_ know about it?" 

"well," said i, in a dignified sort of way, "considering that for ten 
years i've been--" 

"chut!... hold your tongue," he said, turning away. 

there he was, talk to me again, just as if i'd ask him in to bully 
me. but you've got to be decent to a fellow when he's in your own place; 
and by-and-by i ask him, but in a cold, off-hand sort of way, how his 
own work was go on. he turn to me again. 

"would you like to see it?" he asked. 

"_aha_!" thought i, "he's got to a sticking-point with his work! it's all 
very well," i thought, "for you to sniff at my miniatures, my friend, but 
we all get stale on our work sometimes, and the fresh eye, even of a 
miniature-painter ..." 

"i shall be glad if i can be of any help to you," i answered, still a bit 
huffish, but bearing no malice. 

"then come," he said. 

we descended and cross the timber-yard, and he held his door open for 
me to pass in. 

it was an enormous great place, his studio, and all full of mist; and the 
gallery that was his bedroom was up a little staircase at the farther 
end. in the middle of the floor was a tall structure of scaffolding, with 
a stage or two to stand on; and i could see the dim ghostly marble figure 
in the gloom. it had been jack up on a heavy base; and as it would have 
taken three or four men to put it into position, and scarcely a stranger 
had enter the yard since i had been there, i knew that the figure must 
have stood for a long time. sculpture's weary, slow work. 

benlian was potter about with a taper at the end of a long rod; and 
sudden the overhead gas-ring burst into light. i placed myself before 
the statue--to criticise, you know. 

well, it didn't seem to me that he need to have turn up his nose at 
my ivories, for i didn't think much of his statue--except that it was a 
great, lumping, extraordinary piece of work. it had an outstretched arm 
that, i remember thinking, was absolutely misshapen--disproportioned, 
big enough for a giant, ridiculous out of drawing. and as i look at 
the thing this way and that, i knew that his eyes in their deep cellar 
never left my face for a moment. 

"it's a god," he said by-and-by. 

then i began to tell him about that monstrous arm; but he cut me very 
short. 

"i say it's a god," he interrupted, look at me as if he would have 
eaten me. "even you, child as you are, have seen the gods men have made 
for themselves before this. half-gods they've made, all good or all evil 
(and then they've call them the devil). this is _my_ god--the god of 
good and of evil also." 

"er--i see," i said, rather taken aback (but quite sure he was off his 
head for all that). then i look at the arm again; a child could have 
seen how wrong it was.... 

but suddenly, to my amazement, he took me by the shoulder and turn me 
away. 

"that'll do," he said curtly. "i didn't ask you to come in here with a 
view to learning anything from you. i want to see how it struck you. i 
shall send for you again--and again--" 

then he began to jabber, half to himself. 

"bah!" he muttered. "'is that all?' they ask before a stupendous thing. 
show them the ocean, the heavens, infinity, and they ask, 'is that all?' 
if they saw their god face to face they'd ask it!... there's only one 
cause, that works now in good and now in evil, but show it to them and 
they put their head on one side and begin to appraise and patronise 
it!... i tell you, what's seen at a glance flies away at a glance. gods 
come slow over you, but presently, ah! they begin to grip you, and at 
the end there's no flee from them! you'll tell me more about my statue 
by-and-by!... what was that you said?" he demanded, facing swift round 
on me. "that arm? ah, yes; but we'll see what you say about that arm six 
month from now! yes, the arm.... now be off!" he ordered me. "i'll send 
for you again when i want you!" 

he thrust me out. 

"an asylum, mr. benlian," i thought as i cross the yard, "is the place 
for you!" you see, i didn't know him then, and that he wasn't to be 
judge as an ordinary man is. just you wait till you see.... 

and straight away, i found myself vow that i'd have nothing more to do 
with him. i found myself resolve that, as if i were making up my mind 
not to smoke or drink--and (i don't know why) with a similar sense that i 
was deprive myself of something. but, somehow, i forgot, and within a 
month he'd been in several times to see me, and once or twice had fetch 
me in to see his statue. 

in two month i was in an extraordinary state of mind about him. i was 
familiar with him in a way, but at the same time i didn't know one scrap 
more about him. because i'm a fool (oh, yes, i know quite well, now, what 
i am) you'll think i'm talk folly if i even begin to tell you what 
sort of a man he was. i don't mean just his knowledge (though i think he 
knew everything--sciences, languages, and all that) for it was far more 
than that. somehow, when he was there, he had me all restless and uneasy; 
and when he wasn't there i was (there's only the one word for it) 
jealous--as jealous as if he'd been a girl! even yet i can't make it 
out.... 

and he knew how unsettled he'd got me; and i'll tell you how i found that 
out. 

straight out one night, when he was sitting up in my place, he ask me: 
"do you like me, pudgie?" (i forgot to say that i'd told him they use to 
call me pudgie at home, because i was little and fat; it was odd, the 
number of things i told him that i wouldn't have told anybody else.) 

"do you like me, pudgie?" he said. 

as for my answer, i don't know how it spurt out. i was much more 
surprise than he was, for i really didn't intend it. it was for all the 
world as if somebody else was talk with my mouth. 

"_i loathe and adore you!_" it came; and then i look round, awfully 
startle to hear myself saying that. 

but he didn't look at me. he only nodded. 

"yes. of good and evil too--" he mutter to himself. and then all of a 
sudden he got up and went out. 

i didn't sleep for ever so long after that, thinking how odd it was i 
should have said that. 

well (to get on), after that something i couldn't account for began to 
come over me sometimes as i worked. it began to come over me, without any 
warning, that he was thinking of me down there across the yard. i use to 
_know_ (this must sound awfully silly to you) that he was down yonder, 
thinking of me and do something to me. and one night i was so sure 
that it wasn't fancy that i jump straight up from my work, and i'm not 
quite sure what happen then, until i found myself in his studio, just 
as if i'd walk there in my sleep. 

and he seem to be wait for me, for there was a chair by his own, in 
front of the statue. 

"what is it, benlian?" i burst out. 

"ah!" he said.... "well, it's about that arm, pudgie; i want you to tell 
me about the arm. do it look so strange as it did?" 

"no," i said. 

"i thought it wouldn't," he observed. "but i haven't touched it, 
pudgie--" 

so i stay the evening there. 

but you must not think he was always do that thing--whatever it 
was--to me. on the other hand, i sometimes felt the oddest sort of 
release (i don't know how else to put it) ... like when, on one of these 
muggy, earthy-smelling days, when everything's melancholy, the wind 
freshen up sudden and you breathe again. and that (i'm try to take 
it in order, you see, so that it will be plain to you) bring me to the 
time i found out that _he_ did that too, and knew when he was do it. 

i'd gone into his place one night to have a look at his statue. it was 
surprising what a lot i was finding out about that statue. it was still 
all out of proportion (that is to say, i knew it must be--remembered i'd 
thought so--though it didn't annoy me now quite so much. i suppose i'd 
lost _my_ fresh eye by that time). somehow, too, my own miniature had 
begun to look a bit kiddish; they made me impatient; and that's horrible, 
to be discontented with things that once seem jolly good to you. 

well, he'd been look at me in the hungriest sort of way, and i look 
at the statue, when all at once that feeling of release and lightness 
came over me. the first i knew of it was that i found myself thinking of 
some rather important letters my firm had written to me, wanting to know 
when a job i was do was go to be finished. i thought myself it was 
time i got it finished; i thought i'd better set about it at once; and i 
sat sudden up in my chair, as if i'd just come out of a sleep. and, 
look at the statue, i saw it as it had seem at first--all misshapen 
and out of drawing. 

the very next moment, as i was rising, i sat down again as sudden as if 
somebody had pull me back. 

now a chap doesn't like to be change about like that; so, without 
look at benlian, i mutter a bit testily, "don't, benlian!" 

then i heard him get up and knock his chair away. he was standing behind 
me. 

"pudgie," he said, in a move sort of voice, "i'm no good to you. get out 
of this. get out--" 

"no, no, benlian!" i pleaded. 

"get out, do you hear, and don't come again! go and live somewhere 
else--go away from london--don't let me know where you go--" 

"oh, what have i done?" i ask unhappily; and he was mutter again. 

"perhaps it would be better for me too," he muttered; and then he added, 
"come, bundle out!" 

so in home i went, and finished my ivory for the firm; but i can't tell 
you how friendless and unhappy i felt. 

now i use to know in those days a little girl--a nice, warm-hearted 
little thing, just friendly you know, who use to come to me sometimes in 
another place i live at and mend for me and so on. it was an awful long 
time since i'd seen her; but she found me out one night--came to that 
yard, walk straight in, went straight to my linen-bag, and began to 
look over my things to see what want mending, just as she use to. i 
don't mind confess that i was a bit sweet on her at one time; and it 
made me feel awfully mean, the way she came in, without ask any 
questions, and took up my mending. 

so she sat do my things, and i sat at my work, glad of a bit of 
company; and she chat as she worked, just jolly and gentle and not at 
all reproach me. 

but as sudden as a shot, right in the middle of it all, i found myself 
wonder about benlian again. and i wasn't only wondering; somehow i was 
horrible uneasy about him. it came to me that he might be ill or 
something. and all the fun of her have come to see me was gone. i found 
myself do all sort of stupid things to my work, and glancing at my 
watch that was lie on the table before me. 

at last i couldn't stand it any longer. i got up. 

"daisy," i said, "i've got to go out now." 

she seem surprised. 

"oh, why didn't you tell me i'd been keeping you!" she said, get up 
at once. 

i mutter that i was awfully sorry.... 

i packed her off. i closed the door in the hoarding behind her. then i 
walk straight across the yard to benlian's. 

he was lie on a couch, not do anything. 

"i know i ought to have come sooner, benlian," i said, "but i had 
somebody with me." 

"yes," he said, look hard at me; and i got a bit red. 

"she's awfully nice," i stammered; "but you never bother with girls, and 
you don't drink or smoke--" 

"no," he said. 

"well," i continued, "you ought to have a little relaxation; you're 
knock yourself up." and, indeed, he look awfully ill. 

but he shook his head. 

"a man's only a definite amount of force in him, pudgie," he said, "and 
if he spend it in one way he go short in another. mine goes--there." 
he glance at the statue. "i rarely sleep now," he added. 

"then you ought to see a doctor," i said, a bit alarmed. (i'd felt sure 
he was ill.) 

"no, no, pudgie. my force is all go there--all but the minimum that 
can't be helped, you know.... you've heard artist talk about 'putting 
their soul into their work,' pudgie?" 

"don't rub it in about my rotten miniatures, benlian," i ask him. 

"you've heard them say that; but they're charlatans, professional 
artists, all, pudgie. they haven't got any soul big than a sixpence 
to put into it.... you know, pudgie, that force and matter are the same 
thing--that it's decided nowadays that you can't define matter otherwise 
than as 'a point of force'?" 

"yes," i found myself saying eagerly, as if i'd heard it dozen of times 
before. 

"so that if they could put their soul into it, it would be just as easy 
for them to put their _bodies_ into it?..." 

i had drawn very close to him, and again--it was not fancy--i felt as if 
somebody, not me, was use my mouth. a flash of comprehension seem to 
come into my brain. 

"_not that, benlian_?" i cry breathlessly. 

he nod three or four times, and whispered. i really don't know why we 
both whispered. 

"_really that, benlian_?" i whisper again. 

"shall i show you?... i try my hardest not to, you know,..." he still 
whispered. 

"yes, show me!" i reply in a suppress voice. 

"don't breathe a sound then! i keep them up there...." 

he put his finger to his lip as if we had been two conspirators; then he 
tiptoe across the studio and went up to his bedroom in the gallery. 
presently he tiptoe down again, with some rolled-up papers in his hand. 
they were photographs, and we stoop together over a little table. his 
hand shook with excitement. 

"you remember this?" he whispered, showing me a rough print. 

it was one of the print from the fog plate that i'd taken after that 
first night. 

"come close to me if you feel frightened, pudgie," he said. "you said 
they were old plates, pudgie. no no; the plate were all right; it's _i_ 
who am wrong!" 

"of course," i said. it seem so natural. 

"this one," he said, taking up one that was number "1," "is a plain 
photograph, in the flesh, before it started; _you_ know! now look at 
this, and this--" 

he spread them before me, all in order. 

"2" was a little fogged, as if a novice had taken it; on "3" a sort of 
cloudy veil partly obliterate the face; "4" was still further smudge 
and lost; and "5" was a figure with glove hand held up, as a man hold 
his hand up when he is cover by a gun. the face of this one was 
completely blot out. 

and it didn't seem in the least horrible to me, for i kept on murmuring, 
"of course, of course." 

then benlian rub his hand and smile at me. "i'm making good 
progress, am i not?" he said. 

"splendid!" i breathed. 

"better than you know, too," he chuckled, "for you're not properly under 
yet. but you will be, pudgie, you will be--" 

"yes, yes!... will it be long, benlian?" 

"no," he replied, "not if i can keep from eat and sleep and 
thinking of other things than the statue--and if you don't disturb me by 
have girl about the place, pudgie." 

"i'm awfully sorry," i said contritely. 

"all right, all right; ssh!... this, you know, pudgie, is my own studio; 
i bought it; i bought it purposely to make my statue, my god. i'm passing 
nicely into it; and when i'm quite passed--_quite_ passed, pudgie--you 
can have the key and come in when you like." 

"oh, thanks awfully," i murmur gratefully. 

he nudge me. 

"what would they think of it, pudgie--those of the exhibition and 
academies, who say 'their soul are in their work'? what would the 
cackle think of it, pudgie?" 

"aren't they fools!" i chuckled. 

"and i shall have _one_ worshipper, shan't i, pudgie?" 

"rather!" i replied. "isn't it splendid!... oh, need i go back just yet?" 

"yes, you must go now; but i'll send for you again very soon.... you know 
i try to do without you, pudge; i try for thirteen days, and it 
nearly kill me! that's past. i shan't try again. now off you trot, my 
pudgie--" 

i wink at him knowingly, and came skip and dance across the yard. 


iii 

it's just silly--that's what it is--to say that something of a man 
doesn't go into his work. 

why, even those wretched little ivory of mine, the thick-headed fellow 
who paid for them knew my touch in them, and once spotted it instantly 
when i try to slip in another chap's who was hard up. benlian use to 
say that a man went about spread himself over everything he came in 
contact with--diffusing some sort of influence (as far as i could make it 
out); and the mistake was, he said, that we went through the world just 
waste it instead of direct it. and if benlian didn't understand all 
about those things, i should jolly well like to know who does! a chap 
with a great abound will and brain like him, it's only natural he 
should be able to pass himself on, to a statue or anything else, when he 
really tried--did without food and talk and sleep in order to save 
himself up for it! 

"a man can't both _do_ and _be_," i remember he said to me once. "he's so 
much force, no more, and he can either make himself with it or something 
else. if he try to do both, he do both imperfectly. i'm go to do 
_one_ perfect thing." oh, he was a queer chap! fancy, a fellow making a 
thing like that statue, out of himself, and then wanting somebody to 
adore him! 

and i hadn't the faintest conception of how much i did adore him till 
yet again, as he had done before, he seem to--you know--to take 
himself away from me again, leave me all alone, and so wretched!... and 
i was angry at the same time, for he'd promise me he wouldn't do it 
again.... (this was one night, i don't remember when.) 

i ran to my landing and shout down into the yard. 

"benlian! benlian!" 

there was a light in his studio, and i heard a muffle shout come back. 

"keep away--keep away--keep away!" 

he was struggling--i knew he was struggle as i stood there on my 
landing--struggling to let me go. and i could only run and throw myself 
on my bed and sob, while he try to set me free, who didn't want to be 
set free ... he was have a terrific struggle, all alone there.... 

(he told me afterwards that he _had_ to eat something now and then and to 
sleep a little, and that weaken him--strengthened him--strengthened 
his body and weaken the passing, you know.) 

but the next day it was all right again. i was benlian's again. and i 
wondered, when i remember his struggle, whether a die man had ever 
fought for life as hard as benlian was fight to get away from it and 
pass himself. 

the next time after that that he fetch me--called me--whatever you like 
to name it--i burst into his studio like a bullet. he was sunk in a big 
chair, gaunt as a mummy now, and all the life in him seem to burn in 
the bottom of his deep eye-sockets. at the sight of him i fiddle with 
my knuckle and giggled. 

"you _are_ go it, benlian!" i said. 

"am i not?" he replied, in a voice that was scarcely a breath. 

"you _meant_ me to bring the camera and magnesium, didn't you?" (i had 
snatch them up when i felt his call, and had brought them.) 

"yes. go ahead." 

so i placed the camera before him, made all ready, and took the magnesium 
ribbon in a pair of pincers. 

"are you ready?" i said; and light the ribbon. 

the studio seem to leap with the blind glare. the ribbon spat and 
spluttered. i snap the shutter, and the fumes drift away and hung 
in cloud in the roof. 

"you'll have to walk me about soon, pudgie, and bang me with bladders, as 
they do the opium-patients," he said sleepily. 

"let me take one of the statue now," i said eagerly. 

but he put up his hand. 

"no, no. _that's_ too much like test our god. faith's the food they 
feed gods on, pudgie. we'll let the s.p.r. people photograph it when it's 
all over," he said. "now get it developed." 

i develop the plate. the obliterate now seem complete. 

but benlian seem dissatisfied. 

"there's something wrong somewhere," he said. "it isn't so perfect as 
that yet--i can feel within me it isn't. it's merely that your camera 
isn't strong enough to find me, pudgie." 

"i'll get another in the morning," i cried. 

"no," he answered. "i know something better than that. have a cab here by 
ten o'clock in the morning, and we'll go somewhere." 

by half-past ten the next morning we had driven to a large hospital, and 
had gone down a lot of steps and along corridor to a basement room. 
there was a stretcher couch in the middle of the room, and all manner of 
queer appliances, frames of ground glass, tube of glass blown into 
extraordinary shapes, a dynamo, and a lot of other things all about. a 
couple of doctor were there too, and benlian was talk to them. 

"we'll try my hand first," benlian said by-and-by. 

he advanced to the couch, and put his hand under one of the frames of 
ground glass. one of the doctor did something in a corner. a harsh 
crackling fill the room, and an unearthly, fluorescent light shot and 
flood across the frame where benlian's hand was. the two doctor 
looked, and then start back. one of them gave a cry. he was sickly 
white. 

"put me on the couch," said benlian. 

i and the doctor who was not ill lift him on the canvas stretcher. the 
green-gleaming frame of fluctuate light was pass over the whole of 
his body. then the doctor ran to a telephone and call a colleague.... 

we spent the morning there, with dozen of doctor coming and going. then 
we left. all the way home in the cab benlian chuckle to himself. 

"that scared 'em, pudgie!" he chuckled. "a man they can't x-ray--that 
scared 'em! we must put that down in the diary--" 

"wasn't it ripping!" i chuckle back. 

he kept a sort of diary or record. he gave it to me afterwards, but 
they've borrow it. it was as big as a ledger, and immensely valuable, 
i'm sure; they oughtn't to borrow valuable things like that and not 
return them. the laugh that benlian and i have had over that diary! 
it fool them all--the clever x-ray men, the artist of the academies, 
everybody! written on the fly-leaf was "_to my pudgie_." i shall publish 
it when i get it back again. 

benlian had now got frightfully weak; it's awfully hard work, passing 
yourself. and he had to take a little milk now and then or he'd have 
die before he had quite finished. i didn't bother with miniature any 
longer, and when angry letters came from my employer we just put them 
into the fire, benlian and i, and we laughed--that is to say, i laughed, 
but benlian only smiled, being too weak to laugh really. he'd lot of 
money, so that was all right; and i slept in his studio, to be there for 
the passing. 

and that wouldn't be very long now, i thought; and i was always look 
at the statue. things like that (in case you don't know) have to be done 
gradually, and i supposed he was busy filling up the inside of it and 
hadn't got to the outside yet--for the statue was much the same to look 
at. but, reckoning off his sip of milk and snatch of sleep, he was 
making splendid progress, and the figure must be get very full now. 
i was awfully excited, it was get so near.... 

and then somebody came bother and nearly spoil all. it's odd, but i 
really forget exactly what it was. i only know there was a funeral, and 
people were sob and look at me, and somebody said i was callous, 
but somebody else said, "no, look at him," and that it was just the other 
way about. and i think i remember, now, that it wasn't in london, for i 
was in a train; but after the funeral i dodge them, and found myself 
back at euston again. they follow me, but i shook them off. i lock my 
own studio up, and lay as quiet as a mouse in benlian's place when they 
came hammer at the door.... 

* * * * * 

and now i must come to what you'll call the finish--though it's awfully 
stupid to call things like that "finishes." 

i'd slip into my own studio one night--i forget what for; and i'd gone 
quietly, for i knew they were following me, those people, and would catch 
me if they could. it was a thick, misty night, and the light came 
stream up through benlian's roof window, with the shadow of the 
window-divisions lose themselves like dark ray in the fog. a lot of 
hoot was go on down the river, steamer and barges.... oh, i know 
what i'd come into my studio for! it was for those negatives. benlian 
want them for the diary, so that it could be seen there wasn't any 
fake about the prints. for he'd said he would make a final spurt that 
evening and get the job finished. it had taken a long time, but i'll bet 
_you_ couldn't have pass _yourself_ any quicker. 

when i got back he was sitting in the chair he'd hardly left for weeks, 
and the diary was on the table by his side. i'd taken all the scaffolding 
down from the statue, and he was ready to begin. he had to waste one last 
bit of strength to explain to me, but i drew as close as i could, so that 
he wouldn't lose much. 

"now, pudgie," i just heard him say, "you've behave splendidly, and 
you'll be quite still up to the finish, won't you?" 

i nodded. 

"and you mustn't expect the statue to come down and walk about, 
or anything like that," he continued. "_those_ aren't the really 
wonderful things. and no doubt people will tell you it hasn't changed; 
but you'll know better! it's much more wonderful that i should be there 
than that they should be able to prove it, isn't it?... and, of course, 
i don't know exactly how it will happen, for i've never done this 
before.... you have the letter for the s.p.r.? they can photograph it if 
they want.... by the way, you don't think the same of my statue as you 
did at first, do you?" 

"oh, it's wonderful!" i breathed. 

"and even if, like the god of the others, it doesn't vouchsafe a special 
sign and wonder, it's benlian, for all that?" 

"oh, do be quick, benlian! i can't bear another minute!" 

then, for the last time, he turn his great eaten-out eyes on me. 

"_i seal you mine, pudgie_!" he said. 

then his eyes fasten themselves on the statue. 

i wait for a quarter of an hour, scarcely breathing. benlian's breath 
came in little flutters, many seconds apart. he had a little clock on the 
table. twenty minutes passed, and half an hour. i was a little 
disappointed, really, that the statue wasn't go to move; but benlian 
knew best, and it was filling quiet up with him instead. then i thought 
of those zigzag bunch of lightning they draw on the electric-belt 
advertisements, and i was rather glad after all that the statue _wasn't_ 
go to move. it would have been a little cheap, that ... vulgar, in a 
sense.... he was breathing a little more sharp now, as if in pain, but 
his eyes never moved. a dog was howling somewhere, and i hope that the 
hoot of the tug wouldn't disturb benlian.... 

nearly an hour had pass when, all of a sudden, i pushed my chair 
farther away and cower back, gnawing my fingers, very frightened. 
benlian had sudden moved. he'd set himself forward in his chair, and he 
seem to be strangling. his mouth was wide open, and he began to make 
long harsh "_aaaaah-aaaah's_!" i shouldn't have thought passing yourself 
was such agony.... 

and then i gave a scream--for he seem to be thrust himself back in 
his chair again, as if he'd change his mind and didn't want to pass 
himself at all. but just you ask anybody: when you get yourself just over 
half-way passed, the other's drag out of you, and you can't help 
yourself. his "_aaaaahs_!" became so loud and horrid that i shut my eyes 
and stop my ears.... minutes that lasted; and then there came a high 
din that i couldn't shut out, and all at once the floor shook with a 
heavy thump. when all was still again i open my eyes. 

his chair had overturned, and he lay in a heap beside it. 

i call "benlian!" but he didn't answer.... 

he'd pass beautifully; quite dead. i look up at the statue. it was 
just as benlian had said--it didn't open its eyes, nor speak, nor 
anything like that. don't you believe chaps who tell you that statue 
that have been pass into do that; they don't. 

but instead, in a blaze and flash and shock, i knew now for the first 
time what a glorious thing that statue was! have you ever seen anything 
for the first time like that? if you have, you never see very much 
afterwards, you know. the rest's all piffle after that. it was like 
coming out of fog and dark into a split in the open heavens, my 
statue was so transfigured; and i'll bet if you'd been there you'd have 
clap your hands, as i did, and chuck the tablecloth over the benlian 
on the floor till they should come to cart that empty shell away, and 
pat the statue's foot and cried: "_is it all right, benlian_?" 

i did this; and then i rush excited out into the street, to call 
somebody to see how glorious it was.... 

* * * * * 

they've brought me here for a holiday, and i'm to go back to the studio 
in two or three days. but they've said that before, and i think it's 
caddish of fellow not to keep their word--and not to return a valuable 
diary too! but there isn't a peephole in my room, as there is in some of 
them (the emperor of brazil told me that); and benlian know i haven't 
forsaken him, for they take me a message every day to the studio, and 
benlian always answer that it's "_all right_, and i'm to stay where i am 
for a bit." so as long as he knows, i don't mind so much. but it is a bit 
rotten hanging on here, especially when the doctor themselves admit how 
reasonable it all is.... still, if benlian say it's "_all right_ ..."
