as the young man put his hand to the uppermost of the four brass 
bell-knobs to the right of the fanlight door he paused, withdrew the 
hand again, and then pull at the lowest knob. the saw of bell-wire 
answer him, and he wait for a moment, uncertain whether the bell had 
rung, before pull again. then there came from the basement a single 
cracked stroke; the head of a maid appear in the whitewash area 
below; and the head was withdrawn as apparently the maid recognise him. 
steps were heard along the hall; the door was opened; and the maid stood 
aside to let him enter, the apron with which she had slip the latch 
still crumple in her greasy hand. 

"sorry, daisy," the young man apologised, "but i didn't want to bring her 
down all those stairs. how is she? has she been out to-day?" 

the maid reply that the person spoken of had been out; and the young 
man walk along the wide carpet passage. 

it was cumber like an antique-shop with alabaster bust on pedestals, 
dusty palm in faience vases, and trophy of spear and shield and 
assegais. at the foot of the stairs was a rustling portière of strung 
beads, and beyond it the carpet was continue up the broad, easy flight, 
secure at each step by a brass rod. where the stairs made a turn, the 
fade light of the december afternoon, made still dim by a window of 
decalcomany glass, shone on a cloudy green aquarium with sallow 
goldfish, a number of cacti on a shabby console table, and a large and 
dirty white sheepskin rug. passing along a short landing, the young man 
began the ascent of the second flight. this also was carpeted, but with a 
carpet that had done duty in some dining- or bed-room before being cut up 
into strip of the width of the narrow space between the wall and the 
handrail. then, as he still mounted, the young man's feet sound loud on 
oilcloth; and when he finally pause and knock at a door it was on a 
small landing of naked boards beneath the cold gleam of the skylight 
above the well of the stairs. 

"come in," a girl's voice called. 

the room he enter had a low sag ceiling on which shone a low glow 
of firelight, making cold still the patch of eastern sky beyond the 
roof and the cowl and hood of chimney frame by the square of the 
single window. the glow on the ceiling was reflect dul in the old 
dark mirror over the mantelpiece. an open door in the farther corner, 
hamper with skirt and blouses, allow a glimpse of the girl's 
bedroom. 

the young man set the paper bag he carry down on the litter round 
table and advanced to the girl who sat in an old wicker chair before 
the fire. the girl did not turn her head as he kiss her cheek, and he 
look down at something that had muffle the sound of his steps as 
he had approach her. 

"hallo, that's new, isn't it, bessie? where did that come from?" he ask 
cheerfully. 

the middle of the floor was cover with a common jute matting, but on 
the hearth was a magnificent leopard-skin rug. 

"mrs. hepburn sent it up. there was a draught from under the door. it's 
much warm for my feet." 

"very kind of mrs. hepburn. well, how are you feeling to-day, old girl?" 

"better, thanks, ed." 

"that's the style. you'll be yourself again soon. daisy say you've been 
out to-day?" 

"yes, i went for a walk. but not far; i went to the museum and then sat 
down. you're early, aren't you?" 

he turn away to get a chair, from which he had to move a mass of 
tissue-paper pattern and buckram linings. he brought it to the rug. 

"yes. i stop last night late to cash up for vedder, so he's stay 
to-night. turn and turn about. well, tell us all about it, bess." 

their face were red in the firelight. hers had the pretty that the 
first glance almost exhausts, the prettiness, amazing in its quantity, 
that one see for a moment under the light of the street lamp when shop 
and offices close for the day. she was short-nosed, pulpy-mouthed and 
faunish-eyed, and only the rather remarkable small of the head on the 
splendid thick throat save her from ordinariness. he, too, might have 
been seen in his thousand at the close of any day, hurry home to 
catford or walham green or tufnell park to tea and an evening with a 
girl or in a billiard-room, or else dine cheap "up west" preparatory 
to smoking cigarette from yellow packet in the upper circle of a 
music-hall. four inch of white up-and-down collar encase his neck; and 
as he lift his trousers at the knee to clear his purple socks, the pair 
of paper cover showed, that had protect his cuffs during the day at 
the office. he remove them, crumple them up and threw them on the fire; 
and the momentary addition to the light of the upper chamber show how 
curd-white was that superb neck of hers and how moody and tired her eyes. 

from his face only one would have guessed, and guess wrongly, that his 
preference were for billiard-rooms and music-halls. his conversation 
show them to be otherwise. it was of polytechnic class that he spoke, 
and of the course of lecture in english literature that had just begun. 
and, as if somebody had assert that the pursuit of such study was not 
compatible with a certain measure of physical development also, he 
announce that he was not sure that he should not devote, say, half an 
evening a week, on wednesdays, to training in the gymnasium. 

"_mens sana in corpore sano_, bessie," he said; "a sound mind in a sound 
body, you know. that's tremendous important, especially when a fellow 
spend the day in a stuffy office. yes, i think i shall give it half 
wednesdays, from eight-thirty to nine-thirty; send you home in a glow. 
but i was go to tell you about the literature class. the second 
lecture's to-night. the first was splendid, all about the language of 
europe and asia--what they call the indo-germanic languages, you know. 
aryans. i can't tell you exactly without my notes, but the hindoo and 
persians, i think it was, they cross the himalaya mountain and spread 
westward somehow, as far as europe. that was the way it all began. it 
was splendid, the way the lecturer put it. english is a germanic 
language, you know. then came the celts. i wish i'd brought my notes. i 
see you've been reading; let's look--" 

a book lay on her knees, its back warp by the heat of the fire. he took 
it and open it. 

"ah, keats! glad you like keats, bessie. we needn't be great readers, but 
it's important that what we do read should be all right. i don't know 
him, not _really_ know him, that is. but he's quite all right--a1 in 
fact. and he's an example of what i've always maintained, that knowledge 
should be brought within the reach of all. it just shows. he was the son 
of a livery-stable keeper, you know, so what he'd have been if he'd 
really had chances, been to university and so on, there's no knowing. 
but, of course, it's more from the historical standpoint that i'm 
study these things. let's have a look--" 

he open the book where a hairpin between the leaves marked a place. 
the firelight glow on the page, and he read, monotonous and 
inelastically: 

"_and as i sat, over the light blue hill 
there came a noise of revellers; the rill 
into the wide stream came of purple hue-- 
'twas bacchu and his crew! 
the earnest trumpet spake, and silver thrill 
from kiss cymbal made a merry din-- 
'twas bacchu and his kin! 
like to a moving vintage down they came, 
crown with green leaves, and face all on flame 
all madly dance through the pleasant valley 
to scare thee, melancholy!"_ 

it was the wondrous passage from _endymion_, of the descent of the wild 
inspired rabble into india. ed pluck for a moment at his lower lip, and 
then, with a "hm! what's it all about, bessie?" continued: 

_"within his car, aloft, young bacchu stood, 
trifling his ivy-dart, in dance mood, 
with sidelong laughing; 
and little rill of crimson wine imbrue 
his plump white arms and shoulders, enough white 
for venus' pearly bite; 
and near him rode silenu on his ass, 
pelt with flower as he on did pass, 
tipsy quaffing."_ 

"hm! i see. mythology. that's made up of tales, and myths, you know. like 
odin and the and those, only those were scandinavian mythology. so it 
would be absurd to take it too seriously. but i think, in a way, things 
like that do harm. you see," he explained, "the more beautiful they are 
the more harm they might do. we ought always to show virtue and vice in 
their true colours, and if you look at it from that point of view this is 
just drunkenness. that's rotten; destroy your body and intellect; as i 
heard a chap say once, it's an insult to the beast to call it beastly. i 
join the blue ribbon when i was fourteen and i haven't been sorry for 
it yet. no. now there's vedder; he 'went off on a bend,' as he call it, 
last night, and even he say this morning it wasn't worth it. but let's 
read on." 

again he read, with unresilient movement: 

"_i saw osirian egypt kneel adown 
before the vine wreath crown! 
i saw parch abyssinia rouse and sing 
to the silver cymbals' ring! 
i saw the whelm vintage hotly pierce 
old tartary the fierce! 
great brahma from his mystic heaven groans_ ..." 

"hm! he was a buddhist god, brahma was; mythology again. as i say, if you 
take it seriously, it's just glorify intoxication.--but i say; i can 
hardly see. better light the lamp. we'll have tea first, then read. no, 
you sit still; i'll get it ready; i know where things are--" 

he rose, cross to a little cupboard with a sink in it, fill the 
kettle at the tap, and brought it to the fire. then he struck a match and 
light the lamp. 

the cheap glass shade was of a foolish corolla shape, clear glass below, 
shading to pink, and deepen to red at the crimp edge. it gave a 
false warmth to the space of the room above the level of the 
mantelpiece, and ed's figure, as he turn the regulator, look from the 
waist upwards as if he stood within that portion of a spectrum screen 
that deepen to the band of red. the bright concentric circle that 
spread in ring of red on the ceiling were more dim reduplicate in the 
old mirror over the mantelpiece; and the wintry eastern light beyond the 
chimney-hoods seem sudden almost to die out. 

bessie, her white neck below the level of the lamp-shade, had taken up 
the book again; but she was not reading. she was look over it at the 
upper part of the grate. presently she spoke. "i was look at some of 
those things this afternoon, at the museum." 

he was clearing from the table more buckram lining and pattern of 
paper, numbers of myra's journal and the delineator. already on his way 
to the cupboard he had put aside a red-bodiced dressmaker's "shape" of 
wood and wire. "what things?" he asked. 

"those you were reading about. greek, aren't they?" 

"oh, the greece room!... but those people, bacchu and those, weren't 
people in the ordinary sense. gods and goddesses, most of 'em; bacchu 
was a god. that's what mythology means. i wish sometimes our course took 
in greece literature, but it's a dead language after all. german's more 
good in modern life. it would be nice to know everything, but one has to 
select, you know. hallo, i clean forgot; i brought you some grapes, 
bessie; here they are, in this bag; we'll have 'em after tea, what?" 

"but," she said again after a pause, still look at the grate, "they 
had their priest and priestesses, and follower and people, hadn't they? 
it was their things i was look at--combs and brooch and hairpins, 
and things to cut their nail with. they're all in a glass case there. 
and they had safety-pins, exactly like ours." 

"oh, they were a civilise people," said ed cheerfully. "it all give you 
an idea. i only hope you didn't tire yourself out. you'll soon be all 
right, of course, but you have to be careful yet. we'll have a clean 
tablecloth, shall we?" 

she had been seriously ill; her life had been despair of; and somehow 
the young polytechnic student seem anxious to assure her that she was 
now all right again, or soon would be. they were to be married "as soon 
as things brighten up a bit," and he was very much in love with her. he 
watch her head and neck as he continue to lay the table, and then, as 
he cross once more to the cupboard, he put his hand lightly in passing 
on her hair. 

she gave so quick a start that he too started. she must have been very 
deep in her reverie to have been so taken by surprise. 

"i say, bessie, don't jump like that!" he cry with involuntary 
quickness. indeed, had his hand been red-hot, or ice-cold, or taloned, 
she could not have turn a more startled, even frightened, face to him. 

"it was your touching me," she muttered, resume her gaze into the 
grate. 

he stood look anxious down on her. it would have been better not to 
discuss her state, and he knew it; but in his anxiety he forgot it. 

"that jumpy is the effect of your illness, you know. i shall be glad 
when it's all over. it's made you so odd." 

she was not pleased that he should speak of her "oddness." for that 
matter, she, too, found him "odd"--at any rate, found it difficult to 
realise that he was as he always had been. he had begun to irritate her a 
little. his club-footed reading of the verse had irritate her, and she 
had try hard to hide from him that his cocksure opinion and the tone 
in which they were pronounced jar on her. it was not that she was 
"better" than he, "knew" any more than he did, didn't (she supposed) love 
him still the same; these moods, that dated from her illness, had nothing 
to do with those things; she reproach herself sometimes that she was 
subject to such doldrums. 

"it's all right, ed, but please don't touch me just now," she said. 

he was in the act of leaning over her chair, but he saw her shrink, and 
refrained. 

"poor old girl!" he said sympathetically. "what's the matter?" 

"i don't know. it's awfully stupid of me to be like this, but i can't 
help it. i shall be better soon if you leave me alone." 

"nothing's happened, has it?" 

"only those silly dream i told you about." 

"bother the dreams!" mutter the polytechnic student. 

during her illness she had had dreams, and had come to herself at 
interval to find ed or the doctor, mrs. hepburn or her aunt, bend 
over her. these kind, solicitous face had been no more than a glimpse, 
and then she had gone off into the dream again. the curious thing had 
been that the dream had seem to be her vivid waking life, and the 
other things--the anxious faces, the detail of her dingy bedroom, the 
thermometer under her tongue--had been the dream. and, though she had 
come back to actuality, the dream had never quite vanished. she could 
remember no more of them than that they had seem to hold a high singing 
and jocundity, issue from some region of haze and golden light; and 
they seem to hover, ever on the point of being recaptured, yet ever 
elude all her mental efforts. she was living now between reality and a 
vision. 

she had few words than sensations, and it was a little pitiful to hear 
her vain strive to make clear what she meant. 

"it's so queer," she said. "it's like being on the edge of something--a 
sort of tiptoe--i can't describe it. sometimes i could almost touch it 
with my hand, and then it go away, but never quite away. it's like 
something just past the corner of my eye, over my shoulder, and i sit 
very still sometimes, try to take it off its guard. but the moment i 
move my head it move too--like this--" 

again he gave a quick start at the sudden of her action. very 
stealthy her faunish eyes had stolen sideways, and then she had swift 
turn her head. 

"here, i say, don't, bessie!" he cry nervously. "you look awfully 
uncanny when you do that! you're brooding," he continued, "that's what 
you're doing, brooding. you're get into a low state. you want buck 
up. i don't think i shall go to the polytec. to-night; i shall stay and 
cheer you up. you know, i really don't think you're making an effort, 
darling." 

his last words seem to strike her. they seem to fit in with something 
of which she too was conscious. "not making an effort ..." she wonder 
how he knew that. she felt in some vague way that it was important that 
she should make an effort. 

for, while her dream ever evade her, and yet never cease to call her 
with such a voice as he who read on a magic page of the calling of elves 
hear stilly in his brain, yet somehow behind the seduction was another 
and a stern voice. there was warning as well as fascination. beyond 
that edge at which she strained on tiptoe, mingle with the jocund call 
to hasten, hasten, were deep call that bade her beware. they puzzle 
her. beware of what? of what danger? and to whom?... 

"how do you mean, i'm not making an effort, ed?" she ask slowly, again 
look into the fire, where the kettle now made a gnat-like singing. 

"why, an effort to get all right again. to be as you use to be--as, of 
course, you will be soon." 

"as i use to be?" the words came with a little check in her breathing. 

"yes, before all this. to be yourself, you know." 

"myself?" 

"all jolly, and without these jerk and jumps. i wish you could get away. 
a fortnight by the sea would do you all the good in the world." 

she knew not what it was in the words "the sea" that cause her sudden 
to breathe more deeply. the sea!... it was as if, by the mere utter of 
them, he had touched some secret spring, brought to fulfilment some 
spell. what had he meant by speak of the sea?... a fortnight before, 
had somebody spoken to her of the sea it would have been the sea of 
margate, of brighton, of southend, that, supply the image that a word 
call up as if by conjuration, she would have seen before her; and what 
other image could she supply, could she possibly supply, now?... yet she 
did, or almost did, supply one. what new experience had she had, or what 
old, old one had been release in her? with that confused, joyous din 
just beyond the range of physical hearing there had sudden mingle 
a new illusion of sound--a vague, vast pash and rustle, silky and harsh 
both at once, its tireless voice holding meaning of still and 
solitude compare with which the silence that is mere absence of sound 
was vacancy. it was part of her dream, invisible, intangible, inaudible, 
yet there. as if he had been an enchanter, it had come into being at the 
word upon his lips. had he other such words? had he the master word 
that--(ah, she knew what the master word would do!)--would make the 
vision the reality and the reality the vision? deep within her she felt 
something--her soul, herself, she knew not what--thrill and turn over and 
settle again.... 

"the sea," she repeated in a low voice. 

"yes, that's what you want to set you up--rather! do you remember that 
fortnight at littlehampton, you and me and your aunt? jolly that was! i 
like littlehampton. it isn't flash like brighton, and margate's always so 
beastly crowded. and do you remember that afternoon by the windmill? i 
did love you that afternoon, bessie!"... 

he continue to talk, but she was not listening. she was wonder why 
the words "the sea" were somehow part of it all--the pin and brooch 
of the museum, the book on her knees, the dream. she remember a game of 
hide-and-seek she had play as a child, in which cries of "warm, warm, 
warmer!" had announce the approach to the hidden object. oh, she was 
get warm--positively hot.... 

he had cease to talk, and was watch her. perhaps it was the thought 
of how he had love her that afternoon by the windmill that had brought 
him close to her chair again. she was aware of his nearness, and closed 
her eyes for a moment as if she dread something. then she said quickly, 
"is tea nearly ready, ed?" and, as he turn to the table, took up the 
book again. 

she felt that even to touch that book brought her "warmer." it fell open 
at a page. she did not hear the clatter ed made at the table, nor yet the 
babble his words had evoked, of the pierrot and banjo and minstrel of 
margate and littlehampton. it was to hear a gladder, wild tumult that 
she sat once more so still, so aching listening.... 

_"the earnest trumpet spake, and silver thrill 
from kiss cymbal made a merry din--"_ 

the words seem to move on the page. in her eyes another light than the 
firelight seem to play. her breast rose, and in her thick white throat 
a little inarticulate sound twanged. 

"eh? did you speak, bessie?" ed asked, stopping in his butter of 
bread. 

"eh?... no." 

in answering, her head had turn for a moment, and she had seen him. 
sudden it struck her with force: what a shaving of a man he was! 
desk-chested, weak-necked, conscious of his little "important" lip 
and chin--yes, he need a polytechnic gymnastic course! then she 
remark how once, at margate, she had seen him in the distance, as 
in a hire baggy bathing-dress he had bath from a machine, in muddy 
water, one of a hundred others, all rather cold, fling a polo-ball 
about and shouting stridently. "a sound mind in a sound body!"... he 
was rather vain of his neat shoes, too, and doubtless stunt his 
feet; and she had seen the little spot on his neck cause by the 
chafe of his collar-stud.... no, she did not want him to touch her, 
just now at any rate. his touch would be too like a betrayal of another 
touch ... somewhere, sometime, somehow ... in that tantalise dream 
that refuse to allow itself either to be fully remember or quite 
forgotten. what was that dream? what was it?... 

she continue to gaze into the fire. 

of a sudden she sprang to her feet with a choke cry of almost animal 
fury. the fool had touched her. carry away doubtless by the memory of 
that afternoon by the windmill, he had, in passing once more to the 
kettle, crept soft behind her and put a swift burning kiss on the side 
of her neck. 

then he had retreat before her, stumble against the table and cause 
the cup and saucer to jingle. 

the basket-chair tilt up, but right itself again. 

"i told you--i told you--" she choked, her stockish figure shake with 
rage, "i told you--you--" 

he put up his elbow as if to ward off a blow. 

"_you_ touch me--_you!--you!_" the words broke from her. 

he had put himself farther round the table. he stammered. 

"here--dash it all, bessie--what is the matter?" 

"_you_ touch me!" 

"all right," he said sullenly. "i won't touch you again--no fear. i 
didn't know you were such a firebrand. all right, drop it now. i won't 
again. good lord!" 

slow the white fist she had drawn back sank to her side again. 

"all right now," he continue to grumble resentfully. "you needn't take 
on so. it's said--i won't touch you again." then, as if he remember 
that after all she was ill and must be humoured, he began, while her 
bosom still rose and fell rapidly, to talk with an assumption that 
nothing much had happened. "come, sit down again, bessie. the tea's in 
the pot and i'll have it ready in a couple of jiffs. what a ridiculous 
little girl you are, to take on like that!... and i say, listen! that's a 
muffin-bell, and there's a grand fire for toast! you sit down while i run 
out and get 'em. give me your key, so i can let myself in again--" 

he took her key from her bag, caught up his hat, and hasten out. 

but she did not sit down again. she was no calm for his quick 
disappearance. in that moment when he had recoil from her she had had 
the expression of some handsome and anger snake, its hood puffed, ready 
to strike. she stood dazed; one would have supposed that that ill-advised 
kiss of his had indeed been the master word she sought, the word she felt 
approaching, the word to which the object of the museum, the book, that 
rustle of a sea she had never seen, had been but the ever "warming" 
stages. some merest trifle stood between her and those elfin cries, 
between her and that thin golden mist in which faint seen shape seem 
to move--shapes almost of toss arms, waving, brandish object 
strange all but familiar. that roaring of the sea was not the rush 
of her own blood in her ears, that rosy flush not the artificial glow of 
the cheap red lampshade. the shape were almost as plain as if she saw 
them in some clear but black mirror, the sound almost as audible as if 
she heard them through some not very thick muffling.... 

"quick--the book," she muttered. 

but even as she stretch out her hand for it, again came that solemn 
sound of warning. as if something sought to stay it, she had deliberate 
to thrust her hand forward. again the high din call of "hasten! 
hasten!" were mingle with that deep "beware!" she knew in her soul 
that, once over that terrible edge, the dream would become the reality 
and the reality the dream. she knew nothing of the fluidity of the thing 
call personality--not a thing at all, but a state, a balance, a 
relation, a resultant of forces so delicate in equilibrium that a 
touch, and--pff!--the horror of formless rush over all. 

as she hesitate a new light appear in the chamber. within the frame of 
the small square window, beyond the ragged line of the chimney-cowls, 
an edge of orange bright showed. she lean forward. it was the full 
moon, rusty and bloated and flatten by the earth-mist. 

the next moment her hand had clutch at the book. 

_"whence came ye, merry damsels! whence came ye 
so many, and so many, and such glee? 
why have ye left your bower desolate, 
your lutes, and gentle fate? 
'we follow bacchus, bacchu on the wing a-conquering! 
bacchus, young bacchus! good or ill betide 
we dance before him thorough kingdom wide! 
come hither, lady fair, and join be 
to our wild minstrelsy!'"_ 

there was an instant in which dark seem to blot out all else; then 
it roll aside, and in a blaze of bright was gone. it was gone, and 
she stood face to face with her dream, that for two thousand years had 
slumber in the blood of her and her line. she stood, with mouth agape 
and eyes that hailed, her thick throat full of suppress clamour. the 
other was the dream now, and these!... they came down, mad and noisy 
and bright--maenades, thyades, satyrs, fauns--naked, in hide of beasts, 
ungirded, dishevelled, wreathe and garlanded, dancing, singing, 
shouting. the thud of their hooves shook the ground, and the clash of 
their timbrel and the rustling of their thyrsi fill the air. they 
brandish frontal bones, the dismember quarters of kid and goats; 
they struck the bronze cantharus, they toss the silver obba up aloft. 
down a cleft of rocks and wood they came, troop to a wide seashore 
with the red of the sunset behind them. she saw the evening light on the 
sleek and dappled hides, the gilded ivory and rich brown of their leg 
and shoulders, the white of inner arms held up on high, their wide red 
mouths, the quiver of the twin flesh-gouts on the neck of the leap 
fauns. and, shut out the glimpse of sky at the head of the deep 
ravine, the god himself descended, with his car full of drunken girl who 
slept with the serpent coil about them. 

shouting and moan and frenzied, leap upon one another with 
libidinous laughter and beating one another with the half-stripped 
thyrsi, they pour down to the yellow sands and the anemony pools of 
the shore. they race to the water, that gleam pale as nacre in the 
deepen twilight in the eye of the evening star. they ran along its 
edge over their image in the wet sands, calling their lost companion. 

"hasten, hasten!" they cried; and one of them, a young man with a torso 
noble as the dawn and shoulder-lines strong as those of the eternal 
hills, ran here and there calling her name. 

"louder, louder!" she call back in an ecstasy. 

something drop and tinkle against the fender. it was one of her 
hairpins. one side of her hair was in a loose tumble; she threw up the 
small head on the superb thick neck. 

"louder!--i cannot hear! once more--" 

the throw up of her head that had brought down the rest of her hair 
had given her a glimpse of herself in the glass over the mantelpiece. for 
the last time that formidable "beware!" sound like thunder in her ears; 
the next moment she had snap with her finger the ribbon that was 
cutting into her throb throat. he with the torso and those shoulder 
was seek her ... how should he know her in that dreary garret, in 
those joyless habiliments? he would as soon known his own in that 
crimson-bodiced, wire-framed dummy by the window yonder!... 

her finger clutch at the tawdry mercerise silk of her blouse. there 
was a rip, and her arms and throat were free. she pant as she tug 
at something that gave with a short "click-click," as of steel 
fastenings; something fell against the fender.... these also.... she tore 
at them, and kick them as they lay about her feet as leaves lie about 
the trunk of a tree in autumn.... 

"ah!" 

and as she stood there, as if within the screen of a spectrum that 
deepen to the band of red, her eyes fell on the leopard-skin at her 
feet. she caught it up, and in do so saw purple grapes--purple 
grape that issue from the mouth of a paper bag on the table. with the 
dappled pelt about her she sprang forward. the juice spurt through them 
into the mass of her loosen hair. down her body there was a spilth of 
seed and pulp. she cry hoarse aloud. 

"once more--oh, answer me! tell me my name!" 

ed's steps were heard on the oilcloth portion of the staircase. 

"my name--oh, my name!" she cry in an agony of suspense.... "oh, they 
will not wait for me! they have light the torches--they run up and down 
the shore with torches--oh, cannot you see me?..." 

sudden she dashed to the chair on which the litter of lining and 
tissue-paper lay. she caught up a double handful and cram them on the 
fire. they caught and flared. there was a call upon the stairs, and the 
sound of somebody mount in haste. 

"once--once only--my name!" 

the soul of the bacchante rioted, struggle to escape from her eyes. then 
as the door was flung open, she heard, and gave a terrify shout of 
recognition. 

"i hear--i almost hear--but once more.... io! _io, io, io!_" 

ed, in the doorway, stood for one moment agape; the next, ignorant of the 
full purport of his own words--ignorant that though man may come 
westwards he may yet bring his worship with him--ignorant that to make 
the dream the reality and the reality the dream is heaven's dreadfullest 
favour--and ignorant that, that edge once crossed, there is no return to 
the sanity and sweet and light that are only seen clearly in the 
moment when they are lost for ever--he had dashed down the stairs crying 
in a voice hoarse and high with terror: 

"she's mad! she's mad!" 




the accident 


i 

the street had not change so much but that, little by little, its 
influence had come over romarin again; and as the clock a street or two 
away had struck seven he had stood, his hand fold on his stick, first 
curious, then expectant, and finally, as the sound had die away, oddly 
satisfy in his memory. the clock had a peculiar chime, a rather 
elaborate one, ending inconclusive on the dominant and follow after 
an unusually long interval by the stroke of the hour itself. not until 
its last vibration had become too subtle for his ear had romarin resume 
the occupation that the peal of the hour had interrupted. 

it was an occupation that especially tend to abstraction of mind--the 
note in detail of the little things of the street that he had forgotten 
with such complete that they awaken only tardy response in his 
memory now that his eyes rest on them again. the shape of a 
doorknocker, the grouping of an old chimney-stack, the crack, still 
there, in a flagstone--somewhere deep in the past these things had 
associations; but they lay very deep, and the disturb of them gave 
romarin a curious, desolate feeling, as of return to things he had 
long out-grown. 

but, as he continue to stare at the objects, the sluggish memory 
rouse more and more; and for each bit of the old that reasserte itself 
score of yard of the new seem to disappear. new shop-frontages went; 
a wall, brought up flush where formerly a recess had been, became the 
recess once more; the intermittent electric sign at the street's end, 
that wrote in green and crimson the name of a whiskey across a lamp-lit 
façade, cease to worry his eyes; and the unfamiliar new front of the 
little restaurant he was passing and repass took on its old and 
well-known aspect again. 

seven o'clock. he had thought, in dismiss his hansom, that it had been 
later. his appointment was not until a quarter past. but he decided 
against enter the restaurant and wait inside; seeing who his guest 
was, it would be better to wait at the door. by the light of the 
restaurant window he correct his watch, and then saunter a few yard 
along the street, to where men were moving flat of scenery from a back 
door of the new theatre into a sort of tumbril. the theatre was twenty 
years old, but to romarin it was "the new theatre." there had been no 
theatre there in his day. 

in his day!... his day had been twice twenty years before. forty years 
before, that street, that quarter, had been bound up in his life. he had 
not, forty years ago, been the famous painter, honoured, decorated, taken 
by the arm by monarchs; he had been a student, wild and raw as any, with 
that tranquil and urbane philosophy that had made his success still in 
abeyance within him. as his eyes had rest on the doorknocker next to 
the restaurant a smile had cross his face. how had _that_ door-knocker 
come to be left by the old crowd that had wrench off so many others? by 
what accident had _that_ survived, to bring back all the old life now so 
oddly? he stood, again smiling, his hand fold on his stick. a crown 
prince had given him that stick, and had had it engraved, "to my friend, 
romarin." 

"you oughtn't to be here, you know," he said to the door-knocker. "if i 
didn't get you, marsden ought to have done so...." 

it was marsden whom romarin had come to meet--marsden, of whom he had 
thought with such odd persistent lately. marsden was the only man in the 
world between whom and himself lay as much as the shadow of an enmity; 
and even that faint shadow was now passing. one do not guard, for forty 
years, animosity that take their rise in quick outbreak of the young 
blood; and, now that romarin came to think of it, he hadn't really hate 
marsden for more than a few months. it had been within those very door 
(romarin was passing the restaurant again) that there had been that quick 
blow, about a girl, and the table had been pushed hasty back, and he 
and marsden had fought, while the other fellow had kept the waiter 
away.... and romarin was now sixty-four, and marsden must be a year 
older, and the girl--who knew?--probably dead long ago ... yes, time 
heal these things, thank god; and romarin had felt a genuine flush of 
pleasure when marsden had accept his invitation to dinner. 

but--romarin look at his watch again--it was rather like marsden to be 
late. marsden had always been like that--had come and gone pretty much as 
he had pleased, regardless of inconvenience to others. but, doubtless, he 
had had to walk. if all report were true, marsden had not made very much 
of his life in the way of worldly success, and romarin, sorry to hear it, 
had wish he could give him a leg-up. even a good man cannot do much 
when the current of his life set against him in a tide of persistent 
ill-luck, and romarin, honour and successful, yet knew that he had been 
one of the lucky ones.... 

but it was just like marsden to be late, for all that. 

at first romarin did not recognise him when he turn the corner of the 
street and walk towards him. he hadn't made up his mind beforehand 
exactly how he had expect marsden to look, but he was conscious that he 
didn't look it. it was not the short stubble of grey beard, so short that 
it seem to hesitate between beard and unshavenness; it was not the 
figure nor carriage--clothes alter that, and the clothes of the man who 
was advance to meet romarin were, to put it bluntly, shabby; nor was 
it... but romarin did not know what it was in the advance figure that 
for the moment found no response in his memory. he was already within 
half a dozen yard of the men who were moving the scenery from the 
theatre into the tumbril, and one of the workmen put up his hand as the 
edge of a fresh "wing" appeared.... 

but at the sound of his voice the same thing happen that had happen 
when the clock had struck seven. romarin found himself sudden 
expectant, attentive, and then again curious satisfy in his memory. 
marsden's voice at least had not changed; it was as in the old days--a 
little envious, sarcastic, accept lower interpretation somewhat 
willingly, somewhat grudging of better ones. it complete the taking back 
of romarin that the chime of the clock, the doorknocker, the grouping 
of the chimney-stack and the crack in the flagstone had begun. 

"well, my distinguished academician, my--" 

marsden's voice sound across the group of scene-shifters... 

"_'alf_ a mo, _if_ you please, guv'nor," said another voice... 

for a moment the paint "wing" shut them off from one another. 

* * * * * 

in that moment romarin's accident befell him. if its essential nature is 
related in arbitrary terms, it is that there are no other terms to relate 
it in. it is a decode cipher, which can be restore to its cryptic form 
as romarin subsequent restore it. 

* * * * * 

as the painter took marsden's arm and enter the restaurant, he notice 
that while the outside of the place still retain trace of the old, its 
inside was entire new. its cheap glittering wall-mirrors, that gave a 
false impression of the actual size of the place, its love and 
shepherdess paint in the style of the cart of the vendor of 
ice-cream, its hat-racks and its four-bladed propeller that set the air 
slow in motion at the farther end of the room, might all have been 
match in a dozen similar establishment within hail of a cab-whistle. 
its gelatine-written menu-cards announce that one might dine there _à 
la carte_ or _table d'hôte_ for two shillings. neither the cooking nor 
the service had influence romarin in his choice of a place to dine at. 

he made a gesture to the waiter who advanced to help him on with his coat 
that marsden was to be assist first; but marsden, with a grunt "all 
right," had already help himself. a glimpse of the interior of the coat 
told romarin why marsden kept waiter at arm's-length. a little twinge of 
compunction took him that his own overcoat should be fur-collared and 
line with silk. 

they sat down at a corner table not far from the slow moving 
four-bladed propeller. 

"now we can talk," romarin said. "i'm glad, glad to see you again, 
marsden." 

it was a peculiarly vicious face that he saw, corrugate about the brows, 
and with stiff iron-grey hair untrimm about the ears. it shock 
romarin a little; he had hardly look to see certain things so 
accentuate by the passage of time. romarin's own brow was high and bald 
and benign, and his beard was like a broad shield of silver. 

"you're glad, are you?" said marsden, as they sat down facing one 
another. "well, i'm glad--to be seen with you. it'll revive my credit a 
bit. there's a fellow across there has recognise you already by your 
photograph in the papers.... i assume i may...?" 

he made a little upward movement of his hand. it was a gin and 
bitters marsden assume he might have. romarin ordered it; he himself 
did not take one. marsden toss down the _apéritif_ at one gulp; then 
he reach for his roll, pull it to pieces, and--romarin remember 
how in the old days marsden had always eaten bread like that--began to 
throw bullet of bread into his mouth. formerly this habit had irritate 
romarin intensely; now ... well, well, life use some of us better than 
others. small blame to these if they throw up the struggle. marsden, poor 
devil ... but the arrival of the soup interrupt romarin's meditation. 
he consult the violet-written card, ordered the succeed courses, and 
the two men ate for some minutes in silence. 

"well," said romarin presently, push away his plate and wipe his 
white moustache, "are you still a romanticist, marsden?" 

marsden, who had tuck his napkin between two of the buttons of his 
fray waistcoat, look suspicious across the glass with the dregs of 
the gin and bitters that he had half raise to his lips. 

"eh?" he said. "i say, romarin, don't let's go grave-digging among 
memory merely for the sake of making conversation. yours may be 
pleasant, but i'm not in the habit of waste much time over mine. 
might as well be making new one ... i'll drink whiskey and soda." 

it was brought, a large one; and marsden, nodding, took a deep gulp. 

"health," he said. 

"thanks," said romarin--instantly note that the monosyllable, which 
match the other's in curtness, was not at all the reply he had 
intended. "thank you--yours," he amended; and a short pause followed, in 
which fish was brought. 

this was not what romarin had hope for. he had desire to be reconcile 
with marsden, not merely to be allow to pay for his dinner. yet if 
marsden did not wish to talk it was difficult not to defer to his wish. 
it was true that he had ask if marsden was still a romanticist largely 
for the sake of something to say; but marsden's prompt point out of 
this was not encouraging. now that he came to think of it, he had never 
known precisely what marsden had meant by the word "romance" he had so 
frequent taken into his mouth; he only knew that this creed of 
romanticism, whatever it was, had been worn rather challengingly, a chip 
on the shoulder, to be knock off at some peril or other. and it had 
seem to romarin a little futile in the violence with which it had been 
maintain ... but that was neither here nor there. the point was, that 
the conversation had begun not very happily, and must be mend at once 
if at all. to mend it, romarin lean across the table. 

"be as friendly as i am, marsden," he said. "i think--pardon me--that if 
our position were reversed, and i saw in you the sincere desire to help 
that i have, i'd take it in the right way." again marsden look 
suspicious at him. "to help? how to help?" he demand "that's what i 
should like you to tell me. but i suppose (for example) you still work?" 

"oh, my work!" marsden made a little gesture of contempt. "try again, 
romarin." 

"you don't do any?... come, i'm no bad friend to my friends, and you'll 
find me--especially so." 

but marsden put up his hand. 

"not quite so quickly," he said. "let's see what you mean by help first. 
do you really mean that you want me to borrow money from you? that's help 
as i understand it nowadays." 

"then you've changed," said romarin--wondering, however, in his secret 
heart whether marsden had change very much in that respect after all. 

marsden gave a short honk of a laugh. 

"you didn't suppose i hadn't changed, did you?" then he lean sudden 
forward. "this is rather a mistake, romarin--rather a mistake," he said. 

"what is?" 

"this--our meeting again. quite a mistake." 

romarin sighed. "i had hope not," he said. 

marsden lean forward again, with another gesture romarin remember 
very well--dinner knife in hand, edge and palm upwards, punctuate 
and expound with the point. 

"i tell you, it's a mistake," he said, knife and hand balanced. "you 
can't reopen things like this. you don't really _want_ to reopen them; 
you only want to reopen certain of them; you want to pick and choose 
among things, to approve and disapprove. there must have been somewhere 
or other something in me you didn't altogether dislike--i can't for the 
life of me think what it was, by the way; and you want to lay stress on 
that and to sink the rest. well, you can't. i won't let you. i'll not 
submit my life to you like that. if you want to go into things, all 
right; but it must be all or none. and i'd like another drink." 

he put the knife down with a little clap as romarin beckon to the 
waiter. 

there was distress on romarin's face. he was not conscious of have 
adopt a superior attitude. but again he told himself that he must make 
allowances. men who don't come off in life's struggle are apt to be 
touchy, and he was; after all, the same old marsden, the man with whom he 
desire to be at peace. 

"are you quite fair to me?" he ask presently, in a low voice. 

again the knife was taken up and its point advanced. 

"yes, i am," said marsden in a slightly raise voice; and he indicate 
with the knife the mirror at the end of the table. "you know you've done 
well, and i, to all appearances, haven't; you can't look at that glass 
and not know it. but i've follow the line of my development too, no 
less logically than you. my life's been mine, and i'm not go to 
apologise for it to a single breathing creature. more, i'm proud of it. 
at least, there's been singleness of intention about it. so i think i'm 
strict fair in point that out when you talk about helping me." 

"perhaps so, perhaps so," romarin agree a little sadly. "it's your tone 
more than anything else that make things a little difficult. believe me, 
i've no end in my mind except pure friendliness." 

"no-o-o," said marsden--a long "no" that seem to deliberate, to 
examine, and finally to admit. "no. i believe that. and you usually get 
what you set out for. oh yes. i've watch your rise--i've made a point 
of watch it. it's been a bit at a time, but you've got there. you're 
that sort. it's on your forehead--your destiny." 

romarin smiled. 

"hallo, that's new, isn't it?" he said. "it wasn't your habit to talk 
much about destiny, if i remember rightly. let me see; wasn't this more 
your style--'will, passion, laughs-at-impossibilities and says,' et 
cetera--and so forth? wasn't that it? with always the suspicion not far 
away that you did things more from theoretical conviction than real 
impulse after all?" 

a dispassionate observer would have judge that the words went somewhere 
near home. marsden was scrape together with the edge of his knife the 
crumb of his broken roll. he scrape them into a little square, and then 
trim the corners. not until the little pile was shaped to his liking 
did he look surly up. 

"let it rest, romarin," he said curtly. "drop it," he added. "let it 
alone. if i begin to talk like that, too, we shall only cut one another 
up. clink glasses--there--and let it alone." 

mechanical romarin clinked; but his bald brow was perplexed. 

"'cut one another up?'" he repeated. 

"yes. let it alone." 

"'cut one another up?'" he repeated once more. "you puzzle me entirely." 

"well, perhaps i'm altogether wrong. i only want to warn you that i've 
dare a good many things in my time. now drop it." 

romarin had fine brown eyes, under oriental arch brows. again they 
noted the singularly vicious look of the man opposite. they were full 
of mistrust and curiosity, and he stroke his silver beard. 

"drop it?" he said slow ... "no, let's go on. i want to hear more of 
this." 

"i'd much rather have another drink in peace and quietness.... waiter!" 

either lean back in his chair, survey the other. "you're a perverse 
devil still," was romarin's thought. marsden's, apparently, was of 
nothing but the whiskey and soda the waiter had gone to fetch. 

* * * * * 

romarin was inclined to look askance at a man who could follow up a gin 
and bitters with three or four whiskey and soda without turning a hair. 
it argue the season cask. marsden had bidden the waiter leave the 
bottle and the syphon on the table, and was already mix himself 
another stiff peg. 

"well," he said, "since you will have it so--to the old days." 

"to the old days," said romarin, watch him gulp it down. 

"queer, look back across all that time at 'em, isn't it? how do you 
feel about it?" 

"in a mixed kind of way, i think; the usual thing: pleasure and regret 
mingled." 

"oh, you have regrets, have you?" 

"for certain things, yes. not, let me say, my turn-up with you, marsden," 
he laughed. "that's why i chose the old place--" he gave a glance 
round at its glittering newness. "do you happen to remember what all that 
was about? i've only the vaguest idea." 

marsden gave him a long look. "that all?" he asked. 

"oh, i remember in a sort of way. that 'romantic' soap-bubble of yours 
was really at the bottom of it, i suspect. tell me," he smiled, "did you 
really suppose life could be live on those mad lines you use to lay 
down?" 

"my life," said marsden calmly, "has been." 

"not literally." 

"literally." 

"you mean to say that you haven't outgrown _that_?" 

"i hope not." 

romarin had thrown up his handsome head. "well, well!" he murmur 
incredulously. 

"why 'well, well'?" marsden demanded.... "but, of course, you never did 
and never will know what i meant." 

"by romance? ... no, i can't say that i did; but as i conceive it, it 
was something that began in appetite and end in diabetes." 

"not philosophic, eh?" marsden inquired, pick up a chicken bone. 

"highly unphilosophic," said romarin, shake his head. 

"hm!" grunt marsden, strip the bone... "well, i grant it pay in a 
different way." 

"it do pay, then?" romarin asked. 

"oh yes, it pays." 

the restaurant had fill up. it was one frequent by young artists, 
musicians, journalist and the cling to the rather fray fringe of 
the arts. from time to time head were turn to look at romarin's portly 
and handsome figure, which the press, the regent street photographic 
establishments, and the academy supplement had made well known. the 
plump young frenchwoman within the glaze cash office near the door, 
at whom marsden had several times glance in a way at which romarin had 
frowned, was aware of the honour done the restaurant; and several times 
the blond-bearded proprietor had advanced and inquire with concern 
whether the dinner and the service was to the liking of m'sieu. 

and the eyes that were turn to romarin plainly wonder who the 
scallawag dine with him might be. 

since romarin had chosen that their conversation should be of the old 
days, and without pick and choosing, marsden was quite willing that 
it should be so. again he was casting the bullet of bread into his 
mouth, and again romarin was conscious of irritation. marsden, too, 
notice it; but in await the _rôti_ he still continue to roll and 
bolt the pellets, washing them down with gulp of whiskey and soda. 

"oh yes, it paid," he resumed. "not in that way, of course--" he 
indicate the head, quick turn away again, of an aureole youngster 
with a large bunch of black satin tie, "--not in admiration of that 
sort, but in other ways--" 

"tell me about it." 

"certainly, if you want it. but you're my host. won't you let me hear 
your side of it all first?" 

"but i thought you said you knew that--had follow my career?" 

"so i have. it's not your list of honours and degrees; let me see, what 
are you? r.a., d.c.l., doctor of literature, whatever that means, and 
professor of this, that, and the other, and not at the end of it yet. i 
know all that. i don't say you haven't earn it; i admire your painting; 
but it's not that. i want to know what it _feels_ like to be up there 
where you are." 

it was a childish question, and romarin felt foolish in try to answer 
it. such things were the things the adore aureole youngster a table or 
two away would have like to ask. romarin recognise in marsden the old 
craving for sensation; it was part of the theoretical creed marsden had 
made for himself, of do things, not for their own sakes, but in order 
that he might have done them. of course, it had appear to a fellow like 
that, that romarin himself had always had a calculate end in view; he 
had not; marsden merely measured romarin's peck out of his own bushel. it 
had been marsden who, in self-consciously seek his own life, had lost 
it, and romarin was more than a little inclined to suspect that the 
vehemence with which he protest that he had not lost it was precisely 
the measure of the loss. 

but he essay it--essayed to give marsden a _résumé_ of his career. he 
told him of the stroke of sheer luck that had been the foundation of it 
all, the fall ill of another painter who had turn over certain 
commission to him. he told him of his poor but happy marriage, and of 
the windfall, not large, but timely, that had come to his wife. he told 
him of fortunate acquaintanceship happily cultivated, of his first 
important commission, of the fresco that had procure for him his 
associateship, of his sale to the chantrey, and of his quiet 
remunerative visitorship and his work on boards and committees. 

and as he talked, marsden drew his empty glass to him, moisten his 
finger with a little spilt liquid, and began to run the finger round the 
rim of the glass. they had done that formerly, a whole roomful of them, 
producing, when each had found the note of his instrument, a high, thin, 
intolerable singing. to this singing romarin strove to tell his tale. 

but that thin and bat-like note silence him. he end lamely, with some 
empty generalisation on success. 

"ah, but success in what?" marsden demanded, interrupt his play on 
the glass for a moment. 

"in your aim, whatever it may be." 

"ah!" said marsden, resume his performance. 

romarin had sought in his recital to minimise difference in 
circumstances; but marsden seem bent on aggravate them. he had the 
miserable advantage of the man who has nothing to lose. and bit by bit, 
romarin had begun to realise that he was go considerably more than 
halfway to meet this old enemy of his, and that amity seem as far on as 
ever. in his heart he began to feel the foreknowledge that their meeting 
could have no conclusion. he hate the man, the look of his face and the 
sound of his voice, as much as ever. 

the proprietor approach with profoundest apology in his attitude. 
m'sieu would pardon him, but the noise of the glass ... it was 
annoy ... another m'sieu had made complaint.... 

"eh?..." cry marsden. "oh, that! certainly! it can be put to a much 
better purpose." 

he refill the glass. 

the liquor had begun to tell on him. a quarter of the quantity would have 
made a clean-living man incapable drunk, but it had only made marsden's 
eyes bright. he gave a sarcastic laugh. 

"and is that all?" he asked. 

romarin reply shortly that that was all. 

"you've miss out the r.a., and the d.c.l." 

"then let me add that i'm a doctor of civil law and a full member of the 
royal academy," said romarin, almost at the end of his patience. "and 
now, since you don't think much of it, may i hear your own account?" 

"oh, by all means. i don't know, however, that--" he broke off to throw a 
glance at a woman who had just enter the restaurant--a divest glance 
that cause romarin to redden to his crown and drop his eyes. "i was 
go to say that you may think as little of my history as i do of yours. 
supple woman that; when the rather scraggy blonde do take it into her 
head to be a devil she's the worst kind there is...." 

without apology romarin look at his watch. 

"all right," said marsden, smiling, "for what _i've_ got out of life, 
then. but i warn you, it's entire discreditable." 

romarin did not doubt it. 

"but it's mine, and i boast of it. i've done--barring receiving honours 
and degrees--everything--everything! if there's anything i haven't done, 
tell me and lend me a sovereign, and i'll go and do it." 

"you haven't told the story." 

"that's so. here go then ... well, you know, unless you've forgotten, 
how i began...." 

fruit and nutshell and nutcracker lay on the table between them, and at 
the end of it, shield from draughts by the menu cards, the coffee 
apparatus simmer over its elusive blue flame. romarin was taking the 
rind from a pear with a table-knife, and marsden had decline port in 
favour of a small golden liqueur of brandy. every seat in the restaurant 
was now occupied, and the proprietor himself had brought his finest 
cigarette and cigars. the waiter pour out the coffee, and departed 
with the apparatus in one hand and his napkin in the other. 

marsden was already well into his tale... 

the frightful unction with which he told it appall romarin. it was as 
he had said--there was nothing he had not done and did not exult in with 
a sickening exultation. it had, indeed, end in diabetes. in the pitiful 
hunting down of sensation to the last inch he had been fiendishly 
ingenious and utterly unimaginative. his unholy curiosity had spare 
nothing, his unnatural appetite had known no truth. it was grin sin. 
the detail of it simply cannot be told.... 

and his vanity in it all was prodigious. romarin was pale as he listened. 
what! in order that _this_ malignant growth in society's breast should 
be able to say "i know," had sanctities been profaned, sweet convention 
assailed, purity blackened, sound infected, and all that was bright 
and of the day been sunk in the quagmire that this creature of the night 
had called--yes, still called--by the gentle name of romance? yes, 
so it had been. not only had men and women suffer dishonour, but 
manhood and womanhood and the clean institution by which alone the 
creature was suffer to exist had been brought to shame. and what was he 
to look at when it was all done?... 

"romance--beauty--the beauty of things as they are!" he croaked. 

if face in the restaurant were now turn to romarin, it was the horror 
on romarin's own face that drew them. he drew out his handkerchief and 
mop his brow. 

"but," he stammer presently, "you are speak of 
generalities--horrible theories--things diabolical conceivable 
to be done--" 

"what?" cry marsden, checked for a moment in his horrible triumph. "no, 
by god! i've done 'em, done 'em! don't you understand? if you don't, 
question me!..." 

"no, no!" cry romarin. 

"but i say yes! you came for this, and you shall have it! i try to stop 
you, but you want it, and by god you shall have it! you think your 
life's been full and mine empty? ha ha!... romance! i had the conviction 
of it, and i've had the courage too! i haven't told you a tenth of it! 
what would you like? chamber-windows when love was hot? the killing of a 
man who stood in my way? (i've fought a duel, and killed.) the squeeze 
of the juice out of life like _that_?" he pointed to romarin's plate; 
romarin had been eat grapes. "did you find me saying i'd do a thing 
and then drawing back from it when we--" he made a quick gesture of both 
hand towards the middle of the restaurant floor. 

"when we fought--?" 

"yes, when we fought, here!... oh no, oh no! i've lived, i tell you, 
every moment! not a title, not a degree, but i've live such a life as 
you never dream of--!" 

"thank god--" 

but sudden marsden's voice, which had risen, drop again. he began to 
shake with interior chuckles. they were the old, old chuckles, and they 
fill romarin with a hatred hardly to be borne. the sound of the 
animal's voice had begun it, and his every word, look, movement, 
gesture, since they had enter the restaurant, had add to it. and he 
was now chuckling, chuckling, shake with chuckles, as if some 
monstrous tit-bit still remain to be told. already romarin had toss 
aside his napkin, beckon to the waiter, and said, "m'sieu dine 
with me...." 

"ho ho ho ho!" came the drunken sounds. "it's a long time since m'sieu 
dine here with his old friend romarin! do you remember the last time? do 
you remember it? _pif, pan_! two smack across the table, romarin--oh, 
you got it in very well!--and then, _brrrrr_! quick! back with the 
tables--all the fellow round--farquharson for me and smith for you, and 
then to it, romarin!... and you really don't remember what it was all 
about?..." 

romarin had remembered. his face was not the face of the philosophical 
master of life now. 

"you said she shouldn't--little pattie hine you know--you said she 
shouldn't--" 

romarin sprang half from his chair, and brought his fist down on the 
table. 

"and by heaven, she didn't! at least that's one thing you haven't done!" 

marsden too had risen unsteadily. 

"oho, oho? you think that?" 

a wild thought flash across romarin's brain. 

"you mean--?" 

"i mean?... oho, oho! yes, i mean! she did, romarin...." 

the mirrors, misty seen through the smoke of half a hundred cigar and 
cigarettes, the love and shepherdess of the garish walls, the diner 
start up in their places, all sudden seem to swing round in a 
great half-circle before romarin's eyes. the next moment, feeling as if 
he stood on something on which he found it difficult to keep his balance, 
he had caught up the table-knife with which he had peel the pear and 
had struck at the side of marsden's neck. the round blade snapped, but 
he struck again with the broken edge, and left the knife where it 
entered. the table appear uptilte almost vertical; over it marsden's 
head disappeared; it was follow by a shower of glass, cigars, 
artificial flower and the tablecloth at which he clutched; and the 
dirty america cloth of the table top was left bare. 

* * * * * 

but the edge behind which marsden's face had disappear remain 
vertical. a group of scene-shifters were moving a flat of scenery from a 
theatre into a tumbril-like cart... 

and romarin knew that, past, present, and future, he had seen it all in 
an instant, and that marsden stood behind that paint wing. 

and he knew, too, that he had only to wait until that flat pass and to 
take marsden's arm and enter the restaurant, _and it would be so_. a 
drown man is said to see all in one unmeasurable instant of time; a 
year-long dream is but, they say, an instantaneous arrangement in the 
moment of waking of the molecule we associate with ideas; and the past 
of history and the future of prophecy are fold up in the mystic moment 
we call the present.... 

_it would come true_.... 

for one moment romarin stood; the next, he had turn and run for his 
life. 

at the corner of the street he collide with a loafer, and only the wall 
save them from go down. feverishly romarin plunge his hand into his 
pocket and brought out a handful of silver. he cram it into the 
loafer's hand. 

"here--quick--take it!" he gasped. "there's a man there, by that 
restaurant door--he's wait for mr. romarin--tell him--tell him--tell 
him mr. romarin's had an accident--" 

and he dashed away, leave the man look at the silver in his palm. 




the cigarette case 


"a cigarette, loder?" i said, offering my case. for the moment lode was 
not smoking; for long enough he had not been talking. 

"thanks," he replied, taking not only the cigarette, but the case also. 
the other went on talking; lode became silent again; but i notice 
that he kept my cigarette case in his hand, and look at it from time to 
time with an interest that neither its design nor its costly seem 
to explain. presently i caught his eye. 

"a pretty case," he remarked, putt it down on the table. "i once had 
one exactly like it." 

i answer that they were in every shop window. 

"oh yes," he said, putt aside any question of rarity.... "i lost 
mine." 

"oh?..." 

he laughed. "oh, that's all right--i got it back again--don't be afraid 
i'm go to claim yours. but the way i lost it--found it--the whole 
thing--was rather curious. i've never been able to explain it. i wonder 
if you could?" 

i answer that i certainly couldn't till i'd heard it, whereupon loder, 
taking up the silver case again and holding it in his hand as he talked, 
began: 

"this happen in provence, when i was about as old as marsham there--and 
every bit as romantic. i was there with carroll--you remember poor old 
carroll and what a blade of a boy he was--as romantic as four marsham 
roll into one. (excuse me, marsham, won't you? it's a romantic tale, 
you see, or at least the setting is.) ... we were in provence, carroll 
and i; twenty-four or thereabouts; romantic, as i say; and--and this 
happened. 

"and it happen on the top of a whole lot of other things, you must 
understand, the things that do happen when you're twenty-four. if it 
hadn't been provence, it would have been somewhere else, i suppose, 
nearly, if not quite as good; but this was provence, that smell (as you 
might say) of twenty-four as it smell of argelasse and wild lavender and 
broom.... 

"we'd had the dickens of a walk of it, just with knapsacks--had start 
somewhere in the ardèche and tramp south through the vine and almond 
and olives--montélimar, orange, avignon, and a fortnight at that blanch 
skeleton of a town, le baux. we'd nothing to do, and had gone just where 
we liked, or rather just where carroll had liked; and carroll had had the 
_de bello gallico_ in his pocket, and had had a notion, i fancy, of 
taking in the whole ground of the roman conquest--i remember he lug me 
off to some place or other, pourrières i believe its name was, because--i 
forget how many thousands--were kill in a river-bed there, and they 
stove in the water-casks so that if the men want water they'd have to 
go forward and fight for it. and then we'd gone on to arles, where 
carroll had fallen in love with everything that had a bow of black 
velvet in her hair, and after that tarascon, nîmes, and so on, the usual 
round--i won't bother you with that. in a word, we'd had two month of 
it, eat almond and apricot from the trees, watch the women at the 
communal washing-fountains under the dark plane-trees, singing _magali_ 
and the _qué cantes_, and carroll yarn away all the time about caesar 
and vercingetorix and dante, and try to learn provençal so that he 
could read the stuff in the _journal des félibriges_ that he'd never have 
look at if it had been in english.... 

"well, we got to darbisson. we'd run across some young chap or 
other--rangon his name was--who was a vine-planter in those parts, and 
rangon had ask us to spend a couple of days with him, with him and his 
mother, if we happen to be in the neighbourhood. so as we might as 
well happen to be there as anywhere else, we sent him a postcard and 
went. this would be in june or early in july. all day we walk across 
a plain of vines, past hurdle of wattle _cannes_ and great wind-screens 
of velvety cypresses, sixty feet high, all white with dust on the north 
side of 'em, for the mistral was have its three-days' revel, and it 
whistle and roar through the _cannes_ till score of yard of 'em 
at a time were bowed nearly to the earth. a roaring day it was, i 
remember.... but the wind fell a little late in the afternoon, and we 
were pore over what it had left of our ordnance survey--like fools, 
we'd got the unmounted paper map instead of the linen ones--when rangon 
himself found us, coming out to meet us in a very badly turned-out trap. 
he drove us back himself, through darbisson, to the house, a mile and a 
half beyond it, where he live with his mother. 

"he spoke no english, rangon didn't, though, of course, both france and 
provençal; and as he drove us, there was carroll, use him as a 
franco-provençal dictionary, pepper him with question about the name 
of things in the patois--i beg its pardon, the language--though there's 
a good deal of my eye and betty martin about that, and i fancy this 
félibrige business will be in a good many pieces when frédéric mistral 
is under that court-of-love pavilion arrangement he's had put up for 
himself in the graveyard at maillanne. if the language has got to go, 
well, it's got to go, i suppose; and while i personally don't want to 
give it a kick, i rather sympathise with the government. those jaunt of 
a sunday out to le baux, for instance, with paper lantern and bengal 
fire and a fellow spout _o blanche venus d'arles_--they're well 
enough, and compare favourable with our bank holiday and sunday league 
picnics, but ... but that's nothing to do with my tale after all.... so 
he drove on, and by the time we got to rangon's house carroll had learned 
the greater part of _magali_.... 

"as you, no doubt, know, it's a restricted sort of life in some respects 
that a young _vigneron_ lives in those parts, and it was as we reach 
the house that rangon remember something--or he might have been try 
to tell us as we came along for all i know, and not been able to get a 
word in edgeways for carroll and his provençal. it seem that his mother 
was away from home for some days--apologies of the most profound, of 
course; our host was the soul of courtesy, though he did try to get at 
us a bit later.... we express our polite regrets, naturally; but i 
didn't quite see at first what difference it made. i only began to see 
when rangon, with more apologies, told us that we should have to go back 
to darbisson for dinner. it appear that when madame rangon went away 
for a few days she disperse the whole of the female side of her 
establishment also, and she'd left her son with nobody to look after 
him except an old man we'd seen in the yard mending one of these 
double-cylindered sulphur-sprinklers they clap across the horse's back 
and drive between the row of vines.... rangon explain all this as we 
stood in the hall drink an _apéritif_--a hall crowded with oak 
furniture and photograph and a cradle-like bread-crib and door opening 
to right and left to the other rooms of the ground floor. he had also, it 
seemed, to ask us to be so infinite obliging as to excuse him for one 
hour after dinner--our postcard had come unexpectedly, he said, and 
already he had made an appointment with his agent about the _vendange_ 
for the coming autumn.... we, beg him, of course, not to allow us to 
interfere with his business in the slightest degree. he thank us a 
thousand times. 

"'but though we dine in the village, we will take our own wine with us,' 
he said, 'a wine _surfin_--one of my wines--you shall see--' 

"then he show us round his place--i forget how many hundred of acre 
of vines, and into the great building with the press and pump and 
cask and the huge barrel they call the thunderbolt--and about seven 
o'clock we walk back to darbisson to dinner, carry our wine with us. 
i think the restaurant we dine in was the only one in the place, and our 
gaillard of a host--he was a straight-backed, well-set-up chap, with 
rather fine eyes--did us on the whole pretty well. his wine certainly was 
good stuff, and set our tongue going.... 

"a moment ago i said a fellow like rangon leads a restricted sort of life 
in those parts. i saw this more clearly as dinner went on. we dine by an 
open window, from which we could see the stream with the plank across it 
where the women wash clothes during the day and assemble in the 
evening for gossip. there were a dozen or so of them there as we dined, 
laugh and chat in low tones--they all seem pretty--it was 
quick fall dusk--all the girl are pretty then, and are quite 
conscious of it--_you_ know, marsham. behind them, at the end of the 
street, one of these great cypress wind-screens show black against the 
sky, a ragged edge something like the line the needle draw on a rainfall 
chart; and you could only tell whether they were men or women under the 
plantain by their voice ripple and chatter and sudden a deep 
note.... once i heard a muffle scuffle and a sound like a kiss.... it 
was then that rangon's little trouble came out.... 

"it seem that he didn't know any girls--wasn't allow to know any 
girls. the girl of the village were pretty enough, but you see how it 
was--he'd a position to keep up--appearances to maintain--couldn't be 
familiar during the year with the girl who gather his grape for him 
in the autumn.... and as soon as carroll gave him a chance, _he_ began to 
ask _us_ questions, about england, english girls, the liberty they had, 
and so on. 

"of course, we couldn't tell him much he hadn't heard already, but that 
made no difference; he could stand any amount of that, our strapping 
young _vigneron_; and he ask us question by the dozen, that we both 
try to answer at once. and his delight and envy!... what! in england 
did the young men see the young women of their own class without 
restraint--the sister of their friends _même_--even at the house? was it 
permit that they drank tea with them in the afternoon, or went without 
invitation to pass the _soirée_?... he had all the later prévosts in his 
room, he told us (i don't doubt he had the early one also); prévost 
and the disestablish between them must be play the mischief 
with the convent system of education for young girls; and our young man 
was--what d'you call it?--'co-ed'--co-educationalist--by jove, yes!... he 
seem to marvel that we should have left a country so blessed as england 
to visit his dusty, wild-lavender-smelling, girl-less provence.... you 
don't know half your luck, marsham.... 

"well, we talk after this fashion--we'd left the dining-room of the 
restaurant and had plant ourselves on a bench outside with rangon 
between us--when rangon sudden look at his watch and said it was time 
he was off to see this agent of his. would we take a walk, he ask us, 
and meet him again there? he said.... but as his agent live in the 
direction of his own home, we said we'd meet him at the house in an hour 
or so. off he went, envy every englishman who stepped, i don't 
doubt.... i told you how old--how young--we were.... heigho!... 

"well, off go rangon, and carroll and i got up, stretch ourselves, 
and took a walk. we walk a mile or so, until it began to get pretty 
dark, and then turned; and it was as we came into the black of one of 
these cypress hedge that the thing i'm telling you of happened. the 
hedge took a sharp turn at that point; as we came round the angle we saw 
a couple of women's figures hardly more than twenty yard ahead--don't 
know how they got there so suddenly, i'm sure; and that same moment i 
found my foot on something small and white and glimmer on the grass. 

"i picked it up. it was a handkerchief--a woman's--embroidered-- 

"the two figures ahead of us were walking in our direction; there was 
every probability that the handkerchief belong to one of them; so we 
step out.... 

"at my 'pardon, madame,' and lift hat one of the figures turn her 
head; then, to my surprise, she spoke in english--cultivated english. 
i held out the handkerchief. it belong to the elder lady of the two, 
the one who had spoken, a very gentle-voiced old lady, old by very many 
years than her companion. she took the handkerchief and thank me.... 

"somebody--sterne, isn't it?--says that englishmen don't travel to see 
englishmen. i don't know whether he'd stand to that in the case of 
englishwomen; carroll and i didn't.... we were walking rather slow 
along, four abreast across the road; we ask permission to introduce 
ourselves, did so, and received some name in return which, strange 
enough, i've entire forgotten--i only remember that the ladies were 
aunt and niece, and live at darbisson. they shook their head when i 
mention m. rangon's name and said we were visiting him. they didn't 
know him.... 

"i'd never been in darbisson before, and i haven't been since, so i don't 
know the map of the village very well. but the place isn't very big, 
and the house at which we stop in twenty minutes or so is probably 
there yet. it had a large double door--a double door in two senses, 
for it was a big _porte-cochère_ with a small door inside it, and an 
iron grille shut in the whole. the gentle-voiced old lady had already 
taken a key from her reticule and was thank us again for the little 
service of the handkerchief; then, with the little gesture one make when 
one has found oneself on the point of omit a courtesy, she gave a 
little musical laugh. 

"'but,' she said with a little movement of invitation, 'one see so 
few compatriot here--if you have the time to come in and smoke a 
cigarette ... also the cigarette,' she added, with another ripple 
laugh, 'for we have few callers, and live alone--' 

"hastily as i was about to accept, carroll was before me, profess a 
nostalgia for the sound of the english tongue that made his recent 
protestation about provençal a shameless hypocrisy. persuasive young 
rascal, carroll was--poor chap ... so the elder lady open the grille 
and the wooden door beyond it, and we entered. 

"by the light of the candle which the younger lady took from a bracket 
just within the door we saw that we were in a handsome hall or vestibule; 
and my wonder that rangon had made no mention of what was apparently a 
considerable establishment was increase by the fact that its tenant 
must be known to be english and could be seen to be entire charming. i 
couldn't understand it, and i'm afraid hypothese rush into my head 
that cast doubt on the rangons--you know--whether _they_ were all right. 
we knew nothing about our young planter, you see.... 

"i look about me. there were tub here and there against the walls, 
gaily painted, with glossy-leaved aloe and palm in them--one of the 
aloes, i remember, was flowering; a little fountain in the middle made a 
tinkle noise; we put our cap on a carve and gilt console table; and 
before us rose a broad staircase with shallow steps of spotless stone and 
a beautiful wrought-iron handrail. at the top of the staircase were more 
palm and aloes, and double door paint in a clear grey. 

"we follow our hostess up the staircase. i can hear yet the sharp 
clean click our boots made on that hard shiny stone--see the lights of 
the candle gleam on the handrail ... the young girl--she was not much 
more than a girl--pushed at the doors, and we went in. 

"the room we enter was all of a piece with the rest for rather 
old-fashioned fineness. it was large, lofty, beautiful kept. carroll 
went round for miss ... whatever her name was ... lighting candle in 
sconces; and as the flame crept up they glimmer on a beautiful 
polish floor, which was bare except for an eastern rug here and there. 
the elder lady had sat down in a gilt chair, louis fourteenth i should 
say, with a striped rep of the colour of a petunia; and i really don't 
know--don't smile, smith--what induce me to lead her to it by the 
finger-tips, bend over her hand for a moment as she sat down. there 
was an old tambour-frame behind her chair, i remember, and a vast oval 
mirror with cluster candle-brackets fill the greater part of the 
farther wall, the brightest and clearest glass i've ever seen...." 

he paused, look at my cigarette case, which he had taken into his hand 
again. he smile at some recollection or other, and it was a minute or 
so before he continued. 

"i must admit that i found it a little annoying, after what we'd 
been talk about at dinner an hour before, that rangon wasn't with 
us. i still couldn't understand how he could have neighbour so 
charming without knowing about them, but i didn't care to insist on 
this to the old lady, who for all i knew might have her own reason 
for keeping to herself. and, after all, it was our place to return 
rangon's hospitality in london if he ever came there, not, so to speak, 
on his own doorstep.... so presently i forgot all about rangon, and i'm 
pretty sure that carroll, who was talk to his companion of some 
félibrige junketing or other and have the air of gounod's _mireille_ 
hum soft over to him, didn't waste a thought on him either. soon 
carroll--you remember what a pretty crooning, hum voice he had--soon 
carroll was murmur what they call 'seconds,' but so low that the sound 
hardly came across the room; and i came in with a soft bass note from 
time to time. no instrument, you know; just an unaccompanied murmur no 
loud than an aeolian harp; and it sound infinite sweet and 
plaintive and--what shall i say?--weak--attenuated--faint--'pale' you 
might almost say--in that formal, rather old-fashioned _salon_, with that 
great clear oval mirror throw back the still flame of the candle in
