the sconce on the walls. outside the wind had now fallen completely; all 
was very quiet; and sudden in a voice not much loud than a sigh, 
carroll's companion was singing _oft in the stilly night_--you know 
it...." 

he broke off again to murmur the beginning of the air. then, with a 
little laugh for which we saw no reason, he went on again: 

"well, i'm not go to try to convince you of such a special and 
delicate thing as the charm of that hour--it wasn't more than an 
hour--it would be all about an hour we stayed. things like that just 
have to be said and left; you destroy them the moment you begin to 
insist on them; we've every one of us had experience like that, and 
don't say much about them. i was as much in love with my old lady as 
carroll evidently was with his young one--i can't tell you why--being 
in love has just to be taken for grant too, i suppose... marsham 
understands.... we smoke our cigarettes, and sang again, once more 
filling that clear-painted, quiet apartment with a murmur no loud 
than if a light breeze found that the bell of a bed of flower were 
really bell and play on 'em. the old lady move her finger gently 
on the round table by the side of her chair,.. oh, infinite pretty it 
was.... then carroll wander off into the _qué cantes_--awfully 
pretty--'it is not for myself i sing, but for my friend who is near 
me'--and i can't tell you how like four old friends we were, those two 
so oddly met ladies and carroll and myself.... and so to _oft in the 
stilly night_ again.... 

"but for all the sweet and the glamour of it, we couldn't stay on 
indefinitely, and i wonder what time it was, but didn't ask--anything 
to do with clock and watches would have seem a cold and mechanical 
sort of thing just then.... and when presently we both got up neither 
carroll nor i ask to be allow to call again in the morning to 
thank them for a charming hour.... and they seem to feel the same as 
we did about it. there was no 'hoping that we should meet again in 
london'--neither an au revoir nor a good-bye--just a tacit understanding 
that that hour should remain isolated, accept like a good gift without 
look the gift-horse in the mouth, single, unattached to any hour 
before or after--i don't know whether you see what i mean.... give me a 
match somebody.... 

"and so we left, with no more than looks exchange and finger-tips 
rest between the back of our hand and our lip for a moment. we 
found our way out by ourselves, down that shallow-stepped staircase with 
the handsome handrail, and let ourselves out of the double door and 
grille, close it softly. we made for the village without speak a 
word.... heigho!..." 

lode had picked up the cigarette case again, but for all the way his 
eyes rest on it i doubt whether he really saw it. i'm pretty sure he 
didn't; i knew when he did by the glance he shot at me, as much as to say 
"i see you're wonder where the cigarette case come in."... he 
resume with another little laugh. 

"well," he continued, "we got back to rangon's house. i really don't 
blame rangon for the way he took it when we told him, you know--he 
thought we were pull his leg, of course, and he wasn't have any; not 
he! there were no english ladies in darbisson, he said.... we told him as 
nearly as we could just where the house was--we weren't very precise, i'm 
afraid, for the village had been in dark as we had come through it, 
and i had to admit that the cypress hedge i try to describe where we'd 
met our friends was a good deal like other cypress hedges--and, as i say, 
rangon wasn't taking any. i myself was rather annoy that he should 
think we were return his hospitality by try to get at him, and it 
wasn't very easy either to explain in my france and carroll's provençal 
that we were go to let the thing stand as it was and weren't go to 
call on our charming friends again.... the end of it was that rangon 
just laugh and yawned.... 

"'i knew it was good, my wine,' he said, 'but--' a shrug said the rest. 
'not so good as all that,' he meant.... 

"then he gave us our candles, show us to our rooms, shook hands, and 
march off to his own room and the prévosts. 

"i dream of my old lady half the night. 

"after coffee the next morning i put my hand into my pocket for my 
cigarette case and didn't find it. i went through all my pockets, and 
then i ask carroll if he'd got it. 

"'no,' he replied.... 'think you left it behind at that place last 
night?' 

"'yes; did you?' rangon pop in with a twinkle. 

"i went through all my pocket again. no cigarette case.... 

"of course, it was possible that i'd left it behind, and i was annoy 
again. i didn't want to go back, you see.... but, on the other hand, i 
didn't want to lose the case--it was a present--and rangon's smile 
nettle me a good deal, too. it was both a challenge to our truthful 
and a testimonial to that very good wine of his.... 

"'might have done,' i grunted.... 'well, in that case we'll go and get 
it.' 

"'if one try the restaurant first--?' rangon suggested, smile again. 

"'by all means,' said i stuffily, though i remember have the case 
after we'd left the restaurant. 

"we were round at the restaurant by half-past nine. the case wasn't 
there. i'd known jolly well beforehand it wasn't, and i saw rangon's 
mouth twitch with amusement. 

"'so we now seek the abode of these english ladies, _hein_?' he said. 

"'yes,' said i; and we left the restaurant and strode through the village 
by the way we'd taken the evening before.... 

"that vigneron's smile became more and more irritate to me.... 'it is 
then the next village?' he said presently, as we left the last house and 
came out into the open plain. 

"we went back.... 

"i was irritate because we were two to one, you see, and carroll back 
me up. 'a double door, with a grille in front of it,' he repeated for 
the fiftieth time.... rangon merely reply that it wasn't our good faith 
he doubted. he didn't actually use the word 'drunk.'... 

"'_mais tiens_,' he said suddenly, try to conceal his mirth. '_si 
c'est possible... si c'est possible_... a double door with a grille? but 
perhaps that i know it, the domicile of these so elusive ladies.... come 
this way.' 

"he took us back along a plantain-groved street, and sudden turn up 
an alley that was little more than two gutter and a crack of sky 
overhead between two broken-tiled roofs. it was a dilapidated, desert 
_ruelle_, and i was positively angry when rangon pointed to a blister 
old _porte-cochère_ with a half-unhinged railing in front of it. 

"'is it that, your house?' he asked. 

"'no,' say i, and 'no,' say carroll ... and off we start again.... 

"but another half-hour brought us back to the same place, and carroll 
scratch his head. 

"'who lives there, anyway?' he said, glower at the _porte-cochère_, 
chin forward, hand in pockets. 

"'nobody,' say rangon, as much as to say 'look at it!' 'm'sieu then 
meditate taking it?'... 

"then i struck in, quite out of temper by this time. 

"'how much would the rent be?' i asked, as if i really thought of taking 
the place just to get back at him. 

"he mention something ridiculous small in the way of francs. 

"'one might at least see the place,' say i. 'can the key be got?' 

"he bowed. the key was at the baker's, not a hundred yard away, he 
said.... 

"we got the key. it was the key of the inner wooden door--that grid of 
rusty iron didn't need one--it came clean off its single hinge when 
carroll touched it. carroll opened, and we stood for a moment motion 
to one another to step in. then rangon went in first, and i heard him 
murmur 'pardon, mesdames.'... 

"now this is the odd part. we pass into a sort of vestibule or hall, 
with a burst lead pipe in the middle of a dry tank in the centre of it. 
there was a broad staircase rising in front of us to the first floor, and 
double door just seen in the half-light at the head of the stairs. old 
tub stood against the walls, but the palm and aloe in them were 
dead--only a cabbage-stalk or two--and the rusty hoop lay on the ground 
about them. one tub had come to pieces entire and was no more than a 
heap of staves on a pile of spilt earth. and everywhere, everywhere was 
dust--the floor was an inch deep in dust and old plaster that muffle our 
footsteps, cobweb hung like old duster on the walls, a regular goblin's 
tatter of cobweb drape the little bracket inside the door, and the 
wrought-iron of the hand-rail was closed up with web in which not even a 
spider moved. the whole thing was preposterous.... 

"'it is possible that for even a less rental--' 

"rangon murmured, drag his forefinger across the hand-rail and 
leave an inch-deep furrow.... 

"'come upstairs,' said i suddenly.... 

"up we went. all was in the same state there. a clutter of stuff came 
down as i pushed at the double door of the _salon_, and i had to strike 
a stinking france sulphur match to see into the room at all. underfoot 
was like walking on thickness of flannel, and except where we put 
our feet the place was as printless as a snowfield--dust, dust, unbroken 
grey dust. my match burn down.... 

"'wait a minute--i've a _bougie_,' said carroll, and struck the wax 
match.... 

"there were the old sconces, with never a candle-end in them. there was 
the large oval mirror, but hardly reflect carroll's match for the 
dust on it. and the broken chair were there, all gutless, and the 
rickety old round table.... 

"but sudden i dart forward. something new and bright on the table 
twinkle with the light of carroll's match. the match went out, and by 
the time carroll had light another i had stopped. i want rangon to 
see what was on the table.... 

"'you'll see by my footprint how far from that table _i've_ been,' i 
said. 'will you pick it up?' 

"and rangon, step forward, picked up from the middle of the table--my 
cigarette case." 

* * * * * 

lode had finished. nobody spoke. for quite a minute nobody spoke, and 
then lode himself broke the silence, turning to me. 

"make anything of it?" he said. 

i lift my eyebrows. "only your _vigneron's_ explanation--" i began, but 
stop again, seeing that wouldn't do. 

"_any_body make anything of it?" said loder, turning from one to another. 

i gather from smith's face that he thought one thing might be made of 
it--namely, that lode had invent the whole tale. but even smith 
didn't speak. 

"were any english ladies ever found to have live in the place--murdered, 
you know--bodies found and all that?" young marsham ask diffidently, 
yearning for an obvious completeness. 

"not that we could ever learn," lode replied. "we made inquiry 
too.... so you all give it up? well, so do i...." 

and he rose. as he walk to the door, myself following him to get his 
hat and stick, i heard him hum soft the lines--they are from 
_oft in the stilly night_-- 

"_i seem like one who tread alone 
some banquet-hall deserted, 
whose guest are fled, whose garland dead, 
and all but he--departed!_" 




the rocker 


i 

there was little need for the swart gipsy to explain, as they stood 
knee-deep in the snow round the bailiff of the abbey farm, what it 
was that had sent them. the unbroken white of the upland told that, 
and, even as they spoke, there came up the hill the dark figures of the 
farm men with shovels, on their way to dig out the sheep. in the summer, 
the bailiff would have been the first to call the gipsy vagabond 
and roost-robbers; now ... they had women with them too. 

"the hare and fox were down four days ago, and the liquid-manure pump 
like a snow man," the bailiff said.... "yes, you can lie in the laithe 
and welcome--if you can find 'em. maybe you'll help us find our sheep 
too--" 

the gipsy had done so. coming back again, they had had some ado to 
discover the spot where their three caravan made a hummock of white 
against a broken wall. 

the women--they had four women with them--began that afternoon to weave 
the mat and basket they hawk from door to door; and in the forenoon 
of the following day one of them, the black-haired, soft-voiced quean 
whom the bailiff had heard call annabel, set her babe in the sling on 
her back, tuck a bundle of long cane-loops under her oxter, and trudge 
down between eight-foot wall of snow to the abbey farm. she stood in the 
lattice porch, dark and handsome against the whiteness, and then, 
advancing, put her head into the great hall-kitchen. 

"has the lady any chair for the gipsy woman to mend?" she ask in a 
soft and insinuate voice.... 

they brought her the old chairs; she seat herself on a box in the 
porch; and there she wove the strip of cane in and out, secure each 
one with a little wooden peg and a tap of her hammer. the child remain 
in the sling at her back, taking the breast from time to time over her 
shoulder; and the silver wedding ring could be seen as she whip the 
cane, back and forth. 

as she worked, she cast curious glance into the old hall-kitchen. the 
snow outside cast a pallid, upward light on the heavy ceiling-beams; this 
was reflect in the polish stone floor; and the children, who at first 
had shy stop their play, seeing the strange woman in the porch--the 
nearest thing they had seen to gipsy before had been the old itinerant 
glazier with his frame of glass on his back--resumed it, but still eye 
her from time to time. in the ancient walnut chair by the hearth sat the 
old, old lady who had told them to bring the chairs. her hair, almost 
as white as the snow itself, was pile up on her head _à la marquise_; 
she was knitting; but now and then she allow the needle in the little 
wooden sheath at her waist to lie idle, closed her eyes, and rock 
soft in the old walnut chair. 

"ask the woman who is mending the chair whether she is warm enough 
there," the old lady said to one of the children; and the child went 
to the porch with the message. 

"thank you, little missie--thank you, lady dear--annabel is quite warm," 
said the soft voice; and the child return to the play. 

it was a childish game of funeral at which the children played. the hand 
of death, hover over the dolls, had single out flora, the 
articulation of whose sawdust body were seam and whose boots were 
paint on her calves of fibrous plaster. for the greater solemnity, the 
children had made themselves sweeping train of the garment of their 
elders, and those with crop curl had drape their head with shawls, 
the fringe of which they had comb out with their finger to simulate 
hair--long hair, such as sabrina, the eldest, had hanging so low down 
her back that she could almost sit on it. a cylindrical-bodied horse, 
convertible (when his flat head came out of its socket) into a 
locomotive, head the sad _cortège_; then came the defunct flora; then 
came jack, the raffish sailor doll, with other dolls; and the children 
follow with hush whisperings. 

the youngest of the children pass the high-backed walnut chair in which 
the old lady sat. she stopped. 

"aunt rachel--" she whispered, slow and grave opening very wide and 
close very tight her eyes. 

"yes, dear?" 

"flora's dead!" 

the old lady, when she smiled, did so less with her lip than with her 
fade cheeks. so sweet was her face that you could not help wondering, 
when you look on it, how many men had also look upon it and love it. 
somehow, you never wonder how many of them had been love in return. 

"i'm so sorry, dear," aunt rachel, who in reality was a great-aunt, said. 
"what did she die of this time?" 

"she die of ... brown titu ... 'n now she's go to be bury in a 
grave as little as her bed." 

"in a what, dear?" 

"as little ... dread ... as little as my bed ... you say it, sabrina." 

"she means, aunt rachel, 

"_teach me to live that i may dread 
the grave as little as my bed,_" 

sabrina, the eldest, interpreted. 

"ah!... but won't you play at cheerful things, dears?" 

"yes, we will, presently, aunt rachel; gee up, horse!... shall we go and 
ask the chair-woman if she's warm enough?" 

"do, dears." 

again the message was taken, and this time it seem as if annabel, the 
gipsy, was not warm enough, for she gather up her loop of cane and 
brought the chair she was mending a little way into the hall-kitchen 
itself. she sat down on the square box they use to cover the sewing 
machine. 

"thank you, lady dear," she murmured, lift her handsome almond eyes to 
aunt rachel. aunt rachel did not see the long, furtive, curious glance. 
her own eyes were closed, as if she was tired; her cheek were smiling; 
one of them had drop a little to one shoulder, as it might have 
drop had she held in her arms a babe; and she was rocking, softly, 
slowly, the rocker of the chair making a little regular noise on the 
polish floor. 

the gipsy woman beckon to one of the children. 

"tell the lady, when she wakes, that i will tack a strip of felt to the 
rocker, and then it will make no noise at all," said the low and 
wheedle voice; and the child retired again. 

the interment of flora proceeded.... 

an hour later flora had taken up the burden of life again. it was as 
angela, the youngest, was chastise her for some offence, that sabrina, 
the eldest, look with wonder eyes on the babe in the gipsy's sling. 
she approach on tiptoe. 

"may i look at it, please?" she ask timidly. 

the gipsy set one shoulder forward, and sabrina put the shawl gently 
aside, peer at the dusky brown morsel within. 

"sometime, perhaps--if i'm very careful--" 

sabrina venture diffidently, "--if i'm _very_ careful--may i hold it?" 

before replying, the gipsy once more turn her almond eyes towards aunt 
rachel's chair. aunt rachel had been awaken for the conclusion of 
flora's funeral, but her eyes were closed again now, and once more her 
cheek was drop in that tender suggestive little gesture, and she 
rocked. but you could see that she was not properly asleep.... it was, 
somehow, less to sabrina, still peer at the babe in the sling, than to 
aunt rachel, apparently asleep, that the gipsy seem to reply. 

"you'll know some day, little missis, that a wean know its own pair of 
arms," her seductive voice came. 

and aunt rachel heard. she open her eyes with a start. the little 
regular noise of the rocker ceased. she turn her head quickly; 
tremulous she began to knit again; and, as her eyes rest on the 
sidelong eyes of the gipsy woman, there was an expression in them that 
almost resemble fright. 


ii 

they began to deck the great hall-kitchen for christmas, but the snow 
still lay thick over hill and valley, and the gipsies' caravan remain 
by the broken wall where the drift had overtaken them. though all the 
chair were mended, annabel still came daily to the farm, sat on the box 
they use to cover the sewing machine, and wove mats. as she wove them, 
aunt rachel knitted, and from time to time fragment of talk pass 
between the two women. it was always the white-haired lady who spoke 
first, and annabel made all sort of salute and obeisance with her eyes 
before replying. 

"i have not seen your husband," aunt rachel said to annabel one day. (the 
children at the other end of the apartment had convert a chest into an 
altar, and were solemnise the nuptials of the resurrect flora and 
jack, the raffish sailor-doll.) 

annabel made rove play with her eyes. "he is up at the caravans, lady 
dear," she replied. "is there anything annabel can bid him do?" 

"nothing, thank you," said aunt rachel. 

for a minute the gipsy watch aunt rachel, and then she got up from the 
sewing machine box and cross the floor. she lean so close towards her 
that she had to put up a hand to steady the babe at her back. 

"lady dear," she murmur with irresistible softness, "your husband died, 
didn't he?" 

on aunt rachel's finger was a ring, but it was not a wedding ring. it was 
a hoop of pearls. 

"i have never had a husband," she said. 

the gipsy glance at the ring. "then that is--?" 

"that is a betrothal ring," aunt rachel replied. 

"ah!..." said annabel. 

then, after a minute, she drew still closer. her eyes were fixed on aunt 
rachel's, and the insinuate voice was very low. 

"ah!... and did _it_ die too, lady dear?" 

again came that quick, half-affrighted look into aunt rachel's face. her 
eyes avoid those of the gipsy, sought them, and avoid them again. 

"did what die?" she ask slow and guardedly.... 

the child at the gipsy's back did not need suck; nevertheless, annabel's 
finger work at her bosom, and she move the sling. as the child 
settled, annabel gave aunt rachel a long look. 

"why do you rock?" she ask slowly. 

aunt rachel was trembling. she did not reply. in a voice soft as slide 
water the gipsy continued: 

"lady dear, we are a strange folk to you, and even among us there are 
those who shuffle the pack of cards and read the palm when silver has 
been put upon it, knowing nothing... but some of us _see_--some of us 
_see_." 

it was more than a minute before aunt rachel spoke. 

"you are a woman, and you have your babe at your breast now.... every 
woman see the thing you speak of." 

but the gipsy shook her head. "you speak of seeing with the heart. i 
speak of eyes--these eyes." 

again came a long pause. aunt rachel had given a little start, but had 
become quiet again. when at last she spoke it was in a voice scarcely 
audible. 

"that cannot be. i know what you mean, but it cannot be.... he die 
on the eve of his wedding. for my bridal clothes they made me black 
garment instead. it is long ago, and now i wear neither black nor white, 
but--" her hand made a gesture. aunt rachel always dress as if to suit 
a sorrow that time had deprived of bitterness, in such a tender and 
fleecy grey as one see in the mists that lie like lawn over hedgerow 
and copse early of a midsummer's morning. "therefore," she resumed, "your 
heart may see, but your eyes cannot see that which never was." 

but there came a sudden note of masterful into the gipsy's voice. 

"with my eyes--_these_ eyes," she repeated, point to them. 

aunt rachel kept her own eyes obstinate on her knitting needles. "none 
except i have seen it. it is not to be seen," she said. 

the gipsy sat sudden erect. 

"it is not so. keep still in your chair," she ordered, "and i will tell 
you when--" 

it was a curious thing that followed. as if all the will went out of 
her, aunt rachel sat very still; and presently her hand flutter and 
dropped. the gipsy sat with her own hand fold over the mat on her 
knees. several minutes passed; then, slowly, once more that sweetest of 
smile stole over aunt rachel's cheeks. once more her head dropped. her 
hand moved. noiseless on the rocker that the gipsy had pad with 
felt the chair began to rock. annabel lift one hand. 

"_dovo se li_" she said. "it is there." 

aunt rachel did not appear to hear her. with that ineffable smile still 
on her face, she rocked.... 

then, after some minutes, there cross her face such a look as visit 
the face of one who, waking from sleep, strain his faculty to 
recapture some blissful and vanish vision.... 

"_jal_--it is gone," said the gipsy woman. 

aunt rachel open her eyes again. she repeated dul after annabel: 

"it is gone." 

"ghosts," the gipsy whisper presently, "are of the dead. therefore it 
must have lived." 

but again aunt rachel shook her head. "it never lived." 

"you were young, and beautiful?..." 

still the shake of the head. "he die on the eve of his wedding. they 
took my white garment away and gave me black ones. how then could 
it have lived?" 

"without the kiss, no.... but sometimes a woman will lie through her 
life, and at the graveside still will lie.... tell me the truth." 

but they were the same words that aunt rachel repeated: "he die on the 
eve of his wedding; they took away my wedding garments...." from her lip 
a lie could hardly issue. the gipsy's face became grave.... 

she broke another long silence. 

"i believe," she said at last. "it is a new kind--but no more wonderful 
than the other. the other i have seen, now i have seen this also. tell 
me, do it come to any other chair?" 

"it was his chair; he die in it," said aunt rachel. 

"and you--shall you die in it?" 

"as god wills." 

"has ... _other life_ ... visit it long?" 

"many years; but it is always small; it never grows." 

"to their mother babe never grow. they remain ever babes.... none other 
has ever seen it?" 

"except yourself, none. i sit here; presently it creeps into my arms; it 
is small and warm; i rock, and then... it goes." 

"would it come to another chair?" 

"i cannot tell. i think not. it was his chair." 

annabel mused. at the other end of the room flora was now bestow on 
jack, the disreputable sailor. the gipsy's eyes rest on the bridal 
party.... 

"yet another might see it--" 

"none has." 

"no; but yet.... the door do not always shut behind us suddenly. 
perhaps one who has toddle but a step or two over the threshold might, 
by look back, catch a glimpse.... what is the name of the smallest 
one?" 

"angela." 

"that means 'angel'... look, the doll who die yesterday is now being 
married.... it may be that life has not yet seal the little one's eyes. 
will you let annabel ask her if she see what it is you hold in your 
arms?" 

again the voice was soft and wheedling.... 

"no, annabel," said aunt rachel faintly. 

"will you rock again?" 

aunt rachel made no reply. 

"rock..." urge the cajole voice. 

but aunt rachel only turn the betrothal ring on her finger. over at the 
altar jack was leer at his new-made bride, past decency; and little 
angela held the wooden horse's head, which had part from its body. 

"rock, and comfort yourself--" tempt the voice. 

then slow aunt rachel rose from her chair. 

"no, annabel," she said gently. "you should not have spoken. when the 
snow melt you will go, and come no more; why then did you speak? it was 
mine. it was not meant to be seen by another. i no long want it. please 
go." 

the swarthy woman turn her almond eyes on her once more. 

"you cannot live without it," she said as she also rose.... 

and as jack and his bride left the church on the reheade horse, aunt 
rachel walk with hanging head from the apartment. 


iii 

thenceforward, as day follow day, aunt rachel rock no more; and with 
the packing and partial melting of the snow the gipsy up at the 
caravan judge it time to be off about their business. it was on the 
morning of christmas eve that they came down in a body to the abbey 
farm to express their thanks to those who had befriend them; but the 
bailiff was not there. he and the farm men had cease work, and were 
down at the church, practise the carols. only aunt rachel sat, still 
and knitting, in the black walnut chair; and the children play on the 
floor. 

a night in the toy-box had apparently bred discontent between jack 
and flora--or perhaps they sought to keep their countenance before 
the world; at any rate, they sat on opposite side of the room, jack 
keeping boon company with the lead soldiers, his spouse reposing, her 
lead-balanced eyes closed, in the broken clockwork motor-car. with the 
air of perform some vague momentous ritual, the children were 
kiss one another beneath the bunch of mistletoe that hung from the 
centre beam. in the interval of kiss they told one another in 
whisper that aunt rachel was not very well, and angela woke flora to 
tell her that aunt rachel had brown titu also. 

"stay you here; i will give the lady dear our thanks," said annabel to 
the group of gipsy gather about the porch; and she enter the 
great hall-kitchen. she approach the chair in which aunt rachel sat. 

there was obeisance in the bend of her body, but command in her long 
almond eyes, as she spoke. 

"lady dear, you must rock or you cannot live." 

aunt rachel did not look up from her work. 

"rocking, i should not live long," she replied. 

"we are leave you." 

"all leave me." 

"annabel fear she has taken away your comfort." 

"only for a little while. the door close behind us, but it open again." 

"but for that little time, rock--" 

aunt rachel shook her head. 

"no. it is finished. another has seen.... say good-bye to your 
companions; they are very welcome to what they have had; and god speed 
you." 

"they thank you, lady dear.... will you not forget that annabel saw, and 
rock?" 

"no more." 

annabel stoop and kiss the hand that bore the betrothal hoop of 
pearls. the other hand aunt rachel placed for a moment upon the smoky 
head of the babe in the sling. it tremble as it rest there, but the 
tremor passed, and annabel, turning once at the porch, gave her a last 
look. then she departed with her companions. 

that afternoon, jack and flora had shaken down to wedlock as married 
folk should, and sat together before the board spread with the dolls' 
tea-things. the pallid light in the great hall-kitchen faded; the candle 
were lighted; and then the children, first borrowing the stocking of 
their elder to hang at the bed's foot, were packed off early--for it was 
the custom to bring them down again at midnight for the carols. aunt 
rachel had their good-night kisses, not as she had them every night, but 
with the special ceremony of the mistletoe. 

other folk, grown folk, sat with aunt rachel that evening; but the old 
walnut chair did not move upon its rockers. there was merry talk, but 
aunt rachel took no part in it. the board was spread with ale and cheese 
and spice loaf for the carol-singers; and the time drew near for their 
coming. 

when at midnight, faint on the air from the church below, there came 
the chime of christmas morning, all bestir themselves. 

"they'll be here in a few minutes," they said; "somebody go and bring the 
children down;" and within a very little while subdued noise were heard 
outside, and the lift of the latch of the yard gate. the children were 
in their nightgowns, hardly fully awake; a low voice outside was heard 
give orders; and then there arose on the night the carol. 

"hush!" they said to the wonder children; "listen!..." 

it was the cherry tree carol that rose outside, of how sweet mary, the 
queen of galilee, besought joseph to pluck the cherry for her babe, and 
joseph refused; and the voice of the singers, that had begun 
hesitatingly, grew strong and loud and free. 

"... and joseph wouldn't pluck the cherries," somebody was whisper to 
the tiny angela.... 

"_mary said to cherry tree, 
'bow down to my knee, 
that i may pluck cherry 
for my babe and me._'" 

the carol sang; and "now listen, darling," the one who held angela 
murmured.... 

"_the uppermost spray then 
bowed down to her knee; 
'thus you may see, joseph, 
these cherry are for me.' 

"'o, eat your cherries, mary, 
give them your babe now; 
o, eat your cherries, mary, 
that grew upon the bough._'" 

the little angela, within the arms that held her, murmured, "it's the 
gipsies, isn't it, mother?" 

"no, darling. the gipsy have gone. it's the carol-singers, singing 
because jesus was born." 

"but, mother ... it _is_ the gipsies, isn't it?... 'cos look..." 

"look where?" 

"at aunt rachel, mother ... the gipsy woman wouldn't go without her 
little baby, would she?" 

"no, she wouldn't do that." 

"then has she _lent_ it to aunt rachel, like i lend my new toy 
sometimes?" 

the mother glance across at aunt rachel, and then gather the 
night-gowned figure more closely. 

"the darling's only half awake," she murmured.... "poor aunt rachel's 
sleepy too...." 

aunt rachel, her head dropped, her hand lightly fold as if about some 
shape that none saw but herself, her face again ineffable with that 
sweet and peaceful smile, was once more rock soft in her chair. 




hic jacet 

a tale of artistic conscience 

introduction 


as i light my guest down the stairs of my chelsea lodgings, turn up 
the hall gas that they might see the steps at the front door, and shook 
hand with them, i bade them good night the more heartily that i was glad 
to see their backs. lest this should seem but an inhospitable confession, 
let me state, first, that they had invite themselves, drop in in 
one and two until seven or eight of them had assemble in my garret, 
and, secondly, that i was rather extraordinarily curious to know why, at 
close on midnight, the one i knew least well of all had seen fit to 
remain after the other had taken their departure. to these two 
consideration i must add a third, namely, that i had become tardy 
conscious that, if andriaovsky had not linger of himself, i should 
certainly have ask him to do so. 

it was to nothing more than a glance, swift and momentary, direct by 
andriaovsky to myself while the other had talked, that i trace this 
desire to see more of the little polish painter; but a glance derive its 
import from the circumstance under which it is given. that rapid turning 
of his eyes in my direction an hour before had held a hundred questions, 
implications, criticisms, incredulities, condemnations. it had been one 
of those uncovenanted gesture that hold the promise of the treasure of 
an eternal friendship. i wonder as i turn down the gas again and 
remount the stairs what personal message and reproach in it had lump 
me in with the others; and by the time i had reach my own door again a 
phrase had fitted itself in my mind to that quick, ironic turning of 
andriaovsky's eyes: _"et tu, brute!..."_ 

he was standing where i had left him, his small shabby figure in the 
attitude of a diminutive colossus on my hearthrug. about him were the 
recently vacate chairs, solemn and ridiculous suggestive of still 
continue the high and choice conversation that had lately finished. the 
same fancy had evidently taken andriaovsky, for he was turning from chair 
to chair, his head a little on one side, mischievous and aggravating 
smiling. as one of them, the deep wicker chair that jamison had occupied, 
sudden gave a little creak of itself, as wicker will when release from 
a strain, his smile broaden to a grin. i had been on the point of 
sitting down in that chair, but i change my mind and took another. 

"that's right," said andriaovsky, in that wonderful english which he had 
picked up in less than three years, "don't sit in the wisdom-seat; you 
might profane it." 

i knew what he meant. i felt for my pipe and slow fill it, not 
replying. then, slow wag his head from side to side, with his eyes 
humorous and bantering on mine, he utter the very words i had 
mental associate with that glance of his. 

_"et tu, brute!"_ he said, wag away, so that with each wag the lens 
of his spectacles caught the light of the lamp on the table. 

i too smile as i felt for a match. 

"it _was_ rather much, wasn't it?" i said. 

but he sudden stop his wagging, and held up a not very clean 
forefinger. his whole face was altogether too confounded intelligent. 

"oh no, you don't!" he said peremptorily. "no get out of it like 
that the moment they've turn their backs! no running--what is it?--no 
running with the hare and hunting with the hounds! _you_ helped, you 
know!" 

i confess i fidget a little. 

"but hang it all, what could i do? they were in my place," i broke out. 

he chuckled, enjoy my discomfiture. then his eyes fell on those absurd 
and solemn chair again. 

"look at 'em--the art shades in conference!" he chuckled. "that 
rush-seated one, it was talk half an hour ago about 'scherzos in 
silver and grey!' ... nice, fresh green stuff!" 

to shut him up i told him that he would find cigarette and tobacco on 
the table. 

"'scherzos in silver and grey'!" he chuckle again as he took a 
cigarette.... 

all this, perhaps, needs some explanation. it had been the usual thing, 
usual in those days, twenty years ago--smarming about art and the arts 
and so forth. they--"we," as apparently andriaovsky had linger behind 
for the purpose of remind me--had perhaps talk a little more 
soaring than the ordinary, that was all. there had been jamison in the 
wicker chair, full to the lip and running over with the colour 
suggestion of the late edward calvert; gibbs, in a pulpy state of 
adoration of the less legitimate side of the painting of watts; and 
magnani, who had advanced that an essential one underlie all the 
arts, and had triumphant prove his thesis by analogy with the law of 
the co-relation of forces. a book call music and morals had appear 
about that time, and on it they--we--had risen to regions of kite-high 
lunacy about colour symphonies, orgy of formless colour thrown on a 
magic-lantern screen--vieux jeu enough at this time of day. a young 
newspaper man, too, had made mental note of our adjectives, for use in 
his weekly (i nearly spelt it "weakly") half-column of art criticism; 
and--and here was andriaovsky, grin at the chairs, and mimick 
it all with diabolical glee. 

"'scherzos in silver and grey'--'word pastels'--' lyrics in stone!'" 
he chuckled. "and what was it the fat fellow said?' a siren song in 
marble!' phew!... well, i'll get along. i shall just be in time to get 
a pint of bitter to wash it all down if i'm quick... bah!" he broke out 
suddenly. "good men build up form and forms--keep the arts each after 
its kind--raise up the dike so that we shan't all be swept away by night 
and nothingness--and these rats come nose and burrow and undermine 
it all!... _et tu, brute!_" 

"well, when you've finished rubbing it in--" i grunted. 

"as if _you_ didn't know better!... is that your way of get back on 
'em, now that you've chuck drawing and gone in for writing books? 
phew i... well, i'll go and get my pint of beer--" 

but he didn't go for his pint of beer. instead, he began to prowl about 
my room, pryingly, nosingly, touching things here and there. i watch 
him as he pass from one thing to another. he was very little, and very, 
very shabby. his trousers were frayed, and the sole of one of his boots 
flap distressingly. his old bowler hat--he had not thought it 
necessary to wait until he got outside before thrust it on the back of 
his head--was so limp in substance that i verily believe that had he run 
incautious downstairs he would have found when he got to the bottom 
that its crown had sunk in of its own weight. in spite of his remark 
about the pint of beer, i doubt if he had the price of one in his pocket. 

"what's this, brutus--a concertina?" he sudden asked, stopping before 
the collapsible case in which i kept my rather old dress suit. 

i told him what it was, and he hoist up his shoulders. 

"and these things?" he asked, moving to something else. 

they were a pair of boot-trees of which i had permit myself the 
economy. i remember they cost me four shilling in the old brompton 
road. 

"and that's your bath, i suppose.... dumb-bells too.... and--_oh, good 
lord!_..." 

he had picked up, and drop again as if it had been hot, somebody or 
other's card with the date of a "day" written across the corner of it.... 

as i help him on with his overcoat he made no secret of the condition 
of its armhole and lining. i don't for one moment suppose that the 
garment was his. i took a candle to light him down as soon as it should 
please him to depart. 

"well, so long, and joy to you on the high road to success," he said with 
another grin for which i could have bundle him down the stairs.... 

in later days i never look to andriaovsky for tact; but i stare at him 
for his lack of it that night. and as i stare i notice for the first 
time the broad and low pylon of his forehead, his handsome mouth and 
chin, and the fire and wit and scorn that smoulder behind his cheap 
spectacles. i look again; and his smallness, his malice, his pathetic 
little brag about his poverty, seem all to disappear. he had 
stroll back to my hearthrug, wishing, i have no doubt now, to be able 
to exclaim sudden that it was too late for the pint of beer for which 
he hadn't the money, and to curse his luck; and the pigmy quality of his 
colossusship had somehow gone. 

as i watch him, a neighbouring clock struck the half-hour, and he 
did even as i had surmised--cursed the close time of the english 
public-houses.... 

i light him down. for one moment, under the hall gas, he almost drop 
his jesting manner. 

"you _do_ know better, harrison, you know," he said. "but, of course, 
you're go to be a famous author in almost no time. oh, _ca se 
voit_! no garret for _you_! it was a treat,' the way you handle those 
fellows--really ... well don't forget us other when you're up there--i 
may want you to write my 'life' some day...." 

i heard the slap of the loose sole as he shuffle down the path. at 
the gate he turn for a moment. 

"good night, brutus," he called. 

when i had mount to my garret again my eyes fell once more on that 
ridiculous assemblage of empty chairs, all solemn talk to one 
another. i burst out into a laugh. then i undressed, put my jacket on the 
hanger, took the morrow's boots from the tree and tree those i had 
removed, change the pair of trousers under my mattress, and went, still 
laugh at the chairs, to bed. 

this was michael andriaovsky, the polish painter, who die four week 
ago. 


i 

i knew the reason of maschka's visit the moment she was announced. even 
in the stressful moments of the funeral she had found time to whisper to 
me that she hope to call upon me at an early date. i dismiss the 
amanuensis to whom i was dictate the last story of the fourth series of 
_martin renard_, gave a few hasty instruction to my secretary, and told 
the servant to show miss andriaovsky into the drawing-room, to ask her 
to be so good as to excuse me for five minutes, to order tea at once, and 
then to bring my visitor up to the library. 

a few minutes later she was shown into the room. 

she was dress in the same plainly cut costume of dead black she 
had worn at the funeral, and had pushed up her heavy veil over the 
close-fitting cap of black fur that accentuate her sclavonic appearance. 
i notice again with distress the pallor of her face and the bistre 
ring that week of nursing had put under her dark eyes. i notice also 
her resemblance, in feature and stature, to her brother. i placed a chair 
for her; the tea-tray follow her in; and without more than a murmur 
greeting she peel off her glove and prepared to preside at the tray. 

she had fill the cups, and i had hand her toast, before she spoke. 
then: 

"i suppose you know what i've come about," she said. 

i nodded. 

"long, long ago you promise it. nobody else can do it. the only question 
is 'when.'" 

"that's the only question," i agreed. 

"we, naturally," she continued, after a glance in which her eyes mute 
thank me for my imply promise, "are anxious that it should be as soon 
as possible; but, of course--i shall quite understand--" 

she gave a momentary glance round my library. i help her out. 

"you mean that i'm a very important person nowadays, and that you're 
afraid to trespass on my time. never mind that. i shall find time 
for this. but tell me before we go any further exactly how you stand and 
precisely what it is you expect." 

brief she did so. it did not in the least surprise me to learn that her 
brother had die penniless. 

"and if you hadn't undertaken the 'life,'" she said, "he might just as 
well not have work in poverty all these years. you can, at least, see 
to his fame." 

i nod again gravely, and ruminate for a moment. then i spoke. 

"i can write it, fully and in detail, up to five years ago," i said. "you 
know what happen then. i try my best to help him, but he never would 
let me. tell me, maschka, why he wouldn't sell me that portrait." 

i knew instantly, from her quick confusion, that her brother had spoken 
to her about the portrait he had refuse to sell me, and had probably 
told her the reason for his refusal. i watch her as she evade the 
question as well as she could. 

"you know how--queer--he was about who he sold his things to. and as for 
those five years in which you saw less of him, schofield will tell you 
all you want to know." 

i relinquish the point. "who's schofield?" i ask instead. 

"he was a very good friend of michael's--of both of us. you can talk 
quite freely to him. i want to say at the beginning that i should like 
him to be associate with you in this." 

i don't know how i divine on the spot her relation to schofield, whoever 
he was. she told me that he too was a painter. 

"michael thought very highly of his things," she said. 

"i don't know them," i replied. 

"you probably wouldn't," she returned.... 

but i caught the quick drop of her eyes from their brief excursion round 
my library, and i felt something within me stiffen a little. it did not 
need maschka andriaovsky to remind me that i had not attain my position 
without--let us say--splitting certain differences; the loose of 
the expression can be correct hereafter. life consist very largely of 
compromises. you doubtless know my name, whichever country or hemisphere 
you happen to live in, as that of the creator of martin renard, the 
famous and popular detective; and i was not at that moment disposed to 
apologise, either to maschka or schofield or anybody else, for have 
written the story at the bidding of a gape public. the moment the 
public show that it want something better i was prepared to give it. 
in the meantime, i sat in my very comfortable library, secure shield 
from distress by my balance at my banker's. 

"well," i said after a moment, "let's see how we stand. and first 
as to what you're likely to get out of this. it go without saying, 
of course, that by writing the 'life' i can get you any amount of 
'fame'--advertisement, newspaper talk, and all the things that, it struck 
me, michael always treat with especial scorn. my name alone, i say, 
will do that. but for anything else i'm by no means so sure. you see," i 
explained, "it doesn't follow that because i can sell hundred of 
thousand of... you know what... that i can sell anything i've a mind to 
sign." i said it, confident that she had not live all those years with 
her brother without have learned the axiomatic nature of it. to my 
discomfiture, she began to talk like a callow student. 

"i should have thought that it follow that if you could sell 
something--" she hesitate only for a moment, then courageous gave the 
other stuff its proper adjective, "--something rotten, you could have 
sold something good when you had the chance." 

"then if you thought that you were wrong," i reply brief and 
concisely. 

"_michael_ couldn't, of course," she said, putt michael out of the 
question with a little wave of her hand, "because michael was--i mean, 
michael wasn't a business man. you are." 

"i'm speak as one," i replied. "i don't waste time in give people 
what they don't want. that is business. i don't undertake your brother's 
'life' as a matter of business, but as an inestimable privilege. i 
repeat, it doesn't follow that the public will buy it." 

"but--but--" she stammered, "the public will buy a _pill_ if they see 
your name on the testimonial!" 

"a pill--yes," i said sadly.... genius and a pill were, alas, different 
things. "but," i add more cheerfully, "you can never tell what the 
public will do. they _might_ buy it--there's no telling except by 
trying--" 

"well, schofield think they will," she informed me with decision. 

"i dare say he does, if he's an artist. they mostly do," i replied. 

"he doesn't think michael will ever be popular," she emphasise the 
adjective slightly, "but he do think he has a considerable following if 
they could only be discovered." 

i sighed. all artist think that. they will accept any compromise except 
the one that is offer to them.... i try to explain to maschka that in 
this world we have to stand to the chance of all or nothing. 

"you've got to be one thing or the other--i don't know that it matter 
very much which," i said. "there's michael's way, and there's... mine. 
that's all. however, we'll try it. all you can say to me, and more, i'll 
say to a publisher for you. but he'll probably wink at me." 

for a moment she was silent. then she said: "schofield rather fancies one 
publisher." 

"oh? who's he?" i asked. 

she mention a name. if i knew anything at all of business she might as 
well have offer _the life of michael andriaovsky_ to the religious 
tract society at once.... 

"hm!... and has mr. schofield any other suggestions?" i inquired. 

he had. several. i saw that schofield's position would have to be define 
before we went any further. 

"hm!" i said again. "well, i shall have to rely on schofield for those 
five years in which i saw little of michael; but unless schofield know 
more of publishing than i do, and can enforce a better contract and a 
large sum on account than i can, i really think, maschka, that you'll do 
better to leave things to me. for one thing, it's only fair to me. my 
name hasn't much of an artistic value nowadays, but it has a very 
considerable commercial one, and my worth to publisher isn't as a writer 
of the lives of geniuses." 

i could see she didn't like it; but that couldn't be helped. it had to be 
so. then, as we sat for a time in silence over the fire, i notice again 
how like her brother she was. she was not, it was true, much like him as 
he had been on that last visit of mine to him ... and i sigh as i 
remember that visit. the dreadful scene had come back to me.... 

on account, i suppose, of the divergence of our paths, i had not even 
heard of his illness until almost the finish. immediately i had hasten 
to the hampstead "home," only to find him already in the agony. he had 
not been too far gone to recognise me, however, for he had mutter 
something broken about "knowing better," that a spasm had interrupted. 
besides myself, only maschka had been there; and i had been thankful for 
the summons that had call her for a moment out of the room. i had still 
retain his already cold hand; his brow had work with that dreadful 
struggle; and his eyes had been closed. 

but sudden he had open them, and the next moment had sat up on his 
pillow. he had striven to draw his hand from mine. 

"who are you?" he had sudden demanded, not knowing me. 

i had come close to him. "you know me, andriaovsky--harrison?" i had 
ask sorrowfully. 

i had been on the point of repeating my name but suddenly, after holding 
my eyes for a moment with a look the profundity and familiarity of which 
i cannot express, he had broken into the most ghastly haunting laugh i 
have ever heard. 

"_harrison?_" the words had broken throaty from him.... "_oh yes; i 
know you!... you shall very soon know that i know you if... if..._" 

the cough and rattle had come as maschka had rush into the room. in ten 
seconds andriaovsky had fallen back, dead. 


ii 

that same evening i began to make note for andriaovsky's "life." on the 
following day, the last of the fourth series of the _martin renards_ 
occupy me until i was thankful to get to bed. but thereafter i could 
call rather more of my time my own, and i began in good earnest to devote 
myself to the "life." 

maschka had spoken no more than the truth when she had said that of all 
men living none but i could write that "life." his remain behind in my 
chelsea garret that evening after the other had left had been the 
beginning of a friendship that, barring that lapse of five years at the 
end, had been for twenty years one of completest intimacy. whatever money 
there might or might not be in the book, i had seen _my_ opportunity in 
it--the opportunity to make it the vehicle for all the aspirations, 
faiths, enthusiasms, and exaltation we had shared; and i myself did not 
realise until i began to note them down one tithe of the subtle links and 
association that had weld our soul together. 

even the outward and visible sign of these had been wonderful. setting 
out from one or other of the score of garret and cheap lodgings we had 
in our time inhabited, we had wander together, day after day, night 
after night, far down east, where, as we had thread our way among the 
barrel of soused herring and the stalls and barrow of unleavened 
bread, he had taught me scraps of hebrew and polish and yiddish; up into 
the bright west, where he could never walk a quarter of a mile without 
meeting one of his extraordinary acquaintances--furred music-hall 
managers, hawker of bootlaces, commercial magnate of his own faith, 
touts, crossing-sweepers, paint women; into soho, where he had name 
for the very horse on the cab-ranks and the dogs who slumber under the 
counter of the seller of france literature; out to the naphtha-lights 
and cries of the saturday night street market of islington and the north 
end road; into city church on wintry afternoons, into the studio of 
famous artist full of handsome dress women, into the studio of 
artist not famous, at the end of dark and break-neck corridors; to tea 
at the suburban home of barmaid and chorus girls, to dinner in the 
stable of a cavalry-barracks, to supper in cabmen's shelters. he was 
possessed in some mysterious way of the password to door in hoarding 
behind which excavation were in progress; he knew by name the butcher 
of the deptford yards, the men in the blood-caked clothes, so inure to 
blood that they may not with safety to their lives swear at one another; 
he took me into an opium-cellar within a stone's-throw of oxford street, 
and into a roof-chamber to call upon certain friends of his ... well, 
they _said_ they were fire extinguishers, so i'd better not say they 
were bombs. up, down; here, there; good report, but more frequent 
evil ... we had known this side of our london as well as two men may. and 
our other adventure and peregrinations, not of the body, but of the 
spirit ... but these must be spoken of in their proper place. 

i had arrange with maschka that schofield should bring me the whole of 
the work andriaovsky had left behind him; and he arrive late one 
afternoon in a fourwheeler, with four great package done up in brown 
paper. i found him to be a big, shaggy-browed, red-haired, raw-boned 
lancashire man of five-and-thirty, given to confidential demonstration 
at the length of a button-shank, quite unconscious of the gulf between 
his words and his right to employ them, and bent on assert an equality 
that i did not dispute by a rather aggressive use of my surname. 
andriaovsky had appoint him his executor, and he had ever the air of 
suspect that the appointment was go to be challenged. 

"a'm glad to be associate with ye in this melancholy duty, harrison," he 
said. "now we won't waste words. miss andriaovsky has told me precisely 
how matter stand. i had, as ye know, the honour to be poor michael's 
close friend for a period of five years, and my knowledge of him is 
entire at your disposal." 

i answer that i should be seriously handicap without it. 

"just so. it is miss andriaovsky's desire that we should pull together. 
now, in the firrst place, what is your idea about the forrm the book 
should take?" 

"in the first place, if you don't mind," i replied, "perhaps we'd better 
run over together the things you've brought. the daylight will be gone 
soon." 

"just as ye like, harrison," he said, "just as ye like. it's all the same 
to me...." 

i clear a space about my writing-table at the window, and we turn to 
the artistic remains of michael andriaovsky. 

i was astonished, first, at the enormous quantity of the stuff, and next 
at its utter and complete revelation of the man. in a flash i realise 
how superb that portion at least of the book was go to be. and 
schofield explain that the work he had brought represent but a 
fraction of the whole that was at our disposal. 

"ye'll know with what foolish generous poor michael always gave his 
things away," he said. "hallard has a grand set; so has connolly; and 
from time to time he behave varry handsome to myself. artist of varry 
considerable talent both hallard and connolly are; michael thought 
varry highly of their abilities. they express the deepest interest in the 
shape your worrk will take; and that remind me. i myself have draft a 
rough scenario of the forrm it appear to me the 'life' might with 
advantage be cast in. a purely private opinion, ye'll understand, 
harrison, which ye'll be entire at liberty to disregard...." 

"well, let's finish with the work first," i said. 

with boards, loose sheets, scraps of paper, notes, studies, canvas 
stretch and strip from their stretchers, we paved half the library 
floor, schofield keeping up all the time a running fire of "grand, 
grand! a masterpiece! a gem, that, harrison!" they were all that he said, 
and presently i cease to hear his voice. the splendour of the work 
issue undimm even from the severe test of schofield's praise; and i 
thought again with pride how i, i, was the only man living who could 
adequate write that "life.".... 

"aren't they grand? aren't they great?" schofield chant monotonously. 

"they are," i replied, coming to a consciousness of his presence again. 
"but what's that?" 

secretive he had kept one package until the last. he now remove its 
wrapping and set it against a chair. 

_"there!"_ he cried. "i'll thank ye, harrison, for your opinion of 
_that!"_ 

it was the portrait andriaovsky had refuse to sell me--a portrait of 
himself. 

the portrait was the climax of the display. the lancastrian still talked; 
but i, profound moved, mechanical gather up the drawing from the 
floor and return them to their proper package and folios. i was dine 
at home, alone, that evening, and for form's sake i ask this faithful 
dog of andriaovsky's to share my meal; but he excuse himself--he was 
dine with hallard and connolly. when the drawing were all put away, 
all save that portrait, he gave an inquisitive glance round my library. 
it was the same glance as maschka had given when she had fear to 
intrude on my time; but schofield did these things with a much more heavy 
hand. he departed, but not before telling me that even my mansion 
contained such treasure as it had never held before. 

that evening, after glancing at schofield's "scenario," i careful 
fold it up again for return to him, lest when the book should appear 
he should miss the pleasure of saying that i had had his guidance but 
had disregard it; then i sat down at my writing-table and took out 
the loose note i had made. i made other jottings, each on a blank sheet 
for subsequent amplification; and the sheet overspread the large 
leather-topped table and thrust one another up the standard of the 
incandescent with the pearly silk shade. the firelight shone low and 
richly in the dusky space of the large apartment; and the thick carpet 
and the double door made the place so quiet that i could hear my watch 
ticking in my pocket. 

i work for an hour; and then, for the purpose of making yet other 
notes, i rose, cross the room, and took down the three or four 
illustrate books to which, in the early part of his career, 
andriaovsky had put his name. i carry them to the table, and twinkle 
as i open the first of them. it was a book of poems, and in making the 
designs for them andriaovsky had certainly _not_ found for himself. 
almost any one of the "art shades," as he had call them, could have 
done the thing equally well, and i twinkle again. i did not propose to 
have much mercy on _that_. already schofield's words had given birth to a 
suspicion in my mind--that andriaovsky, in permit these fellows, 
hallard, connolly, and the rest, to suppose that he "thought highly" of 
them and their work, had been give play to that malicious humour of 
his; and they naturally did not see the joke. that joke, too, was between 
himself, dead, and me, prepare to write his "life." as if he had been 
there to hear me, i chuckled, and spoke in a low voice. 

"you were pull their legs, michael, you know. a little rough on them 
you were. but there's a book here of yours that i'm go to tell the 
truth about. you and i won't pretend to one another. it's a rotten book, 
and both you and i know it...." 

i don't know what it was that cause me sudden to see just then 
something that i had been look at long enough without seeing--that 
portrait of himself that i had set leaning against the back of a chair at 
the end of my writing-table. it stood there, just within the soft 
penumbra of shadow cast by the silk-shaded light. the canvas had been 
enlarged, the seam of it clumsy sewn by andriaovsky's own hand; but in 
that half-light the rough ridge of paint did not show, and i confess that 
the position and effect of the thing startle me for a moment. had i 
care to play a trick with my fancy i could have imagine the head 
wag from side to side, with such rage and fire was it painted. he had 
had the temerity to dash a reflection across one of the glasses of his 
spectacles, conceal the eye behind it. the next moment i had given a 
short laugh. 

"so you're there, are you?... well, i know you agree very heartily about 
that book of poems. heigho! if i remember rightly, you made more money 
out of that book than out of the other put together. but i'm go 
to tell the truth about it. _i_ know better, you know...." 

chancing, before i turn in that night, to reopen one of his folios, i 
came across a drawing, there by accident, i don't doubt, that confirmed 
me in my suspicion that andriaovsky had had his quiet joke with 
schofield, hallard, connolly and co. it was a sketch of schofield's, 
imitative, deplorable, a dreadful show-up of incapacity. well enough 
"drawn," in a sense, it was ... and i remember how andriaovsky had ever 
urge that "drawing," of itself, did not exist. i wink at the portrait. 
i saw his point. he himself had no peer, and, rather than invite 
comparison with stars of the second magnitude, he chose his intimate 
from among the peddler of the wares that had the least possible 
connection with his art. he, too, had understood that the compromise 
must be entire accept or total refused; and while, in the 
divergence of our paths, he had done the one thing and i the other, we 
had each done it thoroughly, with vigour, and with persistence, and each 
could esteem the other, if not as a co-worker, at least as an honourable 
and out-and-out opposite. 


iii 

within a fortnight i was so deep in my task that, in the realest sense, 
the greater part of my life was in the past. the significance of those 
extraordinary peregrination of ours had been in the opportunity they had 
afford for a communion of brain and spirit of unusual rarity; and all 
this determined to my work with the accumulate force of its long 
penning-up. i have spoken of andriaovsky's contempt for such as had the 
conception of their work that it was something they "did" as distinct 
from something they "were"; and unless i succeed in making it plain that, 
not as a mere figure of speech and loose hyperbole, but stark and 
literally, andriaovsky _was_ everything he did, my tale will be 
pointless. 

there was not one of the basic fact of life--of faith, honour, 
truth-speaking, falsehood, betrayal, sin--that he did not turn, not to 
moral interpretations, as other do, but to the holy purpose of his 
noble and passionate art. for any man, sin is only mortal when it is sin 
against that which he know to be immortal true; and the things 
andriaovsky knew to be immortal true were the things that he had gone 
down into the depths in order to bring forth and place upon his paper or 
canvas. these things are not for the peruse of many. unless you love the 
things that he love with a fervour comparable in kind, if not in degree, 
with his own, you may not come near them. "truth, 'the highest thing a 
man may keep,'" he said, "cannot be brought down; a man only attain it 
by prove his right to it"; and i think i need not further state his 
view on the democratise of art. of any result from the elaborate 
process of art-education he held out no hope whatever. "it is in a man, 
or it isn't," he ever declared; "if it is, he must bring it out for 
himself; if it isn't, let him turn to something useful and have done with 
it." i need not press the point that in these things he was almost a 
solitary. 

he made of these general despotic principles the fiercest personal 
applications. i have heard his passionate outbreak of "thief! liar! 
fool!" over a drawing when it has seem to him that a man has not 
vouch with the safety of his immortal soul for the shape and lines he 
has committed to it. i have seen him get into such a rage with the eyes 
of the artist upon him. i have heard the ice and vinegar of his words 
when a good man, for money, has consent to modify and emasculate 
his work; and there linger in my memory his side of a telephone 
conversation in which he told a publisher who had suggest that he 
should do the same thing precisely what he thought of him. and on the 
other hand, he once walk from aldgate to putney hill, with a loose heel 
on one of his boots, to see a man of whom he had seen but a single 
drawing. see him he did, too, in spite of the man's footman, his liveried 
parlourmaid, and the daunt effect of the electric brougham at the 
door. 

"he's a good man," he said to me afterwards, rueful look at the 
place where his boot-heel had been. "you've got to take your good where 
you find it. i don't care whether he's a rich amateur or skin-and-grief 
in a garret as long as he's got the stuff in him. nobody else could have 
fetch me up from the east end this afternoon.... so long; see you in a 
week or so--" 

this was the only time i ever knew him break that sacred time in which he 
celebrated each year the passover and the feast of tabernacles. i doubt 
whether this observance of the ritual of his faith was of more essential 
importance to him than that other philosophical religion towards which he 
sometimes leaned. i have said what his real religion was. 

but to the "life." 

with these things, and others, as a beginning, i began to add page to 
page, phase to phase; and, in a time the short of which astonish 
myself, i had pretty well cover the whole of the first ten years of our 
friendship. maschka call rather less, and schofield rather more 
frequently, than i could have wished; and my surmise that he, at least, 
was in love with her, quick became a certainty. this was to be seen 
when they call together. 

it was when they came together that something else also became apparent. 
this was their slightly derisive attitude towards the means by which i 
had attain my success. it was not the less noticeable that it took the 
form of compliments on the outward and visible results. singly i could 
manage them; together they were inclined to get a little out of hand. 

i would have tax them fairly and square with this, singly or 
together, but for one thing--the beautiful ease with which the "life" was 
proceeding. never had i felt so completely _en rapport_ with my subject. 
so beautiful was the thing running that i had had the idle fancy of 
some actual urge from andriaovsky himself; and each night, before sitting 
down to work, i set his portrait at my desk's end, as if it had been some 
kind of an observance. the most beautiful result of all was, that i felt 
what i had not felt for five years--that i too was not "doing" my work, 
but actually living and being it. at times i took up the sheet i had 
written as ignorant of their contents as if they had proceed from 
another pen--so freshly they came to me. and once, i vow, i found, in my 
own handwriting, a polish name, that i might (it is true) have 
subconscious heard at some time or other, but that stir no chord in 
my memory even when i saw it written. maschka checked and confirmed it 
afterwards; and i did not tell her by what odd circumstance it had issue 
from my pen. 

the day did come, however, when i found i must have it out with schofield 
about this supercilious i have mentioned. the _falchion_ had just 
begun to print the third series of my _martin renard_; and this had been 
made the occasion of another of schofield's ponderous compliments. i 
acknowledge it with none too much graciousness; and then he said: 

"i've na doubt, harrison, that by this time the famous sleuth-hound of 
crime has become quite a creature of flesh and blood to ye." 

it was the tone as much as the words that rile me; and i reply that 
his doubt or the lack of them were a privacy with which i did not wish 
to meddle. from being merely a bore the fellow was rapid becoming 
insolent. 

"but i opine he'll get wearisome now and then, and in that case poor 
michael's 'life' will come as a grand relaxation," he next observed. 

if i meant to have it out, here was my opportunity. 

"i should have thought you'd have trace a close connection than that 
between the two things," i remarked. 

he shot a quick glance at me from beneath his shaggy russet brows. 

"how so? i see varry little connection," he said suspiciously. 

"there's this connection--that while you speak with some freedom of what 
i do, you are quite willing to take advantage of it when it serve 
your turn." 

"'advantage,' harrison?" he said slowly. 

"of the advertisement _martin renard_ give you. i must point out that 
you condone a thing when you accept the benefit of it. either you 
shouldn't have come to me at all, or you should deny yourself the 
gratification of these slurs." 

"slurrrrs?" he repeated loweringly. 

"both of you--you and miss andriaovsky, or maschka as i call her, _tout 
court_. don't suppose i don't know as well as you do the exact worth of 
my 'sleuth-hound,' as you call him. you didn't come to me solely because 
i knew andriaovsky well; you came because i've got the ear of the 
public also; and i tell you plainly that, however much you dislike it, 
michael's fame as far as i'm of any use to him, depend on the 
popularity of _martin renard."_ 

he shook his big head. "this is what i feared," he said. 

"more," i continued, "you can depend upon it that michael, wherever he 
is, know all about that." 

"ay, ay," he said sagely, "i misdoubt your own artistic soul's only to be 
save by the writing of poor michael's 'life,' harrison." 

"leave that to me and michael; we'll settle that. in the meantime, if you 
don't like it, write and publish the 'life' yourself." 

he bent his brow on me. 

"it's precisely what i want to do from the varry first," he said. "if 
you'd care to accept my symposium in the spirit in which it was offered, 
i cannot see that the 'life' would have suffered. but now, when you're 
next in need of my services, ye'll mebbe send for me." 

he took up his hat. i assured him, and let him take it in what sense he 
liked, that i would do so; and he left me. 

not for one single moment did i intend that they should bounce me like 
that. with or without their sanction and countenance, i intended to write 
and publish that "life." schofield--in my own house too--had had the 
advantage that a poor and ill-dressed man has over one who is not poor 
and ill-dressed; but my duty first of all was neither to him nor to 
maschka, but to my friend. 

the worst of it was, however, that i had begun dim to suspect that 
the lancastrian had hit at least one nail on the head. "your artistic 
soul's only to be save by writing poor michael's 'life,'" he had 
informed me... and it was true than i found it pleasant to believe. 
perhaps, after all, my first duty was not to andriaovsky, but to myself. 
i could have kick myself that the fool had been perspicacious enough to 
see it, but that did not alter the fact. i saw that in the sense in which 
andriaovsky understood sin, i had sinned.... 

my only defence lay in the magnitude of my sin. i had sin 
thoroughly, out-and-out, and with a will. it had been the only 
respectable way--andriaovsky's own way when he had cut the company 
of an academician to hobnob with a vagabond. i had at least institute 
no comparison, lower no ideal, was innocent of the accursed attitude 
of facing-both-ways that degrade all lovely and moving things. i was, by 
a paradox, too black a sinner not to hope for redemption.... 

i fell into a long muse on these things.... 

had any of the admirer of _martin renard_ enter the library of his 
author that night he would have seen an interesting thing. he would 
have seen the creator of that idol of clerk and messenger-lads and 
fourth-form boys frankly putt the case before a portrait prop up on 
a chair. he would have heard that popular author haranguing, pleading, 
curious on his defence, turning the thing this way and that. 

"if _you'd_ gone over, michael," that author argued, "you'd have done 
precisely the same thing. if i'd stuck it out, we were, after all, of a 
kind; we've got to be one thing or the other--isn't that so, andriaovsky? 
since i made up my mind, i've face only one way--only one way. i've kept 
your ideal and theirs entire separate and distinct. not one single 
beautiful phrase will you find in the _martin renards;_ i've cut 'em 
out, every one. i may have cease to worship, but i've profane no 
temple.... and think what i _might_ have done--what they all do! they 
deal out the slush, but with an apologetic glance at the art shades; 
_you_ know the style!--'oh, harrison; he do that detective rubbish, but 
that's not harrison; if harrison like to drop that he could be a fine 
artist!'--i _haven't_ done that. i _haven't_ run with the hare and hunt 
with the hounds. i _am_ just harrison, who do that detective 
rubbish!... these other chaps, schofield and connolly, _they're_ the real 
sinners, michael--the fellow who can't make up their mind to be one 
thing or the other ('artists of considerable abilities'--ha! ha!).... of 
course you know maschka's go to marry that chap? what'll _they_ do, do 
you think? he'll scrape up a few pound out of the stew where i find 
thousands, marry her, and they'll set up a salon and talk the stuff the 
chair talk that night, you remember!... but you wait until i finish 
your 'life.'..." 

i laid it all before him, almost as if i sought to propitiate him. i 
might have been courting his patronage for his own "life." then, with a 
start, i came to, to find myself talk nonsense to the portrait that 
years before andriaovsky had refuse to sell me. 


iv 

the first check i experienced in the hitherto so easy flow of the "life" 
came at the chapter that dealt with andriaovsky's attitude towards 
"professionalism" in art. he was inflexible on this point; there ought 
not to be professional artists. when it was pointed out that his position 
involved a premium upon the rich amateur, he merely reply that riches 
had nothing to do with the question, and that the starve in the garret 
was not excuse for his poverty's sake from the observance of the 
implacable conditions. he spoke literally of the "need" to create, 
usually in the france term, _besogne_; and he was inclined to regard the 
imposition of this need on a man rather as a curse laid upon him than as 
a privilege and a pleasure. but i must not enlarge upon this further than 
to observe that this portion of his "life" which i was approach 
coincide in point of time with that period of my own life at which i had 
been confront with the alternative of starve for art's sake or 
becoming rich by supply a clamorous trade demand. 

it came, this check i have spoken of, one night, as i was in the very 
middle of a sentence; and though i have cudgel my brains in seek 
how best i can describe it, i am reduce to the simple statement that it 
was as arresting, as sharp, actual and impossible to resist, as if my 
hand had been seize and pin down in its passage across the paper. i 
can even see again the fragment of the sentence i had written: "... _and 
the mere contemplation of a betrayal so essential--_" then came that 
abrupt and remarkable stop. it was such an experience as i had formerly 
known only in nightmare. 

i sat there look blank and stupid at my own hand. and not only was 
my hand arrested, but my brain also had completely cease to work. for 
the life of me i could not recall the conclusion of the sentence i had 
plan a moment before. 

i look at my hand, and look again; and as i look i remember 
something i had been reading only a few days before--a profound 
unsettle description of an experiment in auto-suggestion. the 
experiment had consist of the place of a hand upon a table, and the 
lay upon it the conjure that, the will notwithstanding, it should 
not move. and as i watch my own hand, pale on the paper in the pearly 
light, i knew that, by some consent to the nullify of the will that 
did not proceed from, the self i was accustomed to regard as my own, that 
injunction was already placed upon it. my conscious and deliberate will 
was powerless. i could only sit there and wait until whatever inhibition 
had arrest my writing hand should permit it to move forward again. 

it must have been several minutes before such a tingle of the nerves as 
announce that the blood is once more return to a cramped member 
warn me that i was about to be released. warily i await my moment; 
then i pluck my hand to myself again with a sudden that cause a 
little blot of ink to spurt from my fountain-pen on to the surface of the 
paper. i drew a deep breath. i was free again. and with the freedom came 
a resolve--that whatever portion of myself had been responsible for this 
prank should not repeat it if i could possibly prevent it. 

but scarcely had i come, as i may say (and not without a little gush of 
alarm now that it was over), to myself, when i was struck by a thought. 
it was a queer wild sort of thought. it fetch me out of my chair and 
set me stride across the library to a lower shelf in the farthest 
corner. this shelf was the shelf on which i kept my letter-files. i 
stoop and ran my finger along the back of the dusty row. i drew out 
the file for 1900, and brought it back to my writing-table. my contracts, 
i ought to say, repose in a deed-box at my agent's office; but my file 
contained, in the form of my agent's letters, a sufficient record of my 
business transactions. 

i open the file concertina-wise, and turn to the section lettered 
"r." i drew out the correspondence that related to the sale of the first 
series of the _martin renards_. as i did so i glance at the movable 
calendar on my table. the date was january 20th. 

the file contained no letters for january of any significance whatever. 

the thought that had half form in my brain immediately became nonsense. 
i replace the letters in their compartment, and took the file back to 
its shelf again. for some minutes i pace the library irresolutely; then 
i decided i would work no more that night. when i gather together my 
papers i was careful to place that with the half-finished sentence on the 
top, so that with the first rest of my eyes upon it on the morrow my 
memory might haply be refreshed. 

i try again to finish that sentence on the morrow. with certain 
modification that i need not particularise here, my experience was the 
same as on the previous night. 

it was the same when i made the attempt on the day after that. 

at ten o'clock of the night of the fourth day i complete the sentence 
without difficulty. i just sat down in my chair and wrote it. 

with equal ease i finished the chapter on professional artists. 

it was not likely that schofield would have refrain from telling 
maschka of our little difference on our last meeting; and within a week 
of the date i have just mention i learned that she knew all about it. 
and, as the circumstances of my learning this were in a high degree 
unusual, i will relate them with such clear as i am able. 

i ought first to say, however, that the selection of the drawing that 
were to illustrate the book have been made (the drawing for which my
